But if they are not converted, and frequently occur on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet: then administer to them a cautery with warm water: and it is so that you take a tube of bronze, or iron, or of a vulture's feather: and place it over the clavus, or althelul. Then throw into the tube a small quantity of warm water: hold your hand, and you revolve it with the compression of your hand a little, so that the edge of the tube is imprinted in the root of the nail, and the warm water finds a way to the depth, to the root of the nail: and let the patient wait a little for the biting of the warm water for one hour.
Then dismiss him, for the nail is eradicated with its roots.
Do this with them one after another, until you come upon all that are among them in the body.
Then care for them after they are eradicated, with that which generates flesh from ointments.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in Tremor.
When tremor happens to someone from cold in a nerve, or from a quartan fever, or something else: then it is necessary that you cauterize him with four cauteries, or five, upon the vertebrae of the back, and between each vertebra a cautery, and on his chest a cautery, and upon his stomach a cautery.
For tremor rests with olive cautery, and the health of a cold illness hastens.
Concerning the method of cauterizing Albathir.
Chapter 57.
Sometimes foul things are expelled in the body, that is, pustules, which are made from gross corrupted materials.
Therefore, when they appear, it is necessary that they be cauterized over the whole head of the pustule with a subtle cautery using myrtle wood, the end of which is lit with fire. Or with the root of long birthwort. Or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes they are cauterized in the beginning of their expulsion in this manner: and they do not increase; and the superfluity making them is broken, and they are healed from them.
However, it is necessary that this be done after evacuation with phlebotomy.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a flow of blood from an artery in a wound.
Very often a flow of blood from an artery occurs, which has been cut in a wound, which happens externally, or in the incision of an abscess, or cauterization of a limb, and similar things: and it is difficult to stop it.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then hasten with your hand to the opening of the artery, and place your index finger upon it: and press it hard, until the blood is stopped under your finger, and nothing comes out from it.
Then put small olive cauteries in the fire, and many more large ones: and blow on them until they become very hot.
Then take one of them, either small or large, according to the wound and the place where the artery is broken, and place the cautery upon the vein, after which you quickly remove your finger: and hold the cautery until the blood is stopped.
But if it spurts out when you lift your finger from the opening of the artery, and you extinguish the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from the cauteries which are prepared in the fire.
Therefore do not cease doing this with one after another, until the blood is stopped.
And beware lest you burn the nerve which is there, and some other harm befall the patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, then it is not possible to stop it, especially when the artery is large, except with one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we said. Or with extraction, when it is not extracted. For when it is extracted, its extremities contract, and the blood is stopped. Or it is tied with a strong ligature of thread. Or medicines possessing the property of stanching blood are placed upon it, and it is bound with a suitable constricting pad.
But he who administers its excision with a ligature, and constriction with a cloth, or the application of cauterizing agents and the like, does not completely benefit him, except rarely.
But if this should happen to someone, and a physician or medicine is not present: then he should begin by placing the index finger over the opening of the vein, as we have related, and constricting it strongly, until the blood is stopped from the upper part of the wound, and above the artery. And the finger should not be removed, but the wound should be constantly washed with very cold water until the blood congeals and thickens, and is cut off.
And meanwhile he should consider what he needs from cautery or medicine.
Concerning incision, perforation, and phlebotomy in wounds and similar things.
We have already said in the first chapter, every illness in which cautery with fire and burning medicine is suitable, and its occasions, its causes, and its instruments, and the forms of cauteries.
And I placed chapters there from head to foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same way: that the seeker may be relieved concerning his request.
And before I begin that, it is necessary that you learn, O sons, that in this chapter is something terrifying, that which is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because of that, caution is necessary in it more vehemently, because in the operation which is in this chapter, evacuation with blood often falls, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or incision upon an abscess, or opening of an exit, or healing of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision upon a stone, and things like these: with all of which trembling and fear are associated, and death falls upon many of them.
And I warn you, lest that fall upon you in which there is hesitation, for sometimes there fall to you in this art kinds of men with kinds of illnesses. For among them is he who is angry with his illness, and death is light to him: because of the vehemence of that which he finds from his illness and the length of its malice. And with illness is fear which signifies death.
And among them is he who gives his wealth, and enriches you with it, hoping for health, and his illness is pernicious.
Therefore it is not fitting that you consent to him who comes to you from among them, whose manner is such. And let your caution be greater than your greed and your avarice. And do not presume upon anything of it, except after certain knowledge, which verifies with you that to which a laudable outcome has arrived.
And use in the treatment of all your sick, prognosis and indication of that to which health is suitably directed.
For in that is for you help in acquiring fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire you, O sons, with His understanding, and may He not take away from you direction and inspiration: because that is in His hand.
And I indeed have arranged this Chapter, distinguishing it by chapters, according to what preceded in the chapter on Cautery, from the neck to the feet: that the thing which you wish from it, and which you need, may be lightened for you.
Concerning the treatment of water which gathers in the heads of children.
Chapter 1.
Know that this infirmity very often happens to children at birth, or when the midwife binds the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it also happens from a hidden cause which is not known.
And indeed I have not seen this sickness in others than in children; and to everyone of them whom I saw, death hastened: therefore it seemed to me to leave the operation in it.
And indeed I saw one of them, a child whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger every day, until he could not sit up by himself, because of the size of his head; and the moisture increased, until he died.
And this moisture indeed, either gathers between the skin and the bone, or gathers under the bone upon the dura mater.
And the operation in it, if the moisture is between the skin and the bone, and the abscess is small, then it is necessary that you make an incision in the middle of the head with one cut in width: and let the length of the incision be about two finger-widths, until the moisture flows out.
And this is the shape of the spatula.
If therefore the moisture is more increased, and the abscess is larger, then make two incisions, according to this shape.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on every side: and the water is depressed when you compress it with your hand inwards, and it is not hidden from you: then it is necessary that you make an incision in the middle of the head with three incisions, according to this manner.
And after the incision remove all the moisture. Then bind the incisions with cloths and powders. Then apply to it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then untie the bandage, and treat the wound with tents and ointments.
And do not allow the head to be bound with equal tightness. And feed the patient with all dry food of little moisture, until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And another method of incision is that you consider where the size of the abscess and the collection of water appear.
Because sometimes it is greater in the occiput, or in the sinciput, or on the right, or on the left.
Therefore, ascend with the incision where the abscess and the filling of water appear to you: and incise it according to what is possible for you.
And observe that you do not cut an artery, and that a flow of blood occurs, and that the patient dies from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Concerning the incision of the arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When catarrhs happen to someone in the eyes or in the chest, and it becomes chronic, and the treatment of medicines does not help: then the last treatment in that case is the incision of these two arteries.
Therefore, when you wish to incise them, the patient should shave his head. Then rub the place with a rough cloth, so that the arteries appear. Then the patient should bind his neck with any cloth of his. Then consider where the vein pulses. And the places of both are the places which are depressed behind the ears: and it is rarely hidden, except in certain men.
Then mark with a cautery on each: and incise each with a sharp spatula on both sides, the incision reaching to the bone: and let this be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the spatula under the artery, and incise it upwards with an incision.
And let the length of the incision be about two joined fingers.
For when a vein is incised, blood flows out with a pulsatile flow, which leaps inwards with a frequent leap.
But if the artery does not appear to the senses, then it is necessary to measure from the ear a space of three fingers. Then mark with a cautery, and cut to the bone.
And that which it is necessary to let out of blood, is six ounces and perhaps you let out of it more, or less. All this according to the quantity which appears to you from the strength of the infirm and the repletion of his artery.
Then consider the wound. Then if anything of the sifac remains at the bone, then incise it, lest a hot abscess occur: then bind the wound with linen cloths.
Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When a man suffers from chronic headache, or acute catarrh because of acrid humors and heat in the temples, or chronic headache, severe, and similar to these, and it is treated with methods of medical treatment and it does not help: then we have experienced in these illnesses the extraction of arteries from the temples, or their cauterization, as we have narrated.
And the method of operating in their extraction is that the patient should shave the hairs which are in the temples, and point to the artery appearing in the temple. For it is shown to you by its pulse, and is rarely hidden except in a few men, or by the intensity of cold.
But if it is hidden from you, then let the patient bind his head with any cloth of his: then rub the place with a rough cloth. Or steam the place with warm water, until it appears to you in manifest appearance. Then take the spatula, which I formed a little while ago. Then scrape with it the skin easily, until you reach the artery. Then throw a hook into it, and draw it upwards, until you draw it out of the skin, and make it escape from the tunics which are beneath it on every side.
But if the artery is small, then wrap it with the end of the hook. Then cut from it a part according to the amount by which its ends are lengthened and contracted, and that no flow of blood occurs. For if it is not cut, and if it is not severed, the blood does not entirely ascend.
Then evacuate blood from six ounces down to three.
But if the artery is large, then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with a double thread, strong, of silk or of alhod cords, so that putrefaction does not hasten to it before the healing of the wound, and a flow of blood occurs.
Then cut the excess which is between the two ligatures. Do this at that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery to the bone, with a knife-cautery, until its extremities are cut, and it will stand in the place of the same work.
And it is a better procedure, unless the patient has a fever or is of a heated complexion. Because cautery is among those things which help to end humidities. Therefore it is a better aid.
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries that the place be filled with old lint, and that properly made pledgets be placed upon it.
And after resolution it should be treated with dry medicines which cause flesh to grow, and with linen cloths, until it is healed.
But if meanwhile, while you are operating, a flow of blood from an artery occurs, then hasten to cut it off with a cautery, or to fill the place with zegi, and bind your hand until the blood is cut off. But if none of these things is present to you, then place your finger on the place until the blood clots. And bathe the place with very cold water until the sharpness ceases, and bind it as is necessary.
And of what is lighter and easier in the extraction of an artery, it is to cauterize a vein with this cautery having two knives: and let the sharpness of the two knives be like an almond. However, they are of much less sharpness than two knives. Because if they are sharp, like two knives, coldness quickly affects both: and they do not cut the flesh with speed. Therefore, since there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire is retained in them, and the flesh is cut quickly: therefore this work is better than all work, and lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of the cautery.
And let the space which is between the two knives be the quantity of the thickness of a finger, after you mark two places with a cautery: and place both very hot, until they reach the bone, and cut the vein in two places: so that what is between its extremities is lengthened. For they do not completely consolidate with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
Concerning the treatment of sharp, constant flow of tears from the eyes.
When there are constant tears, and their flow is from the veins which are on the outside of the skull, and nothing of the medicinal cures with medicines helps in them, and you see the face of the sick person already reddening, and he feels a crawling on his forehead, like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are thinned, moist, so that their hairs are already corroded with corrosion, and his eyelids are heated from the sharpness of the tears: then the ancients needed in their cure this operation, and they named it a cure with the knife.
And it is, that you should order the sick person to shave the hairs which are on his forehead. Then incise three equidistant fissures on the forehead according to the length of the forehead: and let the length of the fissure be about two fingers, and one fissure be equidistant to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length a little from the movement of the muscles, and the third from the other side: and beware of cutting the arteries which are on the two sides: and lengthen your hand from the continuation of the two jawbones, and let the space of each incision be the quantity of three joined fingers: and let you have a prepared piece of sponge, or much dried cloth, with which you will dry the blood.
Then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, of which the following is soon the form. From the incision which is from the part of the temples, up to the second incision: and flay with it all the skin, which is between the two incisions, with the periosteum, which is over the bone. Then do that also from the middle incision to the second incision. Then remove this spatula, and insert into the first incision again another instrument, which is called a sharp scalpel, smooth on one side, not sharp on the other side. And this is its form.
And place its sharp side towards the flesh connected with the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone: and push it until it reaches the middle fissure: and cut with it all the vessels which descend from the head to the eyes: except that the incision should be continued up to that which appears of the skin.
Then do that in the middle fissure up to the other fissure.
And after a sufficient quantity of blood has flowed, cleanse the places of the clots of congealed blood.
Then place in every fissure a pledget of old linen, and place over them pledgets soaked in wine and oil, or vinegar. And see that a hot abscess does not occur.
And on the third day, loosen the ligature, and administer a copious infusion with warm water. Then treat it with basilicon ointment and other remedies with which wounds are treated, until it is healed.
Chapter V.
Concerning the treatment of sharp tears and catarrhs affecting the eyes from the interior of the head.
When sharp, acrid, and persistent catarrhs affect someone, and you see their eyes weakened, small, and their sight already impaired; and the eyelids ulcerated, and hairs fall from them, and there is a sharp, painful ache deep in the head, accompanied by sneezing: then understand from these occurrences that those materials and catarrhs do not come except from many veins.
And the best treatment for these is this. And it is that you should instruct the patient to shave his forehead. Then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead, or a slightly longer one laterally, and begin the incision from the left temple to the right; and let the incision reach the bone; and keep your hand away from the temporal muscles, which move during mastication, until the bone is exposed; and you will dry all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with old linen or linen threads. Then bind it above with pledgets; and soak the pledgets with wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not occur.
And when you unbind it, and you see that the hot abscess is diminishing, then you must rub the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regime that causes flesh to grow: for example, if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, and prepare a plaster from them, and apply it to the growth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter VI.
Concerning the treatment of that which falls into the ear.
Whatever falls into the ear is one of four kinds: either a mineral stone, or something similar to stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans; or it is a flowing substance, such as water and vinegar, and things similar to them; or some animal.
When, therefore, a stone falls into the ear, or a kind of stone from among those which are not added to, nor grow in the ear, expose the ear to the sun: if then you see the stone, then instill into the ear something of violet oil, or sesame oil. Then manage its extraction, and make him sneeze with sneezing powders, and block the nostrils at the coming of the sneezing, after you place around the ear pieces of cloth or wool, and extend the ear upwards.
For very often it comes out with this treatment.
But if it does not come out, then manage its extraction with forceps, that is, fine tweezers: whose form is this.
If therefore it comes out with forceps, it is good.
And if not, then manage its extraction with a small, thin, blunt hook.
If therefore it does not come out through that, then make a cannula of bronze, and insert the end of the cannula into the hole of the ear deeply, and plug what is around the cannula with wax softened with oil, so that there is no way for the wind except through the cannula: then extract it with your strong suction.
For very often it comes out with what we have described. But if it does not come out in this way: then take some helkalimbat, or birdlime, that is, prepared glue, with which birds are caught, a small amount: and place it on the end of a probe after you have neatly wrapped cotton around it: then insert it into the hole of the ear easily, after you have dried the ear from moisture.
If therefore it does not come out with everything that we have described, then hasten to incise, before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the method of incision is that the phlebotomist should first bleed the patient in the cephalic vein: and draw out blood from him according to the measure of his strength. Then make the patient sit between your hands, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear near its fat in a depressed place: and let the incision be in the shape of a crescent, until you reach the stone: then remove it with whatever instruments you can.
Then sew up the incision immediately, and treat it until it heals.
But if the thing falling into the ear is one of the grains which grow and swell, then administer its extraction.
But if it does not obey you for exit, then take a thin, subtle spatula, according to this form.
And administer with it the excision of that kind of grain which falls into the ear, and do not do it when you think that the grain is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, until you put it into many pieces: then extract them with hooks, or with a fine probe, or with suction, as we said, for its extraction is alleviated.
However, in the case of water entering the ear, the infirm person ought to use sneezing with the head inclined first: and then he should fill his ear with a little old lint: and he should lie on the ear in which the water is. If therefore it exits through that, it is good.
And if not: then take many stones, about the length of a finger, thin, smooth, and heat them slightly with fire, and let the infirm person insert one of them into the hole of his ear: and let him hop on one foot on that side, and strike with another stone upon the stone which is in the ear. Therefore let him not cease doing that with stone and stone, until all the water comes out.
And then the water comes out so that you take from papyrus, or from a rush, one, and insert one end of it into the ear: and light the other end with fire, until most of it is burned. Then repeat another rush, and do this many times, until all the water comes out: and draw it out with a tube as was previously described for the stone.
But in the extraction of an animal entering it, if it is of small body, like a flea, and similar, treat it as I said in the division.
But if it is of large body, which is apparent to the senses: then manage its extraction with forceps and hooks: and its matter is easier than all things held in the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we said in the division, and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not help: then it is necessary that you look at the ear in the sun. If therefore something of worms appears there, then extract it with forceps, or with thin hooks.
But if nothing appears there, then take a cannula, of which this is the form, narrow below, wide above: and introduce the thin end into the ear according to the amount that the patient tolerates: then suck with it with strong suction. Do this many times, until all the worms come out.
But if they do not obey you to come out, then seal around the cannula with wax, as I said in the case of the stone.
And if they do not come out with what we have said: then administer distillations which the ancients have already experienced in killing worms: and you will find it in the treatise on distillations.
And let your pouring out of oils and medicines into the ear be with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it of silver or bronze, narrow below, with a small hole, wide above.
Therefore let that which pushes what is in the concavity of the cannula be made decently of bronze. And if you wish, take a rod, and wrap cotton around its end with a neat wrapping: then pour oil, or juice, or whatever you wish of these medicines into the cannula: and let it be in the ear: then insert the rod with the cotton from above, and press it with your hand with moderate compression, until the oil is impelled into the concavity of the hearing, and the patient feels it inside: and that which is poured into the ear, should be heated a little with fire: and beware lest the thing that is distilled into it be very cold: for medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter VII.
Concerning the care of obstruction happening in the ear.
Sometimes certain infants come forth from the wombs of their mothers imperforate.
And it has also happened to some men that there is obstruction in the passage of the ear from a wound, or from flesh grown there.
And this obstruction sometimes happens in the depth of the opening of the ear, which sight does not reach; and sometimes it happens near the exterior of the opening, and sight reaches it.
Therefore that which happens in the depth of the ear, and which sight does not comprehend, is in most instances difficult of healing.
And in that which sight reaches, it is necessary that you place the ear of the sick person in the sun, and look into it: then if you see the apparent obstruction, open it with a thin spatula, whose form is this which follows, let its extremity in which there is a certain small width, a certain part of which is sharp: and let the rest of the spatula be of two smooth sides: so that it does not injure the ear.
If therefore the obstruction is from flesh grown in the passage of the ear: then hold it with a thin hook, and cut it with strong ease, until all the flesh is removed.
But if the obstruction is in the depth of the ear: then take a slender, smooth probe, and heat it a little with fire: then insert it into the passage of the ear.
If therefore you feel the obstruction with that which the sick person finds with difficulty of hearing, attempt its opening with ease: and beware lest you injure the nerves of the ear.
Then put into the ear a pledget according to the size of the opening, which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment: do this for several days, until you are sure of the healing of the wound.
Or take a pledget, and dip it in rose water, and sprinkle on it powdered vitriol, and use it.
But if you see the ear already festering: then it is necessary that you remove the pledget, and leave it with another pledget, which you will wrap in cerate which is made with rose oil, until the warm abscess subsides: then treat it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs: then immerse a sponge, or cloths in cold water, and place it over the ear: and use the remaining treatment, which stops the flow of blood, mentioned in the treatise on distillations.
Concerning the treatment of a sty on the eyelid.
Chapter VIII.
These styes which occur on the eyelids, are sometimes moist, and sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary to grasp them with forceps: or with a hook, and to incise them with a spatula from their roots. Then place crushed vitriol on the locations.
But if blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a thin lenticular cautery.
And to cauterize them is better after incision. For many times they return when something remains of their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned, and they do not return.
Concerning the treatment of the hailstone-like growths of the eyelid.
Chapter IX.
Very often something happens in the eyelids which is similar to hail in its strength and hardness, and on account of this it is called hailstone: and it is a collection of thick moisture in the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
Therefore the operation on them is that you should then consider if it is a hailstone which projects from the eyelid, and moves in every direction, and its matter is easy.
Therefore make an incision upon it lengthwise. Then scrape it from every side easily until it comes out. Then suspend it with a hook and cut it off.
But if its excision is not prepared for you except after you penetrate the eyelid with a small incision, then that harms the patient in some way.
But if the fissure is large, then bring it together with a suture and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For ointment restores it and causes it to grow flesh.
And if the hailstone slopes towards the interior of the ear towards the inner surface, then invert the eyelid and suspend the hailstone with a hook, except that you need an incision, and press it from every side.
If the eyelid is lengthened by incision, that does no harm in any way.
Then wash the eye after the incision of the hailstone with salt water, and treat the place with that which causes it to grow flesh, until the patient is healed.
Concerning the treatment of the axirnath of the eyelids.
Chapter X.
Axirnath is fat which is generated in the membranes of the upper eyelid; and it happens most to children; and it weighs down their eyes, and catarrhs happen to them in the mornings, and they cannot look at the sunlight, because tears rush to them, and therefore you see them lying down on their faces always, and they sleep on them, and their eyelids are moist under their eyebrows, which inflation has now covered; and the prominence of the axirnath indeed is apparent to the eye, and when you press the place with your fingers, you feel the axirnath between them.
And the method of its operation is that the patient should put his head in your bosom.
Then take a linen cloth, and prepare lint in it, and make from that lint a circle according to the quantity that contains the axirnath on every side. Then place it over it, and press with your fingers on every side, so that the axirnath may be gathered in the middle of the circle.
Then make a cut in the middle of that moisture with a lancet, making incisions in width, and let not the incision be greater than that which is made in phlebotomy in depth.
Or it is necessary that you cut the whole skin, until you arrive at the axirnath; for in many dispositions the axirnath proceeds from the incision according to the form of a piece of white fat.
Therefore draw it out with the cloth, which you have inserted between your fingers to the exterior: and you turn your hand to the right and to the left, until it is dispersed: and beware, lest you add to the incision, so that you do not arrive with the lancet to the eye, and injure it.
But if the axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the incision, you shall add a little to the incision with ease, until the axirnath comes forth. Then draw it out, as we have said.
Then submerge cloths in vinegar and water, and place them on the place, and bind it with powders.
And there are some men who grind salt, and put it in the hollow of the incision, so that what remains of that moisture may be liquefied.
Then treat it until it is healed.
But if it happens in a place of a hot abscess, then treat it with soothing plasters until it is healed.
Concerning the ways of Ataxmir of the eye, that is, when the eyelid of the eye is softened, whence it happens that it is wrinkled as it ought not to be.
Chapter XI.
When superfluous hairs are born in the eyelids beyond the course of the natural hairs, and grow old: then they harm the eye, and cause various sicknesses to happen, such as continual tears, and softening of the eyelids, and albugo, until it becomes the cause of the destruction of the eye.
And indeed Ataxmir of the eye is cured according to four methods. Or by cautery with fire. Or with a sharp medicine, according to what was premised in the chapter on cautery. Or Ataxmir is cured by incision and suture. Or by tubes, according to what I shall say.
It is necessary that you place the head of the sick person in your bosom. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If therefore it is turned, it is good. And if not: insert a needle into it, in which there is thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with the thread from above, and let it be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the margin of the eyelid, in which the hairs grow: and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it with a radius.
Then make an incision in the inner part of the eyelid under the added hairs with a spatula cutting from the larger lacrimal duct to the smaller lacrimal duct.
Then remove the thread, and place a small pledget of cotton or cloth under the eyelid: then mark above the eyelid with a cautery, which is like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the shape is made according to the size and elevation of the eyelid: because it varies in men.
For there are some who need a large amount to be cut from the eyelid, according to the amount which is softened from the eyelid.
Then with a spatula incise upon the two lines which you marked, and begin from the larger lacrimal duct to the smaller lacrimal duct, and let there be one fissure near the natural hairs, like the thickness of a ray.
Then introduce a hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then flay it completely. Then bring together with a suture the two lips, with a needle and fine woolen thread: and wipe away the blood, and make adhere what is superfluous of the threads to the two eyebrows, with certain sticking things, if you wish to do that: and if not, do not care.
Then leave the suture and threads for three or four days: afterwards treat it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without a suture, and treat it with that which dries and constricts. For the eyelid is raised at the closing of the wound and its bringing together: but the future will be better.
The ancients taught this method of treatment, Ataxmir.
However, there is concern about the patient, and it is from good surgery, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is also in Ataxmir: and it is, that you mark on the eyelid a figure which is like the figure of a myrtle leaf, as we have narrated.
Then raise the eyelid with three hooks, separated or joined together, according to this form which follows, then cut away what is superfluous with small scissors, according to this form, with evenness of incision.
But if it is not possible for you to hold the hook, and it is not equal: then take a needle in which there is thread, and insert it in the middle of the figure: and insert another thread near the larger tear duct, and a second thread near the smaller tear duct: and join the threads together between your fingers with evenness.
Then raise your hand with an even elevation: and raise the skin upon which the mark has been made, as we have narrated.
Then join the two lips of the wound with a suture: and care for it until it heals.
If however an abscess should occur at your incision, or your fissure: soothe that abscess with cerate, and similar things, from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelids also, that their hairs are turned.
Therefore it is necessary that you use what we said in the incision: but suture is more skillful for both. Concerning the treatment of Ataxmir with canes.
And it is so, that you should turn back the eyelid, and cut the fissure which is within, according to what I have related. Then skillfully make two canes, or two slender pieces of wood: whose length should be according to the length of the eyelid, and their width should be less than the width of a spathula: and you have already incised at the extremities of each from both sides where you hold the threads.
Then carefully bring each over that which is superfluous from the eyelid of the eye, and bind the two canes from both sides with a strong binding, and leave it for several days. For the skin, being constricted, will die, and will blacken, and will putrefy, until it falls off by itself.
If therefore it is delayed, then cut it with scissors. Then treat it until it is healed.
When therefore it is consolidated, the eyelid is raised: and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of two cannulas, which you will make according to this figure, and quantity in length and breadth.
Chapter XII.
Concerning the elevation of hairs pricking in the eye with a needle.
The operation in that is, that you take a fine needle, and insert into it a fine, smooth silk thread. Then you bring together its ends, and tie each with a very fine knot: and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then place in the Alinxotati another fine thread under it shortly, and knot its ends if you wish.
Then let the patient place his head in your bosom, and be near the sun so that the operation may be clearly seen: because it is a fine, or small, operation.
Then insert the needle with the Alinxotati into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly, until you pull out from above a natural hair.
Then pull the hair up above the Alinxotati: and insert into the Alinxotati that hair: if it is one, or two, or three, not more.
Then draw your hand with it, until the hair emerges with the Alinxotati in the eyelid with the natural hair.
But if the Alinxotati is pulled, and the hair does not emerge with it: you will pull the Alinxotati downwards, with the thread which you have placed in it, until the Alinxotati emerges from the upper hole, and you see it with the vision of the eye: then indeed you will pull the Alinxotati and the thread: and pull out both, and close the eye: and leave it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres in its place, and flesh grows over it.
But if the hair is short, then either join to it a long hair from the natural hairs, and lift it with it; or leave it, until it lengthens after a time, and then lift it.
Chapter XIII.
When this Alxatrati happens to the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this Alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Accidental, therefore, arises from the healing of a wound, or incision, or cautery, and similar things.
And the method of operating on it is that you should split that consolidation, and separate its lips, and place between them a pledget of linen, and bind it, until it is healed.
And it is not necessary to use in its treatment things which dry and constrict; for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was: rather use in it things which soften, such as fenugreek; and embrocate with water in which mallow has been boiled, linseed; and diachylon ointment, which has been liquefied with some oil; and wrap it with a pledget, and treat it with it.
And the treasure of your treatment is that you strive with all your skill that it not consolidate according to the form which it had at first.
And this operation which we have described is nothing but a certain rectification of the Alxatrati, so that the form of the place does not return completely to what it was.
Concerning the treatment of Alxatrati which occurs in the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkling or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This Alxatrati which occurs below, is that which is truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And accidental is caused by a wound, or fissure, or cautery, and similar things.
And the method of operation in it is, that you take a needle, in which there is double thread, and you insert it into the flesh, and you make it penetrate from the left lachrymal to the right lachrymal, until the thread arrives at the two extremities of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh upwards with a needle, and incise it with a broad spatula. If therefore the shape of the eyelid returns as it ought, it is good.
And if not, take a radius, and place it in the fissure, and turn the eyelid with it: and make two fissures in the inner side of the eyelid, and let the extremities of the two fissures be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and an angle is made from them: until when they are joined their figure becomes this figure, and it is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh by the amount that is its lower sharp side, from that which follows the eyelid. Then bring together the separated parts; sew it with a linen thread.
Then treat them with what we have spoken of, with softening medicines and linen cloths, until they are healed.
And if an abscess occurs from the fissure, and suture, or cautery: then it is necessary that you make a flat fissure under the hairs of the eyelids, even according to what preceded. Then separate the two lips with a linen cloth, according to what we said.
And the sum of the discussion of the treatment of an abscess, when it is above or below, is that the operation should proceed according to what is prepared in the form of an abscess.
For it itself is of many diversities in form: and a skilled craftsman prepares the means however it is possible for him, until he restores the form to its natural form, or near it, and the patient himself, according to what suits him from the operation and instruments in many dispositions.
Chapter XV.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva, or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation happens to many people, so that the upper eyelid is consolidated with the white of the eye, and prevents it from its natural functions.
Therefore it is necessary that you insert the end of a probe under the eyelid, and raise it upwards: or extend it with a hook: then incise the adhesion with a thin spatula, which is not with the sharpness of cutting spatulas, but rather somewhat blunt: so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula is removed, and injures the eye with its sharpness: and let your incision be as if you were scraping off that adhesion easily, until when the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the entire adhesion is healed.
Then pour salt water into the eye, wash it with water: or instill into it from a sieve made, from ziniar dissolved with water.
Then separate the eyelid and eye with a linen cloth: and place on the eye wool infused with egg white: and after the third day use a sieve, and with solidifying agents, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the nail and the prominence of flesh of the tear ducts.
Chapter XVI.
Know that a nail is according to two methods. Or it is sinewy: and it is similar to hard, thin parchment. Or it is not sinewy, similar to congealed white moisture: which when iron touches it, or you try to grasp it with a hook, is cut off, and the hook does not fix in it.
And both kinds do not begin except from the larger lachrymal duct, until it gradually goes, until it covers the sight, and prevents light and the movement of the eye.
And the method of operating on its incision is: that the sick person puts his head in your bosom. Then open his eyes, and lift up the eyelid with your hand. Then collect a small fold of the growth with a hook, and extend it upwards. Then take a needle, and insert into it a hair from the hair of a horse, or an ox, or a cow: and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle a little, and fix it in the middle of the growth: and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the growth with the thread, and extend it upwards: and scrape with the hair the side of the growth which follows the pupil, as if you were sawing it with the hair to its end.
Then incise the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a fine spatula, or with small scissors: and leave the natural flesh of the tear duct, so that a constant flow of tears may not result from its incision.
And sometimes the growth is extended with a hook alone; or with thread, as we have said. Then scrape it with a smooth spatula, of which this is the form.
And beware lest the spatula touch the cornea, and a rupture occur in it, and the pupil be perforated.
Then after the incision, throw into the eye some crushed salt, or something made from  *zinziber*, and leave it until the next day. Then treat it with what is appropriate, until it is healed.
But if the nail is not fleshy and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor is a hook fixed in it, then this type of nail is often treated with medicines, and is healed as I have described in the section.
But if you wish to incise it, then open the eye of the patient, and take a thin, smooth spatula of weak sharpness, according to this form. And shave the nail from above carefully and skillfully. But if you see that it is rarefied and broken, and the shaving impresses upon it, then instill from your hour into the eye a preparation made from ziniar, or from red sief, or crushed salt, and cover the eye until another day.
Then repeat the operation on it until all of it is removed. Unless there is a hot abscess in the eye contrary to this: for then leave it, and treat the abscess until it is healed.
Then repeat the operation on it with a razor until it is healed.
However, if the eminence of the fleshy tear duct causes great harm in the eye, suspend it with a hook and incise a part of it; and do not extend the incision, lest the flow of tears be affected.
Then instill into the tear duct red copper sulfate or something made from verdigris, until it heals.
Concerning the incision of Albucasis, and the flesh growing in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes a red, fleshy growth arises in the eyes of men, either superimposed or covering the pupil, or approaching it, or constricting over the eyelids, and perhaps inverting the eyelids outwards; and it resembles a wild rose.
And the operation in this is also that the patient should place his head in your bosom. Then open his eyes, and gather up all that flesh with hooks suitable for it; and hold it with one forceps, or pair of forceps.
Then incise the flesh at first, until all of it is finished with a scalpel. And beware of the eye, lest you injure it during the operation; and let your incision, by which you incise it, be either with a spatula, which we have described in the incision of the nail; or with small scissors, with which the asebel is collected, according to what its form will be after this chapter.
When therefore your incision is completed, and all the alguardenici is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt; or instil into it red powder, and things similar to it from corrosive medicines; and place over the eye externally cotton with egg white: so that it may be protected from a hot abscess.
But if some of the alguardenici remains, and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a hot abscess, then leave the eye, and treat it with that which allays an abscess.
Then repeat the operation on it until it is healed.
And similarly do with the added flesh which occurs in the eye from this kind.
However, it is necessary that you avoid the treatment of any weak eye, from a natural or accidental cause, by operating with iron; and do not put your hand to any of these operations until you see that the member is able to tolerate that operation.
Concerning the removal of a cataract from the eye.
Chapter XVIII.
Cataracts are red veins which are woven over the eye, and prevent the eye from performing its function; and they weaken the eye with the passage of time.
Therefore it is necessary for you first to consider if the eye in which there is a cataract is strong, and there is no other illness in it besides the cataract: then remove its cataract.
And it is [the case] that you instruct the patient to place his head in your bosom.
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your judgment: and let the hook be a fine double hook, according to this form, or let there be two hooks joined together in one body, according to this form.
Then collect those veins with fine scissors skillfully, and wipe away the blood hour by hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye and resolved; and beware lest you injure the eye with the points of the spatula.
And let your operation be at midday before the sun, and be very firm in your operation, so that you do not cut except those veins.
And when you have finished, then instill into the eye green or red vitriol, so that by its sharpness it corrodes what remains of the sebaceous matter.
But if it is not possible for you to collect all of it at that time, then plaster the eye with that which soothes the pain of the eye, and leave it for a few days, until its pain subsides and it is secured from a hot abscess; then repeat on it in the same way, until it is healed.
And this is the shape of the scissors.
Concerning the reduction of the nasal bone.
Chapter XIX.
Physicians call the nasal bone a fistula, which you treat with a cautery, or with a sharp burning medicine, according to what has preceded in its description.
And if it is not healed, then there is no remedy for it except to make an incision above the abscess at its origin, and to extract all the moisture and pus which is in it, until the bone is exposed.
When therefore the bone is exposed, and you see corruption or blackness in it, then scrape it with an instrument, whose form is this.
And it is called a rasp.
Let it be made of tempered iron, and let its head be round like an awl, which is pointed with a file’s point, or with a graver’s point with a subtle point: and place it upon the corrupted place of the bone.
Then roll it between your fingers, and press your hand gently, until you know that the corruption has been scraped away: and do this several times.
Then restore the place with drying astringent medicines.
If therefore the place consolidates, and flesh grows in it, and the flow of pus is stopped: and it remains for forty days, and is not renewed, and does not fester, and no accident happens to it: then know that it is now healed.
And if not, then there is no way but to return the fistula to the opening of the nose, according to this form.
And it is so that you uncover the second bone with an iron or with a sharp medicine: and when the bone is uncovered, then take a trepan of this form, let its iron end be triangular: and let its wooden part be a pyramid tapering to the end, as you see.
Then place it upon the bone itself: and let it be near the lachrymal bone: and move your hand a little away from the eye. Then rotate your hand with the trepan until it penetrates the bone, and the patient feels it: by this that he finds wind coming out of it, when he holds his hand over his nose.
Therefore then restore the place with drying styptic medicines, as I taught you.
When therefore flesh grows, and the place hardens: then the matter which was poured out to the exterior, returns to the nose: therefore it is lighter on the patient.
Concerning the reduction of a protrusion of the eye.
Chapter 20.
When the eye protrudes in its entirety, and no harm or diminution occurs in vision: then it is necessary that you loosen the patient; then you bleed him; afterwards you apply cupping glasses to the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then place on the eye an ointment which is made from acacia, and aloe, and frankincense, and sarcocolla. Then bind the eye from above with a plaster with many powders.
And the method of the powders is: that you take soft cloths, many, and each cloth of them is doubled four times: make many of them: then place them one upon another, according to the quantity which the eye needs: then bind over them with a hard cloth a strong binding.
And repeat the cupping glasses with suction without scarification.
Then remove the cupping glasses, and leave the eye bound day and night.
But if the binding is again softened, then it is necessary that it be bound tightly: then remove the binding after a day and a night, as we said.
If therefore you see the eye has returned to the tunic of the grape, it is good: and if not, then repeat the plaster, and the powders, and the binding, and the cupping glasses, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the grape tunic.
Chapter XXI.
When a rupture happens in the grape tunic, then it protrudes and comes forth outside the eyelids like a grape seed, and the appearance of the man is disfigured because of it, and it is necessary to incise it: then the operation in it is, according to what I relate: and it is, that you introduce a needle into the root of the grape from the lower to the upper part.
Then introduce another needle in which is a double thread from the lachrymal part, and make it penetrate; and leave the first needle according to its position.
Then incise the place of the double thread, and tie with it the part of the grape around the needle: and tighten it strongly.
Then withdraw the needle, and place over the eye wool infused in egg white: and leave it, until the thread falls with the grape.
Then treat the eye with that which strengthens it, until the wound is healed.
And sometimes the grape is constricted from every part with a circle of cloth, until the eye adds a large prominence.
Then let it be perforated with a thin spatula, which reaches to the depth of the eye, and let the white humor flow, and let the eye descend, and let it enter immediately: then tighten it, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of Alcomnati.
Chapter XXII.
This sickness which is called Alcomnati is nothing but pus which gathers in the eye, similar to descending water, and it is not itself.
And the method of operating on it is that you make the sick person sit on a prepared or straight seat.
Then take his head with your hands from both sides, and move it until you see the pus reach the lower parts of your eyes. Then let it be fixed, and do not let it descend, and the light will be resolved: and the sick person will see things as he saw: and if it does not descend lower, know that it is water.
But if the descent of the pus is not prepared with what we said, then make the sick person sit between your hands. Then take a fine spatula, and pierce with it above the corneal membrane a little at the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the cornea, until the pus comes out.
When therefore it comes out, then instill into the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey: or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey: then let it be treated with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of water which descends into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter XXIII.
We have already spoken of the types of water in the division, and what is suitable for perforation by a statement declaring and explaining it; then take that same thing according to the health.
Then therefore it is necessary that you make the sick person sit between your hands squarely, opposite the light, near the sun: and bind his healthy eye, and bind it tightly: then the eyelid of his eye with your right hand, if the eye in which is the water, is the left eye: or with your left hand, if the eye is the right.
Then take with your right hand an almond, if the eye is the left; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right.
Then place the end of the almond near the cornea, to the thickness of a ray, in the apparent whiteness of the smaller lachrymal eye: then push the almond with force: and you revolve with it your hand, until it penetrates the white of the eye: and you feel that the almond has now arrived at the empty thing.
And it is necessary that the measure of the journey of the almond to the depth be the measure of the space that is from the pupil to the end of the blackness: and that is the cornea of the eye. For you will see them in the pupil itself, in the vision of the eye, because of the clarity of the corneal tunic.
Then place the almond up to the place where the water is. Then press it downwards, one time after another.
If therefore the water descends immediately, then the sick person sees that upon which he opens his eye immediately, and the almond in his eye.
Then rest a little. If therefore the water descends, then remove it a second time, except that the almond comes out.
When therefore it is fixed, and does not ascend: then extract the almond with ease: and you roll your hand gradually.
Then dissolve in water something of clear gem salt: and wash with it the eye inside. Then place outside the eye a whole pledget, or wool infused with rose oil, and egg white: and bind with it the healthy eye.
But we, and those who are of our time, put on it crushed cumin with egg white.
But if the almond does not obey you to enter the eye because of its hardness, because it is from a man whose eye is very hard: then it is necessary that you take a spatula, which is called Alberid, of which this is the form.
Therefore pierce with it only the conjunctiva, and do not put your whole hand in the hole: and it is only that you make a subtle way for the almond.
Then introduce the almond, as we said.
And at the completion of your work, let a properly made bed be prepared for the patient, in which he may sleep on his back in a dark room: and let him be forbidden all movements, and coughing: and put his food that which soothes his nature: and let him not move his head to the right, nor to the left at all.
And let the bandage remain in its position until the third day.
Then unbind it in that dark room, and test his sight: and make him see things. Then reapply the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not fitting that he do this at the time of treatment, or immediately after the incision: rather it is necessary that he avoid it: because water rises quickly through strong staring.
But if a warm abscess occurs, then it is necessary that you unbind the eye before the seventh [day]: and rectify it with that which soothes the abscess, until it is soothed: then restore its sight, and place upon his face an eye-covering by which his sight is governed under it for some days, he himself being in that dark room.
Then let him go out from the house gradually, and converse in his own rooms.
And know that in the cautery the pupil is not excused from vision sometimes: then indeed let him dare to operate.
And now indeed there has come to us a certain one of those who are from Al-Andalus, who said that there was made in Al-Andalus a perforated tube, by which water is sucked.
Truly therefore I have not seen anyone in your land who has made that: nor have I read that in any of the books of the ancients: and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of the kinds of tubes, so that he who is ignorant of them may stand upon them.
Let them be made of bronze properly: and let their extremities be triangular with this same thinness, and the extremity pointed.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the care of flesh grown in the nose.
Sometimes diverse fleshy growths are produced in the nose, of which some resemble a many-legged scorpion; and of these there is cancerous flesh, stony, dark in color; and of these there is that which is soft flesh, not dark in color.
Therefore, in that which of these fleshes is soft, not malignant, nor cancerous, it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands opposite the sun, and open his nostrils, and throw a hook into that flesh; then pull it outwards, and incise what you have grasped of it with a thin, sharp spatula on one side, until you know that all the flesh has now been removed.
But if something remains of it whose excision is not possible, then shave it with one of the fine instruments easily, until nothing of it remains. But if the blood overcomes you, or a hot abscess occurs, then oppose it as is necessary; or if it is of malignant abscesses, then hasten to cauterize it until the blood is cut off and all the flesh is removed.
Then vinegar and water, or wine, shall be thrown into the nose after the incision. But if the nose opens and moisture proceeds from it to the palate, then know that he is now healthy.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it ought, then know that within it there is flesh grown in the upper bones of the nose, to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Then therefore it is necessary that you insert a linen thread of some thickness, and tie many knots in it; and put between each knot the quantity of a finger, or less; and let the sick man contrive to send one end of the thread into the nose with a probe, or with whatever is possible for him after he has made it similar to azer; and let him draw his breath until it reaches the cavity and comes out over his throat.
And indeed boys often do something similar to this in schools; and it is an easy thing for one who wishes.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end which comes out over the nose, and the other which remains in the nose. Then take and saw the flesh with the knots which are on the thread. Do this until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
Then pull out the thread: and after wiping away the blood, put a pledget which you have soaked in Egyptian ointment into the nose: do this for three days, or more, until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
Then finally put a lead tube in the nose for a few days, until it heals.
But if it needs treatment that dries, use that.
To make a head-purge, with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it of silver or bronze: let it be like a small lamp, open like a shell: and its channel similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed tube, like a reed.
And the shell of the instrument for making a head-purge should be full, uncovered; to which there is a handle at the end, with which you hold it when you heat oil in it, or whatever you want from juices and flowing substances.
Chapter 25.
Often a polyp grows in the extremity of the nose, and it becomes large and increases with the days, until its appearance becomes foul.
And because of that it is necessary that you cut it in the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate all of it. Then place on the location either a cautery or a burning medicine which stands in its place.
If therefore its incision is delayed until it becomes large, then consider if it is stony, hard, dark in color, and has little sensation: then do not touch it with iron; because it is a cancerous abscess.
For many times I have seen one who incised such an abscess, and great harm resulted from it to the one who had it.
And if the abscess is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that incision is possible in all of it, then evacuate the infirmity, and incise it without fear, without dread: and treat the location with that which dries and constricts, until it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
Concerning the suture of the nose, and lip, and ear.
Know that when a solution of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, the operation contributes little in them, except in certain men.
Therefore, when something like that happens to someone, you ought to consider if it is a fresh wound with its blood: and then bring together the two lips of the wound with suture: then treat it until it heals.
And if the lips of the solution of continuity are already separated, and each part has become healthy: then it is necessary to scrape away all the part from its apparent skin until it bleeds. Then bring together the two lips with suture, and bind them, and powder over it dragon's blood and crushed olibanum: and put over the powders a piece of diachylon plaster, and some other of the consolidating plasters: and leave it bound for two or three days. Then unbind it, and change the medicine: and leave it until the threads are cut off by themselves. Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
The method of suture is, to bring together the solution of continuity, either with needles, as we have related in the suture of the belly: or bring together the solution with thread, as I have made you know.
Chapter XXVII.
Concerning the extraction of knots of the lips.
Indeed, many people often have hard abscesses similar to barley grains in their inner lips; and some of them are smaller, and some larger.
Therefore it is necessary that you turn back the lip, and incise over each knot, and suspend it with a hook, and incise it on every side.
Then fill the place after the incision with crushed alum, until the bleeding stops.
Then it should be washed with vinegar, and the place should be treated with something astringent, until the wounds are healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
Concerning the incision of proud flesh upon the gum.
Often there grows upon the gum an additional fleshy growth which the ancients called abuluss.
Therefore it is necessary to suspend it with a hook; or to hold it with forceps, and to incise it at its root, and to let the pus or blood flow.
Then place upon the place crushed vitriol, or other styptic, drying powders.
But if that flesh returns after treatment—for often it does return—then incise it a second time and cauterize it; for it does not return after cauterization.
Chapter XXIX.
Concerning the extraction of teeth with iron.
Sometimes rough, foul crusts gather on the surfaces of the teeth, on the inner and outer parts, and between the gums; and sometimes they blacken, and become yellow and green, until corruption from them reaches the gum, and the teeth become foul through it.
Therefore, you ought to make the patient sit between your hands, and his head in your lap, and scrape the molar and tooth on which you see crusts and something like sand, until nothing of them remains; and similarly deal with the blackness, greenness, and yellowness, and the rest, until they are clean.
If, therefore, that which is in them is removed from the beginning of the scraping, it is good; and if not, then repeat the scraping on them another day, a second, and a third, until at last your intention is achieved in what you wish.
And know that a tooth needs scrapers of different shapes and many forms, according to what is prepared for your operation; because the scraper with which a tooth is scraped from below is different from the scraper with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other forms.
And these shapes of scrapers should be prepared beforehand, as you see.
Chapter 30.
Concerning the extraction of aching teeth.
You ought to treat a tooth for its pain with all skill, and delay its extraction. For there is nothing to replace it when it is extracted, since it is a noble substance: until when there is no excuse for its extraction, then it is necessary, when the patient is compelled to extract it, that the aching tooth be made firm and secure for you.
For often pain deceives the patient, and he thinks that it is in the sound tooth and extracts it; then the pain does not depart, until the diseased tooth is extracted.
For we have already seen this from the practice of phlebotomists.
Therefore, when the aching tooth itself is made certain for you, then you ought to scarify around the tooth with a spatula, in which there is some virtue, until the gum is loosened on every side; then move it with your fingers, or with fine forceps at first gradually, until you shake it.
Then place large forceps on it with a good placement, and the patient's head between your knees: which you have now well held, so that it does not move; then pull the tooth straight, so that you do not break it.
If therefore it does not come out, then take one of those instruments, and insert it under it on every side easily, and attempt its movement, as you did at first.
But if the tooth is perforated, or corroded: then you ought to fill that hole with cloth: and bind it with the end of a thin rod, so that it is not broken at the time of your tightening upon it with forceps.
And it is necessary that you act expertly with scarification around the gum on every side abundantly.
And guard your strength, so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and returns upon the patient from it a malignancy which is greater than his first pain.
And beware lest you do what ignorant phlebotomists do in their haste, and boldness over its eradication, except that which we have narrated.
For very often they cause to happen to men great illnesses, which are more difficult to break, and its roots all remain, or some of them: or that they extract it with certain bones of the jaw: as we have testified to that many times.
Then let it be washed after its eradication with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
But if a flow of blood happens from the places (for it happens often) then pound some of zegi, and fill the place with it: and if not, cauterize it if the zegi does not benefit you.
The shapes of the fine forceps with which the tooth is moved at first, should have long ends, a short handle, thick, so that they do not bend when pressure is applied with them to the tooth: let them be like you see thick handles, until when you squeeze with them, they do not give way to each other, nor bend: short ends, and let them be made of good iron, or of well-made steel, straight ends: and at their ends let there be teeth, some of which enter into others, and grip with a decent, strong constriction.
And sometimes the ends are made like the shape of the beak of a bird which is called a stork, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter XXXI.
Concerning the eradication of teeth, and the extraction of fractured jawbones.
When, in the eradication of a tooth which breaks, something remains, then it is necessary that you place a poultice with butter on the place for one or two days, until the place is softened: then introduce into it a small hook, or forceps: the ends of which are similar to the beak of a bird which is called a stork. This, however, is the form of the forceps.
Let their ends be made like a file on the inside: or like aliskilfegi.
If therefore it does not yield to you for extraction with these forceps, then it is necessary that you cut into the root, and uncover all the flesh, and introduce an instrument which is called a small lever, whose form is this.
Let it be short at the end, somewhat thick: and let it not be soft, lest it break.
If therefore the root comes out through it, it is good: and if not, it is necessary to help with these other instruments whose forms are these.
The form of the first is triangular at the end, in which there is a certain thickness.
The form of a triangular end, slender.
And with other instruments and tools which preceded in the matter of teeth.
Also let there be help with this instrument which is similar to a large hook, of this form, of a twisted triangular end, in which there is some thickness, so that it is not broken: and let it not be soaked.
And know that the instruments for teeth are many: and similarly the remaining instruments are not counted, and a skillful artisan, subtle in his art, finds for himself an instrument, according to what the operation and the illnesses themselves signify to him.
Because there are illnesses for which the ancients did not mention an instrument, because of the diversity of their types.
But if a bone is broken from the jaw, or from other bones of the mouth, or rots: then investigate it in its place with that which is suitable to it from the instruments and forceps which I mentioned in the extraction of roots, and help with a gesti, whose form is this.
Let there be some gradual thickness in it, so that the bone may be tightened, or held with it, and not escape and go, until the bone comes out: and restore the place with medicines suitable to it.
But if there is a small rottenness in the bone, then shave it from its rottenness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter XXXII.
Concerning the sawing of teeth prominent above others.
When teeth project not according to their natural course, the form is made ugly by it; and especially when this happens in women and girls, namely, those who are for sale.
Therefore it is necessary that you consider. For if a tooth has grown after another tooth, and is not located, or its sawing or filing is possible, then eradicate it; and if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, of which this is the form.
It is similar to a small beak, and is made of untempered iron, very sharp at the end.
And let your incision, by which you cut it, be done over many days, because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that another tooth is not disturbed.
But if it is prominent and exposed to filing, then file it with a file of untempered iron, of which this is the form.
Let it be entirely of untempered iron, and its handle of very small points, like a file with which needles are made; with which the tooth may be filed little by little over many days easily, so that the tooth is not disturbed and falls out; then smooth it, and finally shave it with some razors.
But if it is a tooth from which a part is broken, and it injures the tongue: then it is necessary that you file it also, until the roughness of that fracture is removed, and it is leveled, and smoothed, and does not injure the tongue, nor impair speech.
Chapter XXXIII.
Concerning the binding of teeth with gold wires.
When a shaking and loosening of the front teeth occurs from a blow or a fall, and the patient cannot bite on what he eats without them falling out, and you have treated them with astringent medicines, and the treatment has not been successful: then the method for them is that they be bound with a gold wire; because silver turns green and rots after a time, but gold never changes.
And let the wire be medium in fineness and thickness according to the amount that it takes between the teeth.
And the method of binding is this: you take a wire and insert the doubled part of it between two healthy teeth; then weave with the two ends of the wire between the loose teeth, whether it be one or more, until you reach with the weave to a healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving to the part from which you began; and tighten your hand with delicacy and skill until it is not moved at all; and let your tightening of the wire be at the roots of the teeth so that it does not come loose.
Then cut off the superfluous ends of the wire with scissors; and join both together and twist them with pliers; and hide them between the healthy tooth and the loose tooth so that it does not injure the tongue; then let it remain thus bound.
But if it becomes loose or is cut, bind them with another wire, and it will remain in this way for the whole time.
And this is the form of the teeth, and the method of binding two healthy teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one or two teeth return to their places after the fall of both, and are retained as we have described, and remain; and this is done only by a skillful, prudent, and experienced craftsman.
And sometimes something from the bones of animals is fitted; and it is made into the shape of a tooth, and is placed in the place from which the tooth is supported and fastened, as we have said; and it remains, and serves for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Sometimes this ligament which occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which a man is born, or accidental, from a wound which has already healed.
And the operation in it is that you open the mouth of the sick person on his head in your lap, and lift up his tongue: then incise that nervous ligament across, until the tongue is released from its restraint.
But if in it there is something of hardness and knotting, and it is from the consolidation of a wound: then throw into it a hook, and split it across, until you incise the ligament, and the knotting is released.
And beware lest there be a fissure in the depth of the flesh, and you incise an artery there, and a flow of blood occurs.
Then let the place of the incision be washed with rose water, or with vinegar, or cold water.
Then place under the tongue a linen pledget, which the sick person should hold throughout the night, so that it may not consolidate again.
But if a flow of blood occurs, then place pounded alum on the place. But if the blood overcomes you, then cauterize the place with a sluggish cautery, which is suitable for it; then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
Sometimes an abscess occurs under the tongue which resembles a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and perhaps it is magnified so much that it fills the mouth.
And the operation in it is that you open the mouth of the sick person before the sun: and consider the abscess.
If therefore you see it of a dark or black color, hard, to which the sick person feels no sensation: then do not touch it, because it is cancer.
And if it inclines to whiteness, in which there is moisture, then throw in it a hook, and incise it with a thin spatula, and free it from every part. But if blood overcomes you at the time of your operation, then put on it crushed verdigris until the blood is stopped: then return to your operation, until you extract it with its entirety.
Then let it be washed with vinegar and salt: and treat it with the remaining appropriate treatment for it, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of an abscess of the two tonsils.
Chapter XXXVI.
Sometimes abscesses occur between the throat glands, that is, glands which resemble the glands which occur externally: and they are called the two tonsils: which when you treat with what we have said in the division, and they are not healed, then consider.
Therefore if the abscess is hard, dark in color, with little sensation: then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is red in color, and its root is thick: then do not touch it even with iron, fearing a flow of blood, but rather leave it until it matures. And then either you will pierce it, or it will break by itself.
And if it is white in color, round, and its root is thin: then it ought to be incised.
And the operation in it is that you consider before its operation if the heat of its abscess has quietly subsided completely, or is lessened by some diminution: then therefore make the sick person sit before the sun, and his head should be in your lap: and open his mouth, and a servant should take [the patient's] hands, and press his tongue downwards with an instrument, of which this is the form.
Let it be made of silver or bronze, which is thin like a little knife.
Therefore, when you press the tongue with it, and the abscess is shown to you, and your sight falls upon it: then take a hook, and fix it in the tonsil, and draw it outwards as much as you can, except that you pull something of the tunics with it.
Then incise it with an instrument, of which this is the form that follows. It is similar to scissors; however, its ends should be curved, so that the opening of each is directly opposite the other sharply: therefore take care that it is made of tempered iron, or imbued with alfuled.
But if this instrument is not present, then incise it with a spatula, of which this is the form. Let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other.
And after you incise one tonsil, incise the other according to this same kind of incision.
Then after the incision let the sick person gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
But if a flow of blood happens to him, let him gargle with water in which pomegranate rinds have been boiled: or myrtle leaves, and things similar to them from among the styptics, until the flow of blood is stopped: then cure him, until he is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses are produced in the throat, besides the two tonsils. Therefore incise them according to what I said in the incision of the two tonsils equally.
