But if the patient is timid, and does not await this operation because of its horror: then administer perforation as I relate it: and it is.
When it is ascertained by you through what we have said about the signs, that the abscess is watery: then it is necessary that you make the patient sit on a raised seat.
Then perforate the abscess in its lower part with a broad spatula: and place the perforation according to the length of the body, and let the opening be wide, until all the water runs out.
Then put a pledget over the place, and bind it, and leave it so that the remaining water may run out: then treat it until the wound is healed.
But if there is opposition to the egress of water at the time when you perforate it: and this is only because a white membrane makes opposition in the orifice of the wound, and prevents the exit of water: then it is necessary that you introduce a feather into the wound, or an instrument with which the water of dropsies is extracted, the form of which has preceded: or add to the operation of the wound a little.
And know that the water returns, and is collected after six months, or thereabouts. When therefore it is collected, then let it be perforated in the same way, and the sick person will pass his days.
Chapter LXIII.
Concerning the treatment of fleshy fistulas.
Know that in the incision upon this fistula, there is fear leading to destruction in many instances. Therefore, it seems to me that its dismissal is to be saved from it.
And I am recalling the operation on it, and the appearance of the abscess.
Therefore I say that many abscesses occur in the bodies from which the testicles are composed; and this happens from many causes, namely.
Or from an acute or sharp superfluity which is poured out to the testicles. Or from a blow.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it; and the abscess is serious.
And perhaps it is a stony abscess, whose color is dark, and there is no sensation in it.
And sometimes it happens from the knotting and swelling of the arteries, as its mention has preceded; or from the swelling of the veins.
Therefore, that which is from the swelling of the arteries is known by the disposition of the abscess when you compress it with your fingers. And it is not necessary to touch it completely.
That, however, which happens from the swelling of the veins, does not disperse anything from it upon compression with the fingers.
And now it is possible that an incision be made upon this kind and upon the remaining flesh; and it is to cut the skin of the testicles. Then extend the testicle upwards, and draw it out with the tunica albuginea; and free the spermatic cord from the vessels, and ligate the vessels; and incise the spermatic cord after you free it from every part of the parts of the testicle.
If therefore the testicle is consolidated with those newly grown  fleshes, then it is necessary that you draw out the testicle, and incise it.
And if there is adhesion between something of the tunica albuginea, or in that which is between the vessels: then it is necessary that you free all that adhesion, and incise it with a round incision.
But if there is a growth of flesh in the place of adhesion which is behind: then it is necessary that you incise all of it, and draw out the testicle, as we said.
When therefore your operation is completed, then fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine: then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter LXIV.
Concerning the treatment of fistulas, which is with a vine.
A vine is a swollen abscess, involved in a certain involution, similar to a bunch of grapes, with softening of the testicles: and it makes movement and exercise, and walking difficult for the patient.
And in the treatment of this illness is fear, because of that which preceded its remembrance.
Truly it is necessary that we speak of the operation in it according to what the first ones did.
Therefore I say that it is necessary that you make the sick person sit on a raised seat. Then push the suspensory of the testicles downwards. Then hold the skin of the testicles with your fingers, with the vessels which are near the penis: and let another assistant hold them, and stretch them with a strong extension.
Then incise with a broad, sharp spatula with a transverse incision in the direction of the vessels, until the vessels are revealed.
Then flay from every side, as we told you in the extraction of the arteries which are in the temples.
Afterwards fix in them a needle in which is a double thread, and tie it in the first places where the veins meet: and tie it again in the last of them. Then cut them in the middle with a straight incision according to the length of the body, and extract what is gathered from foul, corrupted humors.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds, in which pus is generated, so that the vessels which nourish one testicle may fall away. For there is no harm through it.
But if a vein affects all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be extracted with the vessels on account of this: lest the egg be deprived of nourishment, because of the incision, and waste away, or dry up, and there be no help with them.
Chapter 65.
Concerning the treatment of intestinal fistula.
The outcome of this fistula is from a section which happens in the sifach extended over the belly in the groin region from the mirach of the belly.
Therefore the intestines descend from that rupture to one of the testicles. And this rupture is either from the sifach, or from its extension.
And these two types happen from many causes: either from a blow, or a jump, or a shout, or lifting a heavy thing, and similar things.
And the sign, when it is from the extension of the sifach. Is that it happens gradually over a long time, and it does not happen suddenly: and there is an equal abscess towards the depth: therefore, because the sifach compresses the intestine.
And its sign when it is from the section of the sifach, because it causes a vehement pain suddenly from its beginning: and the abscess is different, appearing under the skin nearby: and that is because of the exit of the intestine, and its course to the outside of the sifach.
And sometimes zirbum exits with the intestine: and this is called intestinal Ramex, and zirbia: and sometimes it happens with flatulence.
And sometimes excrement runs into the intestine, and is retained there: and with it is the ruin of the infirm; because it causes a bad pain, and especially when it is pressed.
And in the cure of the species of this illness with iron there is fear: wherefore it is necessary that the case be avoided in it.
And the method of operation is that you instruct the infirm to return the intestine with his hand to the inside of its concavity, if it obeys return. Then he is laid supine upon his neck between your hands: and raise his legs.
Then extend the skin which follows the skin to the upper parts, and cut the whole skin of the testicles lengthwise. Then fix hooks in the two lips of the incision, according to what you need to open the incision with them: and let the incision be of such a size that, if possible, the egg may come out of it.
Then flay the siphak, and what is under the skin of the testicles, until when the white siphak is uncovered, hard on every side: then insert your index finger into that which follows the testicle, into that which is between the white siphak, which is under the skin of the egg, and the second siphak, and absolutely loosen with it the adhesion which is in the posterior part of the egg.
Then move the skin of the testicles inwards with your right hand: and with this extend the white siphak upwards with your left hand: and lift the egg with the siphak to the part of the incision: and instruct the assistant to extend the egg upwards: and you loosen the adhesion which is behind with complete solution.
And search with your fingers, lest there be something there of the intestine involved in the hard white siphak. But if you find something of it, then push it downwards to the belly.
Then take a needle, in which is a thick thread, made of linen from ten threads: and insert it at the end of the siphak, that is, of the tunic, which is under the skin of the testicles which follows the incision: then cut the ends which project from the thread, until there are four threads. Then arrange some of them over some in the form of a cross: and tie with them the alsifach, of which we spoke, because it is under the skin of the testicles with a strong ligature, until it cannot send anything to them from the vessels which nourish them: so that a hot abscess does not result from it. And place another ligature again outside the first ligature, less than two fingers distant from it. And after these two ligatures leave from the siphak, which is under the skin of the testicles, the quantity of the size of a finger: and cut away all the rest according to the roundness, and remove the testicle from it.
Then cut in the lower part of the skin of the testicles with an incision, from which blood and pus flows: as we have related in what has gone before.
Then apply wool soaked in oil, and place it in the wound: and apply the ligature which we have described. And sometimes the white siphak which we have mentioned is cauterized, after its incision, fearing the flow of blood. For this often happens. And leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling off is delayed, then let it be moistened with warm water: and place upon it that which we have mentioned, until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the remaining treatment of wounds, until it is healed.
Chapter 66.
Concerning a cupping instrument with a nozzle.
In this cupping instrument I have not seen anyone who dared to operate on it with iron.
And the first ones said that it is done as we have narrated in the cupping instrument which is with a tube. That is, to tie the vessels after the incision upon it with ease below; then cut in the middle, and finally treat it with that which opens it, until the vessels fall out: and treat the wound, according to what we have said, until it heals.
Chapter 67.
Concerning the rupture which occurs in the groin.
When a rupture occurs in the groin, as we have said, and the place protrudes, and nothing of the intestines descends to the testicles: and if it descends, it is little, and returns at all hours: truly if time is prolonged with it, a fissure is added in the sifach, until the intestines descend into the sifach.
And this happens from the extension of the sifach, which is in the groin, as we have said. That is, because the sifach is extended; then it softens, and the place suppurates, and protrudes.
And its treatment is with a cautery, as its account preceded.
And sometimes it is cured with iron, according to this method: and it is that the sick person lies on his back between your hands. Then cut the place of the rupture of the abscesses and the protruding part, with a cut across, according to the amount of three fingers.
Then constrict the alsifak and what are under the skin, until when the white sifach is uncovered, which is under the skin which follows it.
Then take a radius, and put it on the place protruding from the sifach, and press it to the depth of the belly.
Then sew two prominent places on the end of the radius from the sifach, and connect one of them with the other with a suture; then remove the end of the radius.
And do not cut the alsifach completely, nor stretch the egg, nor anything else, as I made you know in the treatment of intestinal fistulas.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds. Therefore, when you cut the threads, clean it and dress the wound until it heals.
For the sisach contracts, and no prominence is added to the rupture.
And cautery is better in this place, because it is closer to healing.
Chapter 68.
Concerning the softening of the skin of the testicles.
Often the skin of the testicles is softened in some men, on account of which the flesh between them is softened, and its appearance is made foul.
Therefore it is necessary for him who is asked about its treatment to make the sick man lie on his back, and to cut away all the skin which is softened down to the natural skin.
Then he should bring together the two lips with a suture: and if you wish, first remove the excess of the softened skin with three or four sutures, and secure the suture.
Then incise between the sutures.
Then treat it with the treatment of other wounds, until it is healed, and the threads fall out.
Chapter 69.
Concerning castration.
Know that castration is forbidden in our law: and because of that it would be permitted to me not to mention it in this my book: and its mention is not necessary except for two reasons.
One of which is that it should be in the knowledge of the physician when he is questioned about it: and that he should know the treatment of him to whom it happens.
And the other reason is that we very often need the castration of certain animals, for our benefit thereby, such as rams, he-goats, and cats, and similar animals.
I say therefore that castration is done in two ways. Or by attrition. Or by fissure and incision.
Now in that which is done by attrition, the method of operation is: that we make the animal sit in warm water until its testicles are softened and relaxed, and hang down; then rub them with your hand until they are resolved and do not appear to the touch.
But in castration by fissure and incision, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament and cut above each testicle with one incision, until when two testicles come forth: then incise them after you have skinned each one, and do not leave on them of the sifak anything except the thin sifak which is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that which is done by attrition: because attrition perhaps leaves something of the testicles, wherefore the animal desires coitus.
Then take care of the wound until it heals.
Chapter 70.
Concerning the care of a hermaphrodite in two ways.
A hermaphrodite from men is according to two ways. One of them is that which appears in that which follows the space, or in the skin of the testicles, in that which is between the two testicles, a figure like the vulva of a woman, in which there are hairs: and sometimes urine flows from that which is in the skin of the testicles.
In women, however, there is one type, and it is the vulva of a woman above the pubis like very small male testicles, projecting outwards: of which one is like a man's penis, and two like two testicles.
And the treatment of the three types, two from men, and one type from women, is: because it is necessary that you cut away the added flesh, until the impression of them disappears. Then treat them with the treatment of other wounds.
In the second type of men, however, from which urine exits, which is in the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor is there any treatment at all.
Chapter LXXI.
Concerning the incision of condyloma, and the protruding flesh in the vulvas of women.
Condyloma perhaps is added to a natural thing, until it is defiled and its appearance becomes foul: and sometimes it is magnified in some women so much, until it expands as in men, and it arrives even to coitus.
Therefore it is necessary that you hold the excess of condyloma with the hand, or with a hook, and incise: and do not be excessive in the incision, especially in the depth of the root: lest a flow of blood occur.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds, until it is healed.
Flesh, however, which arises, is flesh which is born in the orifice of the womb, until it fills it: and perhaps it goes out to the exterior according to the likeness of a tail: and because of that some first name the illness a tailed illness. Therefore it is necessary that we incise it as we incise condyloma, and treat it until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of alratica, which is the imperforate vulva of a woman.
Chapter LXXII.
Alratica is, as it were, the imperforate vulva of a woman, or a small opening. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is from a preceding illness: and it is indeed made either from added, newly grown flesh, or from a thin membrane, or a thick one: and it occurs either deep in the womb, or on its sides, or in its upper or lower part: and it prevents intercourse and conception, and childbirth, and perhaps prevents menstruation.
And its recognition is made by the inspection of the midwife, if the obstruction is apparent and near.
But if it is not apparent, then seek it with your fingers, or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is from a thin membrane, and is near the two lips: then hasten, and break it.
And it is so that you place pledgets on the two lips.
Then place the index fingers of your two hands together, and the woman should be on her back with her legs spread. Then spread the two lips forcefully until the thin membrane is broken and the obstruction is opened.
Then take wool and soak it in oil, and place it on the area: and let the woman have intercourse every day, so that the area does not heal in another way.
But if the membrane is thick, then it is necessary to cut it with a broad spatula similar to a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is of fleshly origin, then suspend it with hooks and incise it: and have with you medicines which stanch the flow of blood without biting, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense combined with egg white.
Then administer a wide lead cannula, so that the wound does not heal quickly, keep it in place for several days: and administer a tampon of dry linen, then treat it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
And sometimes the birth of other flesh occurs in the womb: therefore it is necessary that they be incised in this manner: provided it is not a cancerous abscess; for a cancerous abscess which is in the womb, must never be counteracted with iron at all.
Chapter LXXIII.
Concerning the treatment of hemorrhoids, Athehelil, and Bothor rubea in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids indeed are an opening of the orifices of the veins, until much blood flows from them constantly.
When therefore hemorrhoids become old, they become thelil: and I have already spoken in the division of their species, and their signs: and I shall say here which of them receive treatment, and which not.
I say therefore, that hemorrhoids which are in the orifice of the womb, and do not appear to the sense: then there is no treatment for it with iron.
But those of them which are in the orifice of the womb upon which sensation falls, are those which are cured.
Therefore it is necessary that you introduce the woman into a cold house. Then extend the thelil with scissors, or with a rough cloth, and cut them from their roots.
Then pulverize over them at the flow of blood from one of the incisive powders without mastication: such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense, and similar to them.
Then let her push her feet against the wall for one hour. Afterwards, place her in cold water.
But if the flow of blood persists: then make her sit in a decoction of sumac, and pomegranate bark, and galls, and similar things.
Then take barley flour, and mix it with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster with it over her back.
If therefore blood is drawn off with it, it is good: and if not, apply cupping glasses to the back and breasts, without scarification.
Then take wool, and immerse it in the juice of tarthith, or the juice of arnoglossa, or the juice of the branches of alaulat, that is, aglanter, with Pontic wine, and apply it to the place.
When therefore the abscess is quiet, then treat the place with ointments, until it is healed.
Albarth however is red, similar to the heads of alhaasse, and is of a rough appearance.
Therefore it is necessary that you incise what appears of it, according to what I said in thelil equally: and treat it, until it is healed.
Chapter LXXIIII.
Concerning the perforation of the Exitura which happens in the womb.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the womb, such as cancer, and a stony abscess, and ulcers, and adubelati, and alekilati, that is, herpes, aestiomemus, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and ragadiae, and thelil, and a hot abscess.
And I indeed have already discussed all these illnesses and their types, and their signs, and their cures in the division: it is necessary that I discuss in this treatise the hot abscess which occurs in the womb, when it is of the abscesses which gather pus, and you wish to know how its opening is done with iron.
Therefore it is necessary that you consider. Then if the pain of the abscess in its beginning is acute with its percussion, and inflammation, and fever: and you see it redden: if feeling falls upon it, then do not hurry with its perforation: and treat it with that which aids in maturation, until when the acuteness of the symptoms ceases.
Then it is necessary that you make the woman sit on a seat which has two seats, and she is reclined, or she is laid on her back, and she brings her feet to the lower part of the belly: and her hips are spread, and she puts her arms under her knees, and she ties both with a suitable ligature for that.
Then let the midwife sit on the right side, and administer the instrument by which the orifice of the womb is opened.
And it is necessary when you wish to administer this instrument, that you fix with a probe the depth of the woman's womb: so that that which enters from the instrument is not more than the depth of the womb, and harms the weak woman.
But if the instrument is longer than the depth, then it is necessary that you place pledgets on the covering of the depth of the womb: so that the entry of the whole instrument into the depth of the womb is prevented: and it is necessary that she places the rotating part, which runs in the instrument, from the upper side: and that she holds the instrument, and that the assistant rotates the rotating part, until the depth of the womb is opened.
When therefore the exit appears, and is touched by hand, and is smooth and soft: then it is necessary that you cut it at its head with a broad spatula.
When therefore the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place in the wound a pledget submerged in rose oil: or green oil, in which there is some styptic quality: and place the pledget, and extract the section in the depth of the womb.
And place externally over the womb, and over the vulva, clean wool submerged in water in which mallows have been boiled.
Then let it be treated after two days with an ointment which is suitable for it, until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and wound are washed, so that it is clysterized with water and honey: then with water in which the root of lily, or long birthwort has been boiled: then it returns to the ointment.
But if the wound is hidden internally in the womb, then it is necessary that you abstain from its treatment with iron, nay, treat it with that which we have said in the division.
Chapter 72.
The teaching of obstetricians, how live embryos, having emerged according to a non-natural form, are to be treated.
It is necessary for the obstetrician to know first the form of natural birth.
Therefore, among its signs is that when you see a woman being compressed downwards, and she desires to smell the air, and that which is upon her of the birth is lightened, and the exit of the embryo hastens: then know that this birth is according to the natural form, and its exit is head first, and the afterbirth with it: or suspended, or hanging from the navel.
Therefore, when you see these signs, then it is necessary that her belly be compressed, so that the embryo descends quickly.
For when it descends head first, the afterbirth descends with it, and it is cleansed from those superfluities with a good cleansing.
But that which is besides this birth, is unnatural, blameworthy.
Because sometimes the fetus exits on its feet, and its body exits before its head, and its feet, and one of its hands, or one of its feet: or its head and its hands exit: or its belly exits: and perhaps it is turned upon its neck, and similar blameworthy forms.
Therefore it is necessary for the midwife to be prudent, skillful in all these things, and to beware of error and deception: and I am showing every form of them, and what its nature is, and let it be signified by that, and let her attend to it.
When the fetus exits head first in its natural manner, and it becomes strenuous for the woman, and the birth becomes difficult for her, and you see her strength already weakened: then make her sit on a seat, or let women hold her: and vaporize her vulva with a decoction of fenugreek, and with warm oils.
Then let the midwife insert a small spatula between her fingers, and let her break the secundine with it, and cut it with her nail, until that which is in it of moisture flows out: and press the woman's belly, until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then it is necessary that the woman be given a clyster of fenugreek mucilage with sesame oil.
Then instruct her after the clyster, that she be compressed; and make her sneeze with a conserve, and hold her mouth and nose for one hour. For the fetus will exit immediately.
But if two hands of the fetus come out first, then it is necessary that you gently and gradually return them. But if it does not obey in entering, then place the woman on a seat, and raise her feet upwards; then shake the seat upon the ground; and let the woman be held, so that she does not fall upon shaking.
If therefore the hands do not come out, and the fetus dies, then incise them, and extract the remainder of the fetus; and tie its hand with a cloth, and extract it evenly: for it will come out.
The exit of the embryo upon its feet. When the fetus comes out upon its feet, then it is necessary that you push them upwards; then turn the fetus gradually; afterwards straighten it.
Then let him take one of its feet, and extend it gradually.
When therefore they come out as far as the hips, then instruct him that it be compressed, and make it sneeze with sneezing powders: for it will come out, if God wills.
And if it does not come out with what we have narrated, then return the fetus gradually, until you place it according to its natural form. For it itself will then come out with an easy exit.
But if it is prevented with all that we have narrated: then take from marshmallow mucilage, and sesame oil, and dissolved gum: and pound all in a mortar with good pounding: then anoint with it the woman's vulva, and the lower part of her belly. Then make her sit in warm water, until it reaches her hypochondria.
Therefore, when you see that her lower parts are now warmed, then make her a pessary of myrrh: and insert it. When therefore the pessary remains for one hour, then make her sit on a seat. Then make her sneeze: and pinch her mouth and nose: and press the lower part of the belly with gentle pressure. For the fetus will come out immediately.
The exit of the fetus is over the knees and hands. When it comes out in this way, then he will succeed in inserting his hands gradually. Then the woman will be laid back on her neck, and her feet will hang downwards; and she will be lying on the bed, and she will clasp her hands. Then press on her belly gradually. Then remove what is under her feet from the pillows, until she is prepared in the lower part. But if the fetus does not come out, then take its feet and move them with a strong movement. Then press what is above the hypochondria gradually, until the fetus ascends to the upper parts. Then let him insert his hands and gradually level the fetus: and instruct the woman to bear down, until the fetus comes out.
The exit of the fetus hanging laterally from the hands. His hand will be used in the reduction. But if he cannot reduce it, then raise the woman and make her walk.
But if she cannot walk, then make her lie on the bed: and shake her legs with a strong shaking.
Then administer the liniment which you have prepared from mucilages.
If childbirth takes her, then make her sit on a seat. Then strive to reduce the hand, and adjust the fetus according to its natural form; and make the woman sneeze, and instruct her to strain herself until it comes out.
The exit of the fetus is over its neck, with its hands outstretched, and its face is towards its mother's back. The midwife ought to hold the hand of the fetus, then gradually rotate it and adjust it easily.
When therefore it is adjusted, then place on her vulva what we have narrated of oil and mucilages.
Then instruct her to move gradually. When therefore you have done that, then make her sit on a seat, and instruct her to lean to her right side. Then instruct her to bear down; and make her sneeze with sneezing powders.
The exit of the prepared fetus is on the side. When it descends to the orifice of the womb in this manner with the afterbirth, then tear the afterbirth with your fingernail until it is evacuated of moisture.
If therefore it is suspended over the face of the fetus, and its neck, then incise it from the navel: so that it is not retained, and dies.
Then push the fetus inwards, until you place it as is necessary: and instruct the woman to bear down several times: for it will itself come out easily.
The exit of twins, or of many fetuses. Know that twins are frequently born; and sometimes three, and four are born. However, that is unusual and rarely happens.
Five, however, is outside of nature, and they do not fully live: and a single secundine contains them, however many they may be: and they are separated by a sifach, distinguishing between them, bound in the umbilicus of each of them.
And sometimes they exit as a single fetus exits by a natural, easy exit; and they exit by a blameworthy exit, as we have narrated.
But if some of them are difficult at birth, and they arrive at that which we have spoken of from unnatural forms, then treat them as we have narrated: and let there be a skilled midwife, and let her do what she does with speed and slowness, and let her devise according to every form with that which is prepared for her from skill leading to health.
A recollection of that which is formed in the womb from embryos, and is aborted.
Sometimes one, and two, and three, and four, and five, and six, and seven, and more than ten are formed in the womb: and it has been verified by me, that a woman aborting emitted seven, and another fifteen, all formed.
Which was done by the measurement of the glorious wise man.
Therefore all this ought to be in the knowledge of midwives. For perhaps something similar to that will come to her: therefore let her oppose it with what is necessary.
Chapter LXXVI.
Concerning the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you treat a dead fetus with what I have said in the division concerning medication, and it does not come out, and you are forced to use an operation with the hand, then it is necessary that you consider.
If therefore the woman is of sound constitution, and is not afflicted with any of the accidents from which loss is feared for her: then it is necessary that the woman be laid supine on a bed, on her back, and her head be inclined lower, and her legs raised: and a servant should hold her from two sides, or she should be bound to the bed, so that her body is not pulled during the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the os uteri with moist oils with marshmallow, fenugreek, and linseed mucilage.
Then let the midwife anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages: then let her insert it into the womb with ease. Then let her seek with it the place in which to fix hooks on the fetus, and suitable places for it.
Consider then, if the fetus is prepared to descend on its head: then fix hooks in its eyes, or in its neck, or in its mouth, or in its palate, or under its chin, or in its clavicle, or in places near the ribs, and under the hypochondria.
But if the descent is on its feet, then in the pubis and its middle ribs.
And it is necessary that she hold the hook with her right hand: and place its curve in that which is between the fingers of her left hand: and insert it with her hand with ease, and fix the hook in one of those places which we have mentioned, until it reaches the empty thing.
Then let her fix in opposition to it another hook, or a third, if she needs: so that the contraction is equal, and does not lean to one side.
Then let it be extended with an equal extension, and let the extension not be according to straightness only, but rather let it be moved to every part, so that its exit is eased: as is done with a tooth at its extraction.
And it is necessary that that which is between them be softened for the extension. But if a part of it is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife anoint some fingers with oil, and insert them from the side, so that she may roll out with them that which is retained.
If therefore a part of the fetus comes out, then let the hooks be changed to another place which is higher: do this until the whole fetus comes out.
If therefore a hand comes out before other things, and it is not possible to reduce it because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it. Then pull it out until, when it comes out completely, you incise it either at the joint of the sole or at the elbow; and thus you must do in the other hand and in the two feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or a constriction happens to it in the exit, or water is collected in its head, then it is necessary that she insert a spiny spatula between the fingers and split the head with it and draw out the water; or break it with the instrument which is called *Almisdac*, whose form comes with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter which is after this.
Similarly, if the fetus is of a large-headed nature, then it is necessary that you cut the *algengematu*; or pull it out with *misdach*, as we said. Then pull out the bones with forceps.
If the head comes out and is constricted at the fork, then let it be split until the moisture which is in the chest is poured out.
But if it is not entangled, nor does it yield to exit, then cut the head piecemeal, according to what is possible for you.
But if there is an abscess in the lower part of the belly, or if there is dropsy in it, then it is necessary that you pierce it until the moisture runs out of it.
However, if the fetus is on its feet, then its extraction is easy, and its direction to the mouth of the womb is light.
But if it is constricted at the belly or chest, then it is necessary that you extract it with the cloth which you have wrapped around your hand, and split the belly or chest until that which is in them runs out.
But if the remaining limbs are removed and the head remains, then let the midwife insert her left hand. Therefore if the mouth of the womb is open, then let her insert her hand into the depth of the womb, and search with it for the head, and draw it with her fingers to the mouth of the womb; then let her insert into it one hook or two hooks, and draw it out with them.
But if the mouth of the womb is constricted because of a hot abscess which has happened to it, then it is not necessary that labor should be undertaken upon it; rather it is necessary then to administer the effusion of humid, oily things and apply plasters; and let her sit in waters which soften and moisten.
But if the fetus is on its side, then it is possible that it may be straightened; then let that which we said concerning a living fetus be administered.
But if that is not possible, then let it be cut piecemeal and extracted.
And it is necessary that you leave absolutely nothing of the afterbirth inside.
Therefore, when you have completed your operation, then administer what we said concerning hot abscesses which occur in the womb.
But if a flow of blood occurs, then let her sit in astringent waters, and administer the remaining treatment.
And I indeed have already seen a woman in whose womb a fetus died; then she became pregnant over it another time, afterwards another fetus also died. Therefore, after a long time an abscess occurred in her navel, and it swelled until it opened: and it began to emit pus.
Therefore I was called to her treatment, and I treated her for a long time: and the wound did not heal.
Therefore I placed upon it some plasters of strong attraction: and a bone came out of the place. Then several days passed, and another bone came out. Therefore I wondered at this, since there is no bone in the belly.
Therefore I thought that they were from the bones of a dead fetus.
Therefore I examined the wound, and extracted many bones, the woman being in better condition: and she now lived in this way for a long time, and emitted little pus from the place.
And I did not confirm this unusual thing, except because there is knowledge in it, and help for that which the skillful physician administers with the hand in the treatment.
Chapter LXXVII.
Concerning the forms of instruments necessary in the extraction of a fetus.
The form of the spindle by which the matrix is opened.
Here below is the form of a constrictor, by which books are bound, which has equally two spindles at the two ends of two pieces of wood. However, this spindle ought to be more slender than the constrictor: and it ought to be made either of ebony or of boxwood.
And let the width of each piece of wood be about two fingers: and the border of each of the two is about one finger, and the length of each of them is a palm and a half: and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two additions of the same kind of wood, which are now fastened to both, whose length is half a palm and a little more: and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the matrix, so that it opens with them when you revolve the two spindles.
Another form similar to that one, more slender, and lighter.
Let it be made of ebony or boxwood, according to the likeness of forceps. However, at its extremity let there be two additions, as you see here above: and the length of each addition is about a palm: and the width of each is two digits.
When therefore you wish to open the womb with it, then make the woman sit on a bed, with her legs hanging down, open that which is between her legs. Then insert these two joined additions into the womb: and you are holding the extremity of the instrument below between her hips. Then open your hand with the instrument, as you do with forceps, equally according to the amount you wish of the opening of the mouth of the womb, until the midwife does what she wants.
The shape of another speculum than the ancients described, the shape of a pusher by which the fetus is pushed.
The shape of the Almisdac, with which a large head is crushed: and it is similar to forceps, which have teeth at the end, as you see.
And sometimes it is oblong, like forceps according to this shape, as you see, which has teeth like saw teeth, with which it is cut and crushed.
The shape of a hook having one point.
The shape of a hook having two points.
The shape of a spatula having two ends for cutting off the head.
The shape of two spatulas, also broad.
All these instruments are practiced, and the more their types are multiplied and prepared by the craftsman, the quicker he is at his work, and the more highly regarded among men, because of their quantity.
Therefore you should not despise any of these instruments, but have them prepared at hand. For their necessity is inexcusable.
Chapter 78.
Concerning the extraction of the afterbirth.
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you ought to instruct the woman to hold her breath. Then make her sneeze with a sternutatory; and press your hand upon her mouth and nostrils. If therefore it comes out by this, it is good.
If not, then take a pot, and pierce a hole in the lid; and put into it herbs that open the mouth of the womb, such as calamus, rue, and dill, and chamomile, and sage, and cassia lignea, and centaury: take all these herbs, or some of them, and immerse them with water, and put them over the fire.
Then place a reed tube over the hole in the lid of the pot, and the other end into the mouth of the womb; and let her hold it until the vapor reaches the cavity of the womb.
Then make her sneeze, as we said. For the afterbirth will come out easily.
But if it remains after this treatment, and does not come out: then instruct the midwife to immerse her hand in sesame oil, or marshmallow mucilage. Then let her insert it into the vulva, and search for the afterbirth with it. When therefore she has found it, then let her take it, and extend it gradually.
But if it is attached deep in the womb, then let her insert her hand, as we have described, until when she finds the afterbirth, and pull it out gradually in a straight line, so that the womb does not fall during forceful pulling: rather it is necessary that it be moved easily to the right and left sides: then let the amount of pulling be added. For then it obeys, and is preserved, and escapes from adhesion.
But if the mouth of the womb is constricted, as we have already described: treat it with sneezing, and with medication from the pot with herbs.
But if it does not come out with all that we have described, then beware lest you tire yourself over it in repeated extraction.
Truly it is necessary that what comes out to the mouth of the womb from it, be tied to the woman's hip. Then give her a clyster with tetrapharmacon ointment. For it will putrefy it after days, and it will dissolve, and it will come out.
However, when it putrefies, a bad odor rises from it to the stomach and head, and it harms the weak woman. Therefore, suitable fumigations ought to be administered to it.
Some of the ancients, however, have already experienced the fumigation of nasturtium and dried figs.
The form of the instrument by which a woman is fumigated in the retention of the secundines and menses.
Let it be made of glass similar to an alembic, or of brass; and let the wide end be placed over the fire; and let the fumigation be placed on the coals: let her hold it until that fumigation is consumed; and repeat another.
Chapter LXXIX.
Concerning the cure of an imperforate anus.
Many boys are born, and their anuses are imperforate, whom a subtle membrane has already obstructed. Therefore, it is necessary that the midwife perforate that membrane with her finger; and if not, let her perforate it with a sharp spatula; and let her beware of the muscle, lest she touch it.
Then place wool soaked in wine and oil upon it. Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
But if you fear that it will become obstructed, then place a lead cannula in the opening for many days, and let it be removed when the boy wishes to defecate.
And now, obstruction of the anus also happens from the healing of a wound, or an abscess. Therefore, that consolidation must be cut, then treated with what we have described in the treatment and lead.
Chapter Eighty.
Concerning the treatment of fistulas which occur in the lower part.
Fistulas which occur in the lower part are a knotting and a swelling which occur near the anus externally: or in the space of one finger; and it is one fistula, or several.
When therefore that knotting becomes old, it opens, and a watery white moisture or thin pus flows from it.
And sometimes these fistulas are penetrating. Therefore penetrating ones are recognized by that which comes out with the excretion and flatulence, when the sick person uses excretion; and perhaps a worm comes out of them.
And some of these fistulas, when they are penetrating, reach as far as the bladder and as far as the urinary passage. And some of them penetrate to the hip joint and to the coccyx.
However, what distinguishes a fistula penetrating to the anus from one that does not is that you insert your index finger into the anus and insert a thin probe into the fistula made of air or iron, when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair into it until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
But if you do not feel it completely, nor does anything like excretion, flatulence, or a worm, as I said, come forth from the opening, then know that it is not penetrating; therefore, hasten to the treatment.
And when a fistula penetrates to the bladder or to the urinary meatus, then its sign is the exit of urine from it, and this prevents the place from being consolidated with medicines.
If, however, it penetrates to the hip joint or to the hip bone, then its sign is that a probe reaches there if there is no tortuosity in it to the bone; and the finding of pain around the part, and that pus always exits from it; and that medication is not effective in it, nor does it consolidate with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas there is absolutely no cure; and their treatment is labor and vanity for the foolish physician upon whom it is performed.
However, in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing is hoped for with a saw, according to what I relate to you, and I have already proven it: and it is so that the patient lies on his back between your hands; and raise his legs above his hips, and his hips are inclined towards his belly. Then insert a lead or bronze probe, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know where the probe reaches.
But if the patient feels it around the anus, then you must insert your index finger into the anus.
Therefore, if you feel the probe has already penetrated by itself through the fistula, besides what you feel between your finger and the fistula itself, or flesh: then you know for certain that it is penetrating: therefore do not labor over it, since there is no cure for it, as we have said.
However, it has been said that in some men the flesh heals, and for the medication by which help is hoped for it is that you heat a thin cautery according to the size of the fistula, as preceded, and insert it burning into the fistula until it reaches around the anus. Then repeat this twice or three times, until you know that all those added, filtered, flesh-like tissues, which are similar to the quill of a bird's feather, are now cauterized.
Then treat it with linen cloths wrapped in butter, until those cauterized tissues come out: then treat it with ointments that generate and consolidate flesh.
If therefore it heals, it is good.
And if not, then it never heals with any other treatment.
And if a probe emerges, and does not reach your finger which is in the anus, and there is between it and the probe a thick covering of flesh or fat, and you see a fistula in what follows the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you proceed cutting with the probe that is in the fistula, until the incision reaches where the end of the probe reaches, and the probe is freed and falls out.
Then cleanse those filtered tissues, which resemble the quill of a bird's feather, so that nothing remains of them.
But if blood overcomes you, and separates you from your operation so that you cut those tissues, then it is better, and of quicker help, to cauterize it with fire or with a sharp medicine.
Because cautery with fire brings together good conditions, the burning of those added tissues, and the cutting off of blood, and the drying of humors.
Then treat it with linen cloths wrapped in butter, or with crushed cabbage leaves, until the place emits pus, and those burnt tissues come out in the pus.
Then treat the wound with consolidating ointments, generating hard flesh: and there are medicines in which there is astringency and drying.
For when they are restored, then it is healed: and you do not fear a recurrence.
But if a fistula has already reached the depth of the anus, and is elongated from the superfluity of the body: then insert your finger into the anus, and investigate with it.
If therefore you feel a probe, and between you and it is a covering of fat or flesh, and it is close to penetration: then there is no operation in that, except according to the way of trust and hope.
That is, because one of three methods is administered in it: either to cauterize it, as we said: or to cut it away, until you obtain its concavity, so that it is possible for you to insert lint, and treatment from nearby. And do not proceed far with the incision, lest you cut the muscle containing the anus, and there happens to the patient an involuntary discharge of excrement. Then treat it with what we have said.
For perhaps it will heal: or you will lengthen that covering, and cleanse it after you have cut it  almost to the anus, as we said, either with a probe or with another instrument of sharp extremity. Then cleanse those filtered tissues which are in the incision, as far as you can with exploration.
Then attempt the repair of the whole wound with an incision and the upper opening where it is consolidated: and a fistula remains open in the inner part of the anus, and it will be easier for the patient.
And sometimes a fistula is sewn up in this way: and it is, when you insert a probe into the fistula: and it is at the side of the anus near the surface of the body with the skin and margin of the anus: then take a probe of perforated extremity, which is like an aleskef needle, that is, of those preparing leathers, according to this form. And insert into it a thread wound from five threads, and around it. Then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula, until it reaches its depth.
If therefore it penetrates into the margin of the anus internally nearby, then pull the thread out of that opening: so that you insert your finger into the anus, and pull out the end of the thread, and bring the two ends together: and tighten both, and leave it for one or two days: and the more the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well, until those fleshy parts which are between the two ends of the thread are cut, and fall away: then care for the wound, until it is consolidated, and healed.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating: then make it penetrate in whatever way is prepared for you, unless it is very deep. For it is not in your power to do this because of the muscle, so that you do not cut it.
Then do with it as we have said, until it is healed.
The shape of the spatulite with a spine by which the fistula is split, whose curvature is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not cut what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter LXXXI.
Concerning the suturing of hemorrhoids, from which blood flows from their incision: and the care of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are according to two methods. For they are either within the anus, similar to bladders of coals, or plums; or as if they themselves were grape seeds. And of these there are small ones and large ones: and blood flows from them always, and there is one, and there are many. And they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those which are outside the anus, are for the most part few in moisture, from which flows a yellowish water, or a little blood with a constant flow: and they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is within the anus is, that you instruct the patient to strain: and it is pressed out, until it leaves the anus, and the athelil appear to you. Therefore, also suspend them with hooks: or hold them with your nail. Then cut them at their roots.
But if the hooks do not hold them, because of their moisture and softening: then take them with a rough cloth, or with a pledget of woolen cloth, and pull them out with your fingers. Then incise them, and sprinkle on them after incision some warm powders, that they may stand in place of a cautery. Or cauterize them according to what preceded in the chapter on cauterization. Then treat them with the remaining treatment, until they are healed.
But if the anus does not obey evacuation, then give the patient an enema in which there is a little mordacity, so that what is in the anus may be washed out with it, and it may obey evacuation quickly, when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are external to the anus, then their matter is light, easy: and it is that you seize them with your nail, or seize them with a hook, and incise them. Then treat them with what we have said, until they are healed.
And whoever avoids incision with iron, then it is necessary that you administer their suture according to this method. Take a wound thread, and insert it into a needle. Then draw the hemorrhoid upwards, and make it penetrate with the needle at its root on the other side: and wrap the two ends of the thread, in the lower part of the hemorrhoid, and let them be lateral: and bind the hemorrhoid with a strong binding. Then knot the thread and remove the needle.
Do this with all the hemorrhoids, and leave one of them: nor tie it, so that an excess of blood may flow from it.
Then place over the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil, or cerate, and place it over the anus: and instruct the patient to rest. Then leave it until it falls off.
When therefore the hemorrhoids fall off, treat them with ointments and the rest of the treatment, until they are healed.
Concerning the treatment of fissures. For they often happen from the dryness of the stool and the constriction of nature.
Therefore, when they become old and medicine does not work on them, then it is necessary that you scrape them with the point of a spatula or with your nail, until they become moist and the upper layer which prevents them from healing is removed from them. Then treat them until they heal as is necessary.
But if they do not heal, then repeat the scraping with more force than before, until they bleed much and are excoriated; then treat them until they are healed.
Concerning the treatment of inverted and non-inverted almirmar, and of dry athelil and formica.
Chapter LXXXII.
Know that almirmar is nothing but a round nodule, the color of the body, similar to the head of a nail; and it occurs throughout the body, especially in the lower parts of the feet and toes; and pain from them occurs when walking.
Therefore it is necessary that what is around the nail be cut, and held with forceps or a hook, and eradicated from its root.
Then treat it; and if you wish, cauterize it according to what preceded in its chapter.
From dry athelil, however, there is a small, rough, filtered, round eminence on the surface of the body. And they frequently occur on the hands of children.
And its treatment is that you extend the athelil and incise it: or bind it with a silk thread: or with hair, until it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize it according to what was previously stated with fire, or with a sharp medicine: and now inverted and uninverted athelil are eradicated with the instrument which I shall describe.
An ant, moreover, is also a small, filtered, thick eminence on the surface of the body, going very deep: and when cold finds it, something similar to the biting of an ant is felt in it, and it happens throughout the body: and it occurs most frequently in the hands.
And its treatment is that you take a small tube from a goose quill, or a strong vulture quill: and place that tube over the ant, until the circle of the tube contains parts of it. Then rotate your hand with the tube until you cut what is around the ant: and place it deep in the flesh, and eradicate the ant from its root.
And if you wish, do that with a bronze or iron tube, according to this method.
Let the top of the tube be of a height drawing a rolled-up stopper, so that its grip and its rotation on the finger may be eased.
And if you wish, cauterize the ant as was previously described in the cauterization of Athelil in the chapter on cautery.
Chapter 83.
Concerning the forms of instruments which are used for enemas in diseases of the anus, and for the loosening of the bowels, and for colic.
Sometimes a clyster is made of silver, or of brass, or of cast or hammered bronze.
And sometimes it is made from these instruments, small and large, according to those in whom they are administered.
Therefore that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small; and of those whose anuses are weak or painful, so also should their enemas be.
A large clyster.
Its tube, that is, the upper end to which the bag is tied, should be wide in this manner: and it should have a distinction so that the bag may be tied as you see, and its lower end which is inserted into the anus should be blunt, that is, not open, very smooth, tapering to a point: and on one of its sides there should be two openings, and on the other side one opening.
And let the size of the opening be according to the thickness of the fingers, and a little thicker, and let the bag in which the medicines are placed be made of an animal's bladder: or of sheepskin, which should be in the shape of a small asofrat, whose root is one palm.
And let it be perforated with many holes as it is rolled up, and let the space which is between each hole be the thickness of a finger. Then insert into those holes strong thread of raw flax, which consists of ten threads, or thereabouts, and sew with it the bag like an asofrat. Therefore, when the bag is placed in it, let it be tied at the head of the clyster between two separating parts with a strong ligature. Then clysterize with it.
And this is the form of the circle of the bags here depicted, and of the holes.
The method of clysterizing with it is: that you fill the bag or bladder with medicine which is warmed according to the patient's tolerance; and beware lest it be excessively hot or excessively cold.
Then the patient is laid on his back, and he raises his legs upwards; and place under him an inthaan, that is, a leather on which people lie, or a linen cloth.
Then anoint the end of the clyster with oil, or with egg white, or with fenugreek mucilage, or the like. Then introduce it into the anus gently. Then let the attendant take the bag with both hands together, and squeeze it forcefully until he empties all that is in the clyster into the intestine.
Then withdraw the clyster, and let the patient raise his legs against the wall.
If the medicine causes him to evacuate, then let him hold it as much as he can. And if he sleeps with it the whole night, it is more effective in its help.
But if you give him a clyster for diseases of looseness of the bowels, and its evacuation is rapid: then repeat the clyster until he is healed.
But if there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus, then let the clyster be very subtle, gentle, from among those clysters with which children are clysterized.
And this is the form of a subtle clyster over which the skin is tight.
Chapter LXXXIV.
I have already spoken in the division concerning the treatment of wounds which was suitable for the book, and I shall here speak of their treatment with the hand and with medicines according to the completion.
Therefore I say that wounds are diversified according to that by which wounds are made, and according to the place upon which the wound falls.
Therefore the things by which wounds are made are many times such as the throwing of a stone, or the incision of a sword or knife, or a blow with a lance, or wood, or arrow, and similar things from many things.
Moreover, wounds which are made according to the places of the body are such as a wound which falls upon the head, or neck, or breast, or belly, or liver, and similar members.
And I am narrating the treatments of certain wounds, so that I may put that reasoning and rule concerning the remaining wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds, because the recollection of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
I say therefore. When a simple wound happens in the head, and there is no fracture of the bone: consider then, if it is from the throw of a stone, or similar things, and it has now broken only the skin: and it is a large wound, and you fear concerning the bad outcome of a hot abscess: then hasten, and immediately bleed the cephalic vein: and do not delay this: and draw from him blood according to the quantity of his strength: if nothing is prohibiting phlebotomy, especially if the blood which flows from the wound is little. And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part. And let the patient beware of repletion, and wine, and coarse foods.
But if something prohibits bloodletting, then let him reduce his food, and not approach meats, nor wines.
And place on the wound, if a hot abscess happens to it, a cotum infused in rose oil alone, or with wine in which there is astringency.
But if you are secure from a hot abscess, then place on the wound, if it is fresh with its blood, and the air has not changed it, the powder which I shall relate shortly: and if the air has already changed it, then place on it certain ointments, until it is healed.
But if the wound is large, or is from the incision of a sword, and similar things: and its lips do not come together by themselves, then bring them together with a suture, according to what I shall relate in the wounds of the belly concerning their suture.
But if the wound is already bared from the bone, and hangs, and there is no retention of it except in a small suspensory: then incise it. Then treat the wound with medicines, and restore in it hard flesh in place of skin, and instruct the patient to be nourished with foods in which there is thickness, such as are heads, and cooked wheat, until it is like porridge: and similar things.
But if you find putrefaction in the skin, and it does not adhere to the bone: then incise all the putrefied part, and throw it away: then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery, or vein from which blood flows: and it is not cut off by medicines, then inquire about the artery. If therefore you find it uncut, then cut it with a spatula, or tie it. But if necessity demands when that which we have said does not help, then cauterize it until the bleeding stops.
But if these wounds are small, simple, then their treatment is easy, light: so that you sprinkle on them this powder: because it consolidates them before the air changes them.
Method of incarnative powder. Take one part of frankincense, and two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of quicklime: let the whole be ground very finely, and sifted: and let the wound be filled with it, and bound tightly, until it adheres well to it, and coagulates with the blood.
Then bind it over with powders, and leave it, and do not unbind it while it remains, so that it may not fester. For the medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes the lime alone does this, when you do not have frankincense and dragon's blood at hand; and the dragon's blood also performs the same operation in many large wounds, when it is appropriate to apply it and bind it, and they are fresh wounds with their own blood, which the air has not altered.
But if the air has already altered them in some way, especially in summer, then this powder is not suitable: therefore treat it with what we have said, so that you apply certain digestive ointments, or apply poultices of barley flour made with water and honey, until they discharge pus. Then treat them with the remaining treatment until they are healed.
But if a fracture of the bone occurs with the wound, and it is small, then extract it with forceps; and I will now describe the treatment of head fractures in what is to come.
Concerning wounds of the neck.
If, however, a wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in its treatment and that of a head wound when it is simple.
But if a nerve or artery of the neck has been cut, then if it is a nerve, there is nothing to be done except to avoid treating it with something that causes strong astringency, such as ziniar and zegi, and similar things. Because they harm nerves and constrict them.
And do not put anything cold on the wound at all, because the substance of the nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is the most superior of the members; and what is used to treat it should be from mild remedies, such as lime washed with fresh water infused several times with oil, or with rose oil, and tutia, when it is also washed with fresh water; and other mineral stones, in this manner, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures; or draw its edges together with powders. Then treat it until it is healed.
But if the wound has depth, or a sinus occurs in its lower part, in which pus has already gathered, then perforate it in the most depressed place in it.
But if an artery has already been cut in the wound, and a flow of its blood has resulted, then separate or cut it, and tie it; or cauterize it, if necessity demands it.
But if the vertebrae of the throat, or the whole of it, have been injured, and the alhuadagi have been saved, then bring together the edges of the skin with a suture over the throat tube, and do not touch the throat, rather level it, and restore it to its natural shape. Then bind it with a suitable bandage, and leave it for several days. Then treat it with ointments which possess astringency and drying properties, until it is healed.
But if the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then examine it: then if there are fragments of bone in it, extract all of them, or as many as you can: and what is not prepared, leave it, until the wound becomes putrid, and its discharge is separated and lessened.
But if the wound is fresh, then sprinkle upon it the powder which I have described. But if bone is seen in the wound, and a fistula forms, then treat it with that whose mention came in its chapter.
Concerning a wound in the shoulder blades.
If there is a wound from a lance or a knife, and you see its depth, then consider. Therefore if wind escapes from the wound when the sick person breathes out from it, then know that the wound is mortal.
But if it is not deep, and is fresh with its blood: then do not put powder on it at first, nor bind it up, lest the blood be retained in its depth and return to the heart and kill the sick person: but put on it attractive ointment, and dissolve it twice a day.
If however you do not have ointment at hand, then place old linen in the opening of the wound, so that it may absorb what comes out of it in the form of moisture: and make the sick person sleep on the wound, so that what gathers in it may flow out.
If however three days or more have passed since the wounding, and the sick person has not suffered spasm, nor a bad trembling of the heart, nor constriction in breathing, and you see his remaining conditions to be good: then know that the wound is safe. Therefore treat it with linen cloths and the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
If however its healing is delayed, and it always opens up again, then know that a fistula has now formed. Therefore treat it according to its own chapter.
And if the wound is from the incision of a sword or knife, and is simple on the surface of the chest or back: then treat it with what preceded concerning suture, if it is large; or with powder, if it is small.
But if it has now made an impression on the bone, and fragments have fallen from it: then explore the wound, and hasten to extract those fragments, if they are separated. If however they are not separated, then leave them until the wound becomes putrid, for their extraction is then easier.
Moreover, the judgment in the treatment of other accidental wounds in the other members is the judgment that we have spoken of.
Concerning wounds of the abdomen, and the protrusion of the intestine, and their exit.
The rupture which happens to the abdomen is great, moderate, and small.
Therefore, from a great one sometimes the intestine, or a number of intestines, protrudes.
Therefore their replacement and their suture is more forceful and more difficult.
And a small rupture also becomes difficult in another way. Therefore it follows that their reduction is more difficult if you do not hasten in their replacement to their place immediately: because it is inflated, opened, and thickened: therefore its entry becomes difficult.
Therefore, on account of this, the middle rupture of ruptures is better: because with it the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is difficult in these two types.
Know that the suture of the vein is according to four methods.
Two common sutures are suitable for the suture of the abdomen, and for the suture of the other wounds of the body: and two other sutures properly belong to the other wounds. However, they are more proper with the suture of the abdomen: and I will relate to you each one with explanation and clarification.
I say therefore, that when the wound is small, and something of the intestine comes out of it, and it becomes difficult to reduce it: then that is due to one of two methods. Either because of the smallness of the disruption, as we have said: Or because there is inflation in the intestine because of the coldness of the air.
When therefore it is so, then it is necessary that you warm it, so that you submerge a sponge, or a cloth moistened in lukewarm water alone. Or boil in water squill, and cyperus, and spica, and bathe the intestine with it, until the inflation is resolved.
Sometimes wine in which there is astringency does that: and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflation.
When therefore you see the inflation already resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which marshmallow, or mallow has been boiled. For by that its entry is eased with a small thing.
But if its return is excused after this treatment, then cut a little in the rupture, with the instrument with which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first treatment is better when it is prepared, than section: and constraint to cut is not done after necessity and failure of the instrument.
Let one part of it be twisted, sharp, and the other part of it not sharp, and let the thin end not be with the thinness of a spatula, but rather be somewhat blunter: and it is that which resembles a cautery, as you see.
Therefore, when the wound is enlarged, and the intestine enters, then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form in its own proper place, if possible on that: because it is better.
But when the rupture is large in the lower part of the belly: then it is necessary that you make the sick person lie on his back, and put his legs higher than his head. And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then place his head and chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly if there is a rupture in one of the two parts of the belly, then put your intention and your consideration always on this, that the part in which is the wound be higher than the other part.
And so it is necessary that you administer that in large wounds, and in medium wounds.
But in small wounds, put it according to what is prepared for you.
Then arrange that a pious assistant be present between your hands, who holds the whole rupture with his hand: and bring together its lips. Then uncover from it to him who follows the suture something after something: and this is the method of the common suture of one.
And it is that you take a needle, or a number of needles according to the quantity of the size of the wound. Then leave from the extremity of the rupture the quantity of the ring finger, that is, of the physician: and fix one needle except that you insert into it thread at two extremities of the skin, with two extremities of the sifac which is under the skin from the interiors, until you cause it to penetrate from the other side, and you have now brought together two extremities of the skin, and two extremities of the sifac: and four rows are made.
Then bind with doubled thread around the needle several times from two sides together, until the two lips of the wound are brought together with a decent binding.
Then leave the thickness of a finger also, and fix in them another needle: afterwards tie it with thread, as you did with the first needle. Therefore do not cease doing that with as many needles as you need, until you complete the constriction of the whole wound.
And let the needles be medium between thickness and thinness: because thin needles very quickly cut the flesh: and thick ones are difficult to enter the skin: on account of that it is necessary that they be medium in thinness and thickness.
And it is not necessary that a needle be fixed in two extremities of the skin which are very close: lest the flesh be quickly cut, and the wound be opened before its consolidation, and they not be lengthened also with the suture, lest the wound be prevented from consolidation.
Then cut off the end of the needles, so that they do not injure the sick person in his sleep: and place on them small cushions of cloth on every side, which hold the ends of the needles: and leave them, until you know that the wound is now consolidated.
And indeed this kind of suture with needles is more suitable in small wounds. Because one needle, or two, or about that many, suffice in their suture.
The second suture, however, is common, so that you join together with the suture four extremities: two extremities of the skin, and two extremities of the fascia at one time, with a needle in which is wound a thread equal in fineness and thickness.
Then when you have penetrated with the needle these four extremities, return the needle from the part from which you began, so that the drawn thread falls from the upper part of the wound: so that the suture is, like the seam of a cloth with which cloths are bound together, and place between each suture the quantity of the thickness of the little finger.
And this suture is known to men.
And with this suture I sewed a wound which had happened to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the disruption of the wound was greater than one palm: and there protruded from the intestines about two palms of the middle intestine, and the disruption was in the middle of the belly.
Therefore I replaced it after its intestine had stood outside the wound twenty-four hours.
Therefore the wound was consolidated in a space which was about fifteen days: and I treated him, and he was healed: and he lived after that many years, occupied in his affairs.
And the physicians had judged that he would not be healed.
And it was by a miracle that I did not treat it with ointment. Because I was in a place where no ointment was found, nor anything of medicines. Therefore I placed old linen on the wound twice a day, and I diligently washed it with honey water, until it was healed.
But I will describe two proper kinds of suture according to the account of the speech.
And it is, that you begin with a sharp suture: and you insert the needle from the outer to the inner part.
Therefore when the needle penetrates the skin, and the muscle going according to the length and breadth of the whole body: leave the end of the sifac on the side in which you inserted the needle, and make it penetrate to the other end from the inner part to the outer in the other end of the mirach.
Therefore when you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate accordingly in the same end of the mirach from the outer part to the inner: and leave the end of the sifac, which is on this side, and make the needle penetrate to the other end from the inner part to the outer: and make it penetrate with your penetration by which you make it penetrate in the sifac at the end of the mirach, which is in its part, until it penetrates the whole of it.
Then begin also from this same side, and sew it with the end which is from the sifac on the other side, and draw the needle out of the skin which it penetrated.
Then return it into that skin, and sew the end of the sifac, which is on the other side, with this end of the mirach: and draw it out of the skin which is on its side, and do that once, and again: and do it turn after turn, until you sew the whole outside according to that example.
And the sum of this suture is, that you sew the suture of the leather workers by which they sew hides: so that you sew the sifac once from one side with one end of the skin: and that you leave it another time from another side, until you complete it.
The second kind of proper suture, which Galen mentioned.
This speech of his is related, that you sew it according to the likeness, by which some physicians sew, so that they bring together each part to its own naturally fitting counterpart: and apply the end of the sifac to its other end, and the end of the mirach to its other end. And that is done according to what I tell you.
It is necessary that you fix the needle in the end of the mirach nearest to you on the outer side, and make it penetrate to the interior in that alone: and leave the two ends of the sifac. Then revolve the needle, and make it penetrate from the outer part to the inner part in the two ends of the sifac, namely on both sides.
Then reverse both, also make them penetrate from the inside to the outside, into the two other ends of the mirach, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the common easy suture, and it is the suture whose mention preceded.
This is therefore also the word of Galen.
And some said. The suture of the abdomen is straightened with two needles. That is, to insert one thread into both of them: and begin with the insertion of the needle, from that which is with you, and make it penetrate to the other side, according to the suture of those who sew hides equally.
And know that when the rupture is in the middle of the abdomen: then its suture is more difficult than in other places of the abdomen.
The judgment, however, of the medication of a wound, is the judgment of other wounds.
That is, because when you obtain a fresh wound with its blood, before the air alters it: and you reduce the intestine, and its own [self], and you fit it: then place on it incarnative powder.
But if the air has already altered the wound: then place on it certain ointments which are present to you, until they produce pus: and the threads fall away, and the sifac and mirach consolidate. Then treat it as is the treatment of other wounds, until it is healed.
But if medicines are not present to you, then place on it immediately, from the time it begins to emit pus, old lint: and change it twice a day, as I taught you, until it is healed.
For you do not need other medication in most instances, when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound will communicate to the principal members in the lesion, then it is necessary that you submerge soft wool in oil of moderate warmth, or in rose oil: and place it around the areas which are between the groins, and under the armpits.
But if he feels pain, or pricking, or putrefaction in his intestines (for this often happens) then clysterize him with astringent, black, warm wine: especially if putrefaction has already reached the intestine, and has become an exit penetrating to its concavity.
And know that that which is from the large intestine is of easier healing: and that which is from the small intestine is of more difficult healing.
But the intestine which is called the Jejunum, does not receive healing from a wound which happens to it at all: and that is because of its multitude, which is of veins, and their size, and because of the subtlety of its body, and the proximity of its nature to the nerves.
