Phlebotomy, however, of the Salvatella vein of the right hand, benefits liver diseases.
And the manner of its phlebotomy is that you constrict the Salvatella of the hand with a ligature, or with your hand, after you have put it in warm water, until the vein swells and is clearly demonstrated to the sense. Then phlebotomize it transversely. And if you incise it completely, it will not harm it in any way.
And take care not to wound your hand with the phlebotome. For beneath it are the nerves of the fingers bared of flesh.
Then return the hand to warm water, and let the blood flow into it. For if you do not return it to warm water, the blood will coagulate in the opening of the vein, and will be prevented from flowing.
Therefore, when you have drawn out the necessary quantity of blood, then place oil and salt on the vein: so that it does not quickly clot.
And similarly it is necessary that you do with every constricted branch.
The help, however, of phlebotomy from the left hand is: because it contributes to the illnesses of the spleen: and similarly do in its phlebotomy, as you did in the second equally.
The help of phlebotomy in the saphenous vein, however, is for illnesses which are in the lower part of the body, such as illnesses of the womb, and retention of menstrual blood, and weaknesses of the kidneys, and old ulcers of the hips and legs, and similar illnesses.
And the manner of its phlebotomy is that the sick person puts his foot into warm water: and makes friction upon it, until the veins swell: and tie above the joint of the foot with a strap. And indeed the place of the vein is near the heel, appearing towards the thumb: and many branches ramify from it in the surface of the foot.
Therefore, phlebotomize it in its larger branch, or near the heel in its collection: because it is better and safer.
But if you phlebotomize it in the surface of the foot, then beware of the nerves which are beneath it on the surface of the foot.
And place your phlebotomy with which you phlebotomize it transversely, as if you wish to cut it: and let the phlebotomist be skillful.
But if the exit of blood is prevented, then bathe his foot in warm water: and let the blood flow into it, until it is evacuated.
But if he misses the vein in the first attempt, then repeat with the phlebotomy a little higher, for the place is safe, and you do not fear harm from it, when you avoid the nerves, as we said.
And similarly with the saphenous vein of the other foot equally.
The location of the sciatic veins, as we said, is near the heel on the outer side.
And the benefit of its phlebotomy is for pain in the hip, when it is due to hot blood.
And the manner of its phlebotomy is that the patient should enter the bath and hurry; and bind his leg from the side of the hip up to above the heel by four fingers, with a long thin band; for it does not appear except because of that.
When therefore it appears, then phlebotomize it according to whatever method is possible for you, or according to its direction: and it is better, either to incise it with an incision, or to cut it with a section. For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in most men.
For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in most men. But if you do not find it, and it does not appear to the sense at all: then phlebotomize one of its branches, and that is the one which appears on the dorsum of the foot, and towards the auriculare; and beware of the nerves, and let the quantity of blood you wish flow out.
Then loosen the constriction, and place leather over the place of phlebotomy, and bind the place. For it heals quickly.
Concerning cupping, and the quality of its use.
Cupping instruments are made from horns, and from brass, and from glass.
And cupping is done in two ways. With scarification and the extraction of blood: and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification, is according to two methods. Or it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And there are fourteen places on the body for cupping which is administered with scarification and bloodletting. Of which one is, the cupping of Alnocrati, and it is the back of the head: and Alkehel, and it is the middle of the neck: and the cuppings of Alhagdahim, and they are the two sides of the neck on both sides together: and the cuppings of the chin, and it is under the jaw in the lower part of the mouth: and the cuppings of the two shoulder blades: and the cuppings of Alachosos above the end of the spine: and the cuppings of the two elbows, and they are the middle of the two arms: and the cuppings of the two thighs: and the cuppings of the two ankles.
And cupping indeed does not draw blood from you from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of that, strength does not fall from them, as is the case with phlebotomy. Nor is it necessary that cupping be administered either with fire or without fire in any illnesses which occur from repletion, until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity prompts cupping because of illness or custom, we administer it at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at whatever time it may be.
Which is because there are men in whom blood is multiplied until it needs extraction by cupping, and they find heaviness and headache in their head.
And some of them find fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. And some of them find itching in their face and forehead, and darkness and dullness in their eyes.
And some of them scratch the place of their cupping. And some of them have increased laughter. And some of them find the taste of blood in their mouth; or their tongue is ulcerated, and they spit blood. And some of them have increased sleep. And some of them see blood and redness, and the slain, and wounds, and things similar to them in their sleep.
Therefore, when we see any of these things, and especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we then order that cupping be done after two or three hours of the day have passed.
The benefit of cupping the nape of the neck is that it helps with heaviness in the head and what is poured out into the eyes. However, it is necessary that this be after evacuation of the whole body.
And indeed this cupping is in place of cephalic phlebotomy.
And it is not permitted that one use it who is of cold complexion, or in whom there is catarrh. For it brings great harm to him.
And because of that it is not necessary that the elderly use it, and those in whose head are cold illnesses.
But he who is assiduous over it, forgetfulness is generated in him.
And because of that it is necessary that you instruct the cupper that he should remove his hand with the cupping glass a little to the lower parts, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
Cupping or Alkehel is in place of the incision of the mediana cephalica vein, and the incision of the basilica vein, and because of that it benefits asthma, and constriction of breath, and fissure of the instrument of breath, and cough, and repletion.
And it is necessary that the Alkehel cupping glass be applied gently, because if it is placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Cupping of alhagdahim, however, benefits pains occurring in the head, and ophthalmia, and hemicrania, and perforation, and pain in the roots of the teeth: and it is in place of the incision of the basilica vein.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper not to press his hand with the scarification, so that he does not cut an artery, and a flow of blood occurs.
Cupping under the chin, however, benefits canker sores in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar illnesses which are in the mouth: and it stands in place of the incision of the alieherich, which are in the two lips.
Cupping of the shoulder blades, however, benefits alchafacham, which is caused by repletion and heat.
Cupping, however, of the inner parts of the two thighs benefits in place of the phlebotomy of the three veins, the basilica, the median, and the cephalic: because it itself draws blood from all those small veins which are in the flesh: and those small veins draw from other thicker veins, until the attraction reaches the three veins.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper not to proceed far in scarification: because the place is denuded of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
Cupping, however, which is applied over the alchausos, benefits hemorrhoids of the anus, and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper that the cupping glass be large, and that it be made of air: because the place needs strong suction, and perhaps thin cupping glasses break: and let it be scarified with a large scarification.
Cupping of the legs, however, diminishes noticeably: because they draw blood from the whole body, and benefit old pains in the kidneys, and the womb, and the bladder: they provoke menstruation, and benefit boils, and ademenul: and they stand in place of the phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and the alhorcobeni.
However, they weaken the body greatly, and cause syncope to occur in many men.
And the benefit of cupping of the alhorcobeni is close to the benefit of cupping of the two legs.
The quality of the placement of cupping glasses is that you place the cupping glass first empty, and suck with moderate suction: and do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but place them quickly, and remove them quickly, so that the humors come to the place with equal arrival: and do not cease to iterate this and continue, until you see the place now reddened and swollen, and the redness of blood appears: then therefore scarify, and repeat the suction gradually. Then consider the disposition of the bodies.
For whoever of men is of tender flesh, of sparse pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once, not more: lest the place be ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to enlarge the scarification, and deepen it a little: and moderate the suction in ease, and speed, and gentle movement.
But if there is thickness in the blood, then it is necessary that you scarify him twice. In the first instance indeed a way is opened for the thinning of the blood, and its watery part: and in the second indeed for the thorough investigation of the extraction of thick blood.
But if the blood is very foul, then let a scarification be made a third time: that the ultimate result may be achieved.
And finally, when we wish to extract blood gradually, or a little, one scarification is sufficient for us. But if we wish the extraction of much blood, we scarify with many scarifications.
And if we measure that the blood is thick: then it is necessary that it be scarified with a deep scarification: and the equal limit in scarification, is the depth of the skin.
What is that which it is necessary to administer of oils at the placing of cupping-glasses, and what the one who applies cupping-glasses should beware of.
Indeed in him whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are constricted, it is necessary that the places to be cupped be anointed with opening, softening, resolving oils: if it is in the time of summer, such as is oil of alkeiri, or oil of violets, or oil of sweet almonds, or oil of gourd seed.
And if it is in the time of winter, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or sarbatine oil: and similar things.
If however the superfluity is thick, cold: then let the oil be marjoram oil, or balsam oil, or pennyroyal oil, or dill oil: and similar things.
If however he who is to be cupped has large pores, tender flesh: then it is necessary that he be forbidden oil, and it is necessary that they wash the places of the cupping-glasses after cupping with rose water, or with cold water, or with water of solanum, or with gourd water, or with purslane water: and similar things.
But he whose blood is of much humidity, let him wash the cuppings with vinegar, or with myrtle water, and sumac: and similar things.
But he whose superfluities are thick, let him wash his cuppings with old wine, or with water of  henbane, or a decoction of dill, or chamomile: and similar things.
And it is necessary that cupping be avoided in the bath, or immediately after the bath; indeed it is necessary that it be administered after leaving the bath for an hour, or for two hours.
And it is not necessary that anyone sleep after cupping.
What is it by which the one to be cupped, or bled, ought to be regulated before cupping?
And after it, it is necessary that you consider first: then if the cupped or bled one is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation prevail over his blood: then it is necessary that he receive cooling things, such as pomegranate, and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and succory, and julep: and similar things.
And put among his foods chickens, and the flesh of young sheep, and foods seasoned with verjuice: and similar things.
And whose complexion is cold, it is necessary that he take in drink syrup of honey, or of almiua, or alsecaniabin made from seeds: and let him take moderately fragrant wine, which is between old and new: and let him be instructed to use a small quantity of food. And put among his food chickens, and canebir; and sparrows, that is, small birds; and he should eat young pigeons.
And it is necessary that drink on the day of cupping and bloodletting be more than food: and it is necessary that there be given to drink to certain men in the hours theriac alfaroch, or a medicine of musk, that is, nutmeg, or electuary before cupping: and before bloodletting, or after: that the principal members may be strengthened, and the blood thinned. And it is not necessary that they take it in heated drink.
Concerning cupping without scarification. Cuppings, however, which are without scarification, are cuppings which are placed upon the liver, and spleen, and gout, and upon the belly, navel, and the regions of the kidneys, and the hip joint: because these members do not tolerate scarification upon them.
And it is not sought by them, except either the drawing of blood from a member to a member:  just as we place a cupping glass upon the breasts in the sickness of nasal bleeding. And we use them to resolve cold, trapped flatulence in a member: just as we place a cupping glass upon the belly and navel. For it itself rarefies the member, and warms it, and removes pain therefore, because it resolves that flatulence.
It is also placed upon the kidneys, when there occurs in them obstruction, or a stone. For by the virtue of its attraction perhaps it opens the obstruction, or removes the stone from its place.
And similarly it acts when placed upon the liver and spleen for rubbing flatulence in both of them.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty with only incision. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of warm water in cases of pleurisy: and that is so that the cupping glass may be filled, and it being large, with warm water alone, or water in which suitable herbs have been boiled.
The form of a cupping glass administered with fire.
Let the width of its opening be that of two open fingers, according to what we have formed: and let its depth be half a palm: and let there be in its side, in the place which is about its middle, a small hole, according to the size that a needle may enter it: let it be made of bronze or brass, of thick edges, smooth, even, polished: so that it may not injure the limb when placed upon it: and let there be across its middle a transverse rod of bronze or iron, where a candle with a fire is placed.
And now this cupping glass is made large, larger than that which we have described, and a little smaller than that, according to the illnesses and the age of the one who uses it.
For cupping glasses for children and thin people are different from cupping glasses for men and those of corpulent bodies.
The method of applying this cupping glass with fire to a limb is to light a small linen wick decently with fire, or a small wax candle: and place it on the middle of the transverse rod which is in the middle of the cupping glass: so that the lit fire is around the lower part of the cupping glass, so that the body of the patient is not burned.
Then let it be placed on the limb: and a finger should be placed over the hole which I mentioned, until when we hold the cupping glass; while we need it, we remove the finger, and the vapor will go out through that hole, and the cupping glass will be immediately removed.
Then relight the linen wick in its own way, and repeat it, if you need it.
In the cupping glass however which is administered in the illness of pleurisy with water, there is no transverse rod, nor hole: and it is not administered, except that it is filled with water, and placed on the limb only.
And this is its form.
And this cupping glass, the larger it is, so that it can hold much water, is better.
Concerning the application of leeches.
Leeches are not applied in many places except in those members where the application of cupping glasses is not possible, because of their smallness, such as the lip and the gums, and similar things. Or in a member denuded of flesh, such as a finger and nose, and similar things.
And the manner of using them is: that you take leeches which are in fresh waters, clean from putrefaction.
Then let them be left day and night in fresh water until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their bellies. Then the body is evacuated first with phlebotomy and with cupping glasses. Then wipe the member of the sick person until it is red. Afterwards, let them be placed upon it.
Therefore, when they are full and fall off, and suction of the place with a cupping glass is possible, then it is most effective in helping; and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then with much water, and let it be rubbed, and squeezed out.
But if the flow of blood is prolonged after the falling off of the leeches, and it is oozing: then dip a linen cloth in cold water, and place it on top, until the oozing stops.
But if the blood increases, then powder over it crushed alum, or galls, and similar things from among the styptics: until the blood stops.
Or let there be placed on the place halves of shelled beans: and let them remain, until the beans adhere to the place, for the blood will stop.
And it is necessary, if a repetition of leeches is necessary, when it is possible, to change them.
But if leeches are prevented from attaching, then let the place be wiped with fresh blood. Or prick the places with a needle, until something of the blood comes out. Then let them be placed, for when they sense something of the blood, they adhere immediately.
Therefore when you wish them to fall off, sprinkle on them something of powdered aloes, or salt, or ashes: and they will fall off immediately.
This chapter is also very necessary in the art of medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations occurring in bones.
Know, O sons, that foolish doctors and laymen boast of this chapter, who have never inspected the book of the ancients, nor read a letter from it.
And for this reason it happens that this method is deprived from the sciences in our land. And I never encountered anything good in it, and I did not acquire from it what I acquired except because of the length of reading the books of the ancients, and the study of understanding them, until I extracted the knowledge of it from them.
Then I adhered to experiment and study for the length of my life.
And now I have described to you from it in this chapter all that my knowledge contains: and what my experiment has passed over, after I have abbreviated it for you, and freed it from the burden of length, and abbreviated it with the abbreviation of brevity, and shown it with the clarity of directness: and I have formed in it many forms of the forms of instruments which are used in it, since it is from the addition of directness, as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary that we say in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of the discourse and chapters, whose understanding the craftsman is compelled to first achieve in order to stand upon their truth: namely, so that you, and those who are ready, may know, or may learn this noble art, and others through you.
I say, therefore, that when a fracture, or dislocation, or sprain, or fall happens to someone, then it is necessary that you hasten first to bleed him, and to loosen him, or both together: if nothing prohibits it, such as weakness of strength; or if the one to whom one of these things has happened is a child, or a decrepit old man; or if it is a time of intense heat, or of intense cold.
Then let him be content with his food, cold vegetables, and the meat of birds and kids; and forbid wine, and coarse meats, and being filled with foods and every food that fills the veins with blood. Until when you are secure from a hot abscess, and you do not fear the effusion of matter to the limb: then let the infirm return to his first regime with which his custom ran.
Therefore, when the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the infirm be fed with nourishing foods, with much, coarse, heavy nourishment, in which there is viscosity: such as foods made from wheat cooked with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish; and thick wine, and things similar to them. For with this regime the coagulation of the fracture happens more quickly and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in early old age and the decrepit, it is not possible that they are joined together and consolidated according to their nature always, because of the dryness of their limbs; and those which are in the end of softness are consolidated and joined together, like the bones of small children.
Nevertheless, nature attaches to the broken bone on every side something similar to glue, in which there is thickness, by which it adheres and binds it, until they are glued together and bound together, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until nothing prevents it from its operations: and for this reason it is necessary that you give the infirm nourishment of foods in which there are heaviness, and viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the kinds of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of the limbs: because a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head: and a fracture of the bone of the chest is different from a fracture of the bone of the back: and similarly all the other limbs are different from one another: and I have come with a recollection of every kind of fracture explained in its own Chapter, distinct from another. And the kinds of bone fracture are also diversified in themselves.
Because its fracture is curved, besides which splinters occur in it. And sometimes its fracture is according to the length of the bone: and its fractures are splinters, that is, splinters and additions proceeding, as they are in a reed when it is sharpened with a knife for writing. And not proceeding: and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head: and there is a fracture a small fissure.
And each kind has its own method or instrument in its restoration, according to what will come to its recollection, distinct in its places.
And from what a bone fracture is known is its crookedness, and its prominence: and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
When therefore there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor sound on your touch when you touch the bone with movement, nor does the patient find great pain: then there is no fracture there, indeed it is possible that there is crookedness, or a small fissure: therefore it is not necessary to move it with your hand and compression completely, indeed put on it of the medicines, of which the recollection comes, after that moment which is suitable for the place: then let a thin bandage be applied.
And know that when a place is arched and split in two, besides what happens in it, truly every part is removed from its fellow: then it is necessary for you to begin from your hour to its rectification, equalizing it before a hot abscess happens to it.
But if a hot abscess happens to it, then leave it for a few days, until the abscess rests. Then equalize it according to whichever way can be done with gentleness and skill.
And know that its restoration and its equalization is easier than of a bone in which splinters occur.
Then bind it, according to what recollection of it will come to you.
But if there are splinters in the bone, then extension of this broken member from two parts is necessary, if it is a hand or foot; or with your hand, if the member is small; or with two ropes, or with a rope and a hand.
And let your position be such that you place the member on a level place, according to its natural form, until when the parts of the broken member are extended: then try to make those additions return to their places with all skill, and in whatever way you can: and beware according to your strength, lest pain or injury occur to the weak one through your operation: and try according to your strength to apply one of two bones with its fellow, according to the best methods.
And it is necessary at that time that you try and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see anything different there, correct it and equalize it according to the measure of your strength.
And beware of vehement extension and strong compression, as many foolish people do. For very often they cause by that operation of theirs a hot abscess, or a contraction in the member: as we have already seen that from their operations very often.
Then, after equalization and proper operation and constriction of that member, and rest and tranquility, give advice to the patient about it. And make him beware lest he move it at the hour of his waking and his sleep, in his turning and tossing, and in his evacuation, and all his movements with his utmost strength. And that he should beware lest the position of the member be a position which is followed by pain. That is, because when he feels pain or injury in the disposition of the position of the member, then it is necessary that you change it to another disposition from that with which he does not feel pain.
And be careful with him that its position be that equal, straight one; so that crookedness does not happen in the member when it is restored.
And the quality of the binding of the broken member is as I relate.
Know that broken members are diversified in their smallness and their size and their shape.
Therefore, those of them which are small, such as the arm and finger and shin and similar things, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft, fine cloth. But those of them which are thick, such as the hip and leg, it is necessary that there be in them a wide, firm wrapping.
Because a wide ligature connects a large member and binds it on every side with an equal binding, so that a blemish does not enter it.
Then begin after your evacuation, from the equation, to place a suitable liniment on it, in soft lint over the place of its fracture. Then begin to wrap the ligature over the place of its fracture with three or four wrappings, according to what the member deserves: and tighten your hand a little with the ligature.
Then proceed with it to the upper part from the place of fracture, and tighten less than was your binding with which you bound the broken place. Then extend yourself with the wrapping from the place of fracture a little, and soften the binding gradually, until you take from the healthy place a large amount.
Then take another bandage, and wrap it again over the broken place with wrappings. Afterwards proceed with the wrapping to the lower part from the fracture. And let your work be in the binding of the wrapping, and the softening of it, according to what we said in the first upper wrapping.
Then place between the wrappings some soft tow, or cloth, with which the crookedness of the fracture is evened out, if there is crookedness in it; and if not, do not put anything in it.
Then wrap another bandage over it. Afterwards evenly place over these bandages properly made compresses immediately, if there is no swelling in the place, nor hot abscess.
But if there is swelling or an abscess in it, then place over it that which settles that abscess and removes the swelling; and leave it for some days. Then tighten the compresses over it.
And let the compresses be from the middle of a reed, wide, shaved, skillfully shaped; or let them be from the wood from which sieves are made, which are made from pine, or gerard, that is, from palm branches, or calingi; and let the size of each compress be according to this form, and this same figure.
However, it is necessary that the compress which is placed over the wound itself be somewhat thicker and wider than the other compresses; and the length of the compress should be according to what the limb allows in size and smallness.
Then bind over the compresses with another bandage, according to the same first binding. Then bind over it neatly with threads, as we said of the binding: and it is so that your binding over the place of the fracture is greater; and the more it is extended from the fracture, the lesser the binding.
And it is necessary that the threads be moderate in thickness and thinness, and be of soft linen.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the practice of foolish men placing their threads from twisted linen cords, it is a great error; because the binding falls with them outside the proper quality.
And thin threads again are not suitable; because you will not achieve with them through the binding that which you wish.
And it is not necessary that there be less than a finger between splint and splint.
But if the patient is injured by the ends of the splints after binding in healthy places, then place under them some soft tow, or carded wool, until nothing is injured thereby.
But when there is a fracture with a wound, and a disruption occurs in the skin, I will treat it with a discussion of it in the second chapter according to its particular nature.
And know that not every broken limb needs to be bound with splints from the first day. That is because, when a limb is large, it is not necessary to place splints upon it except after five days, or seven, or more, according to your certainty from the outcome of a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters which the ancients used, which are placed over a fracture and dislocation.
¶ Method of a common abbreviated plaster, restoring a fracture, suitable for many complexions, especially women and children: because it is made of things which neither heat nor cold overcomes.
And it is to take from the dust of the mill, and it is the marrow of flour which hangs on the walls of the mill at the motion of the millstone: and prepare it as it is, except that you sift it with egg white: and make its consistency neither thick nor thin. Then use it.
¶ Method of another plaster, restoring fracture, and dislocation, and torsion. Take almess, and laudanum, and acacia, and inula, and mugath: and the juice of all, of each 10 drachms, of myrrh and aloe, of each 5 drachms, and of alethil 20 drachms. Let all be ground, and sifted, and mixed with the water of alethil: or with egg white, if the complexion of the patient is heated. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, it restores broken bones quickly, and benefits many people.
¶ Method of another plaster, which also benefits fracture and torsion. Take mugath, and mess, and white mallow, of each 10 drachms, of myrrh and aloe, of each 5 drachms, of acacia 6 drachms, of Armenian bole 20 drachms. Let all be ground very finely, and sifted, and made with water, or egg white: and applied.
¶ Method of a plaster for joints and bones separated from their places: and it soothes the pain affecting them, and a fissure of the bone, and fracture. Let greasy wool be taken, and submerged in vinegar and boiled oil: and let it be placed on the place.
And indeed in this plaster there is no power of restoration, but it is good in soothing a hot abscess: and it expels pains properly.
¶ Method of a plaster which restores a broken bone. Take leaves of the wild fig, and leaves of wild poppy: and grind everything, and make a plaster from both.
¶ Method of an abbreviated plaster, administered in the restoration of a bone fracture: and when you wish resolution of the remainder of an abscess. Take of marshmallow root, and chamomile, and violet flowers, and herb flour, of each one part: let all be ground, and made up with wine, if the member is not heated.
But if it is heated, then make it up with moist coriander water, or with water, and use it.
¶ Method of another plaster stronger in resolution than the first one, when a hard abscess occurs in the restoration of a member. Take of marshmallow root, and linseed, and balaustines, and melilot, and bugloss, and violet flower, and chamomile, of each one part; let all be ground, and made up with willow water, or with sweet water, or with wine. All this according to the warmth of the member, and the quiet of its heat.
Hippocrates however did not say in his book that anything should be put on a broken member during its restoration except cerate made from wax and oil: nothing else: and he related that it should be medium between thickness and thinness.
To Galen however it seemed that things should be put on a broken member during its restoration in which there is a drying of heat, such as myrrh, and aloes and frankincense, and things similar to them from those which we have related.
Method of a plaster which contributes to injury and pain. Take mugath, and chickpeas, and cut human hair, or bird feather, and mallow, and salt in equal parts: let them be ground, and sifted, and prepared: and let a plaster be made from it.
The quantity however according to which it is necessary that the bandage remain, then be loosened, is that you consider then, if no pain happens to the patient, nor itching, nor is the broken bone moved from its place: then do not loosen it for many days. But if intense itching or disturbing pain, or swelling happens to him in the place: then hasten, and loosen it at once, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, moistened: and immerse it in warm water, and wash the place with it, until the itching rests, and the pain settles.
But if intense itching or disturbing pain, or swelling happens to him in the place: then hasten, and loosen it at once, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, moistened: and immerse it in warm water, and wash the place with it, until the itching rests, and the pain settles. Then let the limb rest for one hour. Then place on it cut wool, submerged in vinegar and oil, or rose oil: and bind it on for one night, until you are secure from a hot abscess, and the swelling of the limb rests, and its pain is removed.
Then return it to a fine ligature, and a small plaster: and do not bind it with your first constriction, and refine with it, until it is healed.
But if you see that the abscess, and redness, and pain, and swelling, and all the accidents are now completely removed, and you do not need a plaster and ligature, then repeat it as you did in the beginning equally.
But if nothing of what we have said happens to the limb, then do not loosen it except after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when twenty days have passed, the whole of it, according to what appears to you from the loosening of the limb, as we said, until when the fracture adheres, and the coagulation of flesh approaches over it: then also add to the ligature more than your whole first ligature: and also add to the thickening of the patient's food, according to what preceded its recollection.
But if you see the place of the fracture now drying up, and thinning more than it ought: then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Therefore bathe it with warm water at every time when you loosen it, and let this be done every three days, and lessen the ligature a little.
Through this operation therefore nourishment flows to the limb, and it heals quickly.
But what foolish restorers do in the case of a fractured limb, when it is not restored in the first instance as it should be, and is restored according to crookedness, is an error of their operation, and a great danger: and if it were right, the ancients would have said it in their books, and would have operated with it.
And I have seen nowhere any trace of them at all in it, and it is better that it not be done.
Concerning the treatment of an accident in the head.
The kinds of fracture are diverse, and its forms many: and its causes are distinct.
For there is a fracture which is made by a blow with a sword: and the blow removes the bone entirely as far as it reaches the periosteum, which is under the bone, just as an axe does in wood: and on account of this this kind of fracture is called Axial.
Or a sword cuts some of the bone, and cuts its surface, and the incision does not penetrate to its end: and this kind of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, Elevation absolutely.
And there is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is a contusion, or attrition: and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall on a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which already approaches the periosteum, which is under the bone: or it is in the surface of the bone: and a wound of these two fractures is also either small or large.
And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone in the fineness of a hair: and it is a small fissure, and because of that this kind of fracture is called Capillary.
And of these is a fracture which is made by a fall, or blow from a stone, and similar things, which cause a lamina of bone to enter inwards, and a concavity is made in the place: just as happens to bronze cauldrons, when a blow meets them, and a part of them enters inwards.
And this happens very much indeed in the heads of moist bones, such as are the heads of children.
And for all these kinds of fractures separate treatments are made, and I will come to the recollection of all of that in its place.
And we recognize all these kinds of fracture by examination upon it, and investigation of them with probes, and removal of the corrupt flesh which is upon them.
The hair-line fracture however is recognized by this, that it is detected on the bone, and is wiped, and is anointed upon it with encaustic: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment however of fracture, consider first the accidents of the patient: then if you see from the accidents that it signifies by manifest signification a danger, such as vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of understanding, and loss of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and protrusion of the eyes, and redness of them, and similar to them from accidents in many conditions, undoubtedly it is not healed: and if you see accidents not frightening you, and you hope for salvation: then begin its treatment.
That is, if a wounded man comes to you first when he is wounded: and that is in the days of winter, then it is necessary that you strive in the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to every condition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then it is necessary that you hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before that which is under the bone from the membrane is corrupted, and those accidents which we have related happen.
But if the fracture of the bone has now reached the membrane touching the brain, and is with small pieces, and attraction: then it is necessary that you cut the broken and worn part, according to what I tell you.
And it is, that you shave the head of the wounded man: and uncover the bone in whichever way is possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and that which is light upon the patient.
But if a flow of blood or a hot abscess happens to you upon the uncovering of the bone: then oppose it with that which is necessary. And it is, that you fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil: and make the wound rest, until the abscess settles, and you are secure from the flow of blood. Then begin in the treatment of the bone, and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
One method indeed is, that you incise the bone with a narrow-bladed incisor: and this is its form.
Then administer another incisor after the incisor, a little wider than it: and this is its form.
Then administer yet another incisor, wider even than the second.
And the second method is, that you have at hand a number of different incisors: of which some are wider than others, and some of them shorter than others: and let them be sharp at the tips of their extremities, and let them be of tempered iron, or steel: and use care in striking upon the incisor, so that it does not shake the head, and injure it.
But if the bone is perchance hard, then it is necessary that you perforate its circumference before you administer the incisors, with drills which are called non-penetrating drills: and they are not so named except because they do not pass through the limit of the fleshy bone to that which is beyond it: because the drill has a round extremity above that which is under its sharp head, similar to a margin, and a small circle prevents it from sinking and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting for you to take of these drills a large number, each of which suits the quantity of the thickness of the bone, until there is present for every skull a drill: the quantity of whose sharp extremity in length and shortness is according to the quantity of the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of drills, or trepans.
Moreover, the quality of perforating in the circumference of the fractured bone is: that you place the drill upon the bone, and revolve it with your fingers, until you know that the bone has already been penetrated. Then change the drill to another place, and place what is between each hole at the amount of the thickness of a finger, or thereabouts.
Then incise with the incisors what is between each hole of bone: and make it as thin as you can on that from the end of lightness, as we said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand, or with something else from certain instruments which you have prepared for it: such as forceps, and fine scissors.
And it is necessary that you take every precaution lest the drill or incisor touch anything of the dura mater.
Therefore, when you have gone around the bone by perforating, and it has been separated from the dura mater, if it was continuous with it, and you have extracted it, it is necessary that you shave and smooth the end of that which remains in the bone with another instrument similar to the incisor. However, it is necessary that it be thinner and finer than other incisors.
But if something remains of the small bones and splinters, then take them away easily with what you have of instruments. Then treat the wound afterwards with linen cloths and the ointments which we have mentioned.
And another method of operation is easy, completely far from fear: which Galen mentioned, and praised it with great praise: and this is his statement. It is necessary for you first to begin uncovering the part of the bone from the place where the fracture is more severe and more evident, until when you have uncovered that part, and you have placed under it the end of this lenticular incisor, of which this is the form.
And the lenticular part, smooth, does not cut anything; and its sharp part, going in its two sides lengthwise, as you see, so that the lenticular part is supported against the fat, its sharp side falling upon the bone.
Then strike upon the incisor from one side with a small hammer, until you cut through all the bone easily, as if it were rolling; and you should be secure from the membrane, so that nothing happens to it which you fear at all.
And if the physician were not the most foolish of men, and more ignorant of this, nor were he sleeping, then if something of the membrane remained attached to the bone in some places, he would cut it from it with the end of that lenticular incisor, and free it from it easily. For it is freed from it without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the membrane, and that which is cut away from the bone is its surface, and roughness and fine splinters remain in it: then it is necessary that you shave that roughness, and remove those splinters with fine rasps of which you have prepared a number of different sizes, so that it is possible for you to use the most suitable and straightest of them in every place, according to that to which the operation leads you, and the bone itself which is broken, and its shape. And it is necessary that you use in your scraping, with which you first scrape the bone, the wider of those rasps. Then use a finer one after it.
And it is necessary that you use in your scraping, with which you first scrape the bone, the wider of those rasps. Then use a finer one after it. And do not cease doing that continuously, until you arrive at using the finest and most delicate of them all.
In each of the remaining small, hair-like fissures and delicate fractures, it is necessary that you administer care according to that which leads to its straightening.
And it is a matter which is not hidden from anyone who has any study in this art, and has considered what we have written and shown in large fractures.
But if the exposed membrane remains when you remove the bone, then it is necessary that you take a linen cloth, according to the size of the wound, and submerge it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and submerge it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth: and do this as easily as you can so that the fat is not burdened.
Then apply a broad bandage over it; and do not bind it tightly except to the extent that it holds the wound only: and leave it with this dressing for one day, or two days, until you are secure from a hot abscess. Then loosen it, and afterwards apply medicines with which there is superfluous dryness: such as lily roots, and herb flour, and frankincense powder, and birthwort, and similar things; and you should make a powder from these medicines, which is sprinkled over the wound when dry.
And universally apply to it any medicine whose property is to cleanse and not to bite. And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment is clean; nor should there be any dirt from ointment, nor any filth.
And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment is clean; nor should there be any dirt from ointment, nor any filth. Nor allow pus to collect in it at all: because when pus collects on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts it and putrefies it, and great harm befalls the patient afterwards: and there occurs in the dura mater, when the bone is cleaned from it, especially when its care is neglected, blackness on its surface.
Consider then, if the accidents which we have mentioned befall the patient, know that it is undoubtedly harmful.
And if blackness does not occur except from the medicine which is placed upon it, and it is in the virtue of that medicine to make that blackness: then it is necessary that you take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and apply a cloth with them. Then place it on the dura mater; afterwards treat it with the kinds of treatment which are suitable, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that only two of its upper parts, or one of them, are broken from the nose; because these are two bones: since its lower part is cartilaginous, it is not broken, and nothing happens to it except abrasion, and crookedness, and flatness.
If one of the two parts of it is broken, then it is necessary that you insert the little finger into the hole of the nose, and equalize that fracture with your index finger and thumb from the outside, until the nose returns to its natural form; and let this be done skillfully and easily. And beware that no pain occurs to the patient through your operation.
But if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it is necessary that it be set with the end of a radius, in which there is a small thickness.
But if the fracture is in two parts, then do likewise; and hasten with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, at the subsidence of the warm abscess.
Then insert into the end of the nose a pledget of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part; or insert two pledgets if the fracture is in two parts; and let the thickness of the pledget be of such a quantity as to fill the opening of the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers said that the pledgets should be infused in butter, and changed every day. That, however, does not seem to me good; rather, it is necessary that the pledget be infused in egg white, mixed with mill dust. Then leave the pledget until the bone is fixed and the cartilage hardens.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose in place of a pledget, after a soft cloth has been wrapped around it; and it happens that it holds the fracture of the nose more firmly, and that the patient is not prevented from breathing.
And this is not necessary. If you wish, do that; and if you wish, make pledgets.
But if a hot abscess should again occur in the nose while you are operating, plaster the nose with cerate, or with cotton infused in rose oil, or a little diachylon.
But if a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary that you plaster it with sesame flour and frankincense powder mixed with egg white. Then place soft lint over it, and do not bind the nose with anything at all.
But if the bones of the nose are broken by a small fracture, or are comminuted, then it is necessary that you incise over them and extract them with instruments which are suitable for them. Then sew the incision, and treat it with that which causes it to granulate and consolidate, from suitable plasters or ointments.
But if a wound occurs between the nostrils, then it is necessary that you treat it with linen strips; and apply a lead cannula until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of the lower jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and its fracture is not accompanied by a wound: then see if its fracture is only external, and is not broken in two, and is bent inwards: then its recognition is easy.
Therefore, if its fracture is on the right side, you should insert the index finger of the left hand into the mouth of the patient; similarly if the fracture is in the left mandible, then insert the index finger of the right hand: and push with it the hump of the fracture from the inside outwards gently with your other hand from the outside of the bone properly, and with it equalize it.
But if the fracture of the mandible is already separated into two, then it is necessary to administer extension from two parts, according to straightness, until its equalization is possible.
But if shaking or disruption has already happened in the teeth, then bind those which you hope will remain with a thread of gold, or silver, or bristle. Then place cerate over the broken mandible. Then place a doubled cloth over it, and place over the cloth a large, properly made splint; or a piece of sole leather equal in length to the mandible.
Then bind it from above, according to how its bandage is prepared, and how its application is suitable, until it is not destroyed; and instruct the patient to use tranquility and quiet; and give him for food light drinks.
But if you estimate that something of the form is already altered in some way, then hasten and unbind it on the third day. Then rectify what is altered from it, and plaster it with mill dust, with egg white, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the cerate; and place soft wool over the plaster.
While therefore that plaster remains attached to it, and the arrangement of the bone is not altered, leave it, lest you unbind it, until it is healed; or bind the fracture. For often this fracture is bound in three weeks.
But if a warm abscess should happen in the meantime, then administer what we have often said in its sedation, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider. For if a fragment is now separated from the bone, or from the splinter: then be skillful in this, so that you remove those splinters with what is suitable for you in their removal from among the instruments.
But if the opening of the wound is narrow, then enlarge it with a fleam according to the amount of your need.
Then when you have removed those splinters, and nothing of them remains, sew then the opening of the wound, if it is wide; and if not, place upon it of the plasters which are suitable for it, and they will cause it to grow flesh, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the clavicle, when it is broken.
Indeed, the clavicle is most often broken on the anterior side from the side of the shoulder, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it is either broken and separated into two, except that splinters occur in it: and it is easier to restore it.
Or so that splinters occur in the bone: and it is more difficult to restore.
Or so that there is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation in it when the fracture is without a wound, is that you cause two assistants to be present, so that one of them holds the aid which follows the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck toward the part.
Then equalize the fracture with your fingers, until its form is made, according to what is necessary: and let there be in it neither prominence nor concavity.
But if you need more extension, then it is necessary that you place under the armpit of the patient a sphere of cloth or wool: and let its size be according to the quantity of your necessity: and extend and elevate the clavicle, and tighten the sphere with your hand, until you equalize the fracture, according to what is necessary.
But if you cannot pull the end of the clavicle outwards because it has reached the depth, then it is necessary that the patient be laid on his back upon his neck; and place under his shoulder a cushion moderate in size, and let an assistant press his shoulder downwards, until the bone of the clavicle which is deep is raised upwards; and then rectify the fracture and equalize it with your fingers.
But if you feel a splinter from the clavicle, and it moves, then it is necessary that you make an incision upon it, and extract that splinter easily.
But if it is retained in the bone, then make an incision in it with one of the scalpels which you have prepared for it, after you have placed under the clavicle the instrument which preserves the *sifach*: and this instrument is of wood or of iron. And this is its form.
It is similar to a spoon which has no concavity.
And let its width be according to that which you need of it, from the size of the bone, and its smallness. Its length however, also according to what is possible for you for the operation.
And let it have two ends, as you see: and one end be wide, and the other narrow.
But if the disruption of the wound which you cut when you extracted the splinter of bone, is wide, and you are secure from a hot abscess: then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a hot abscess: then fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's fissure.
But if a hot abscess happens, then dip a cloth in rose oil, and vinegar, and wine, and place it on top.
But a constriction of the bone when it is without a wound, and without a cut, is that you place on the bone a plaster made from mill flour with egg white. And place a soft pledget on top of it. Then place a sphere under its tickler, if you need that. Then take a himematem, that is, a wheel which the Saracens hold, which is very long: and let its width be one palm, or about that. Then place a doubled pad over the pledget and plaster. Then take a small splint from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and similarly in length. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the splint in the place of the alhihemat which falls upon the fracture and the place. Then place the alhihemat over the fracture as it is rolled up, and wrap it over its neck, and under its healthy little finger, and under its injured little finger: and bring it over every part, and as you see that the bandage holds the fracture with a decent bandage: and that is, what is not hidden from you, and the whole, so that the splint is not removed from above the broken bone. And on account of this it is necessary that you look at the patient every day. Whenever therefore the bandage softens, and you see the splint is now being removed: then straighten it, and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's sleep on his back, and place under his little finger at his sleep a small cushion at night, so that with it his aid may be raised from his side. Therefore the fracture of the clavicle is elevated by the elevation of the shoulder.
Or bind his arm to his neck; and do not loosen the bandage, unless an accident occurs at the site from itching or from an abscess, until twelve days.
Then renew the plaster, if you see its surface adhering to it. And add to the constriction, and leave it until it is restored and coagulated.
And indeed, the fracture of the clavicle is constricted and strengthened mostly within twenty-eight days; and sometimes it happens in some men in less time than that.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the scapula.
The scapula is rarely fractured, and in its broad part; and only its margins are fractured from it.
Therefore, when a place is fractured from it, and it is fractured in its middle, and this is not known except by touch: then, according to what the figure of the fracture is, attempt its preparation. And reduce it to its natural form in every way possible to your skill.
Then place mill dust with egg white over the place, and soft lint, and place a small cushion of doubled cloth on top. Then place a broad splint of thin board over it, according to the quantity of the whole scapula, or a little larger.
But if there is a concavity under the splint in any place of the scapula, then equalize that concavity with soft lint until the splint descends evenly.
Then bind over it with a long bandage, with decent constriction and firmness of the splint, until it is not moved from its place.
And examine the bandage every day. Whenever, therefore, the bandage loosens, tighten it, and adjust the splint if it moves from its place; and let the patient lie on his healthy side.
And indeed, the fracture is healed in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When therefore that number is completed, then unbind the bandage: and you shall be in safety. For it is from bones whose consolidation is not feared, nor their separation.
But if a fragment of bone protrudes and punctures under the skin: then incise over it, and remove it. And do what we have said in the fracture of the clavicle from the settling of a warm abscess, if anything should result from it.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the breastbone.
The breastbone is sometimes fractured in its middle, and this rarely happens. Its extremities, however, are very often fractured and crushed by accidents.
When its middle, which inclines downwards, is fractured, and severe pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing occur; and perhaps he vomits blood, and a hollow in the fractured bone is evident and not hidden from the sense, its restoration is that the patient be laid on his back; and place a cushion between his shoulder blades.
Then press his shoulders, and bring together the ribs from both sides or flanks with your hands; and carefully make it even, in every way that is possible and suitable, until the shape of the bone is restored as it should be.
Then place a plaster and lint over it, and place on top a splint of thin willow or alder board, or similar light materials, after you have wrapped it in cloth. Then carefully bind it over the fractured bone so that it is not moved; and go with the bandage around the back several times, and tighten it with a suitable constriction.
Then check the bandage every hour, and whenever it loosens, tighten it: and if necessity demands its loosening because of itching, or ulceration which occurs in the place, or pain, or abscess: then hasten, and loosen it, and remove the plaster, and correct what has occurred according to the method of its treatment.
Then replace the plaster, if you see the need for it, and apply a bandage until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of ribs, when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in the thick parts which follow the back, and at their extremities from the front. And attrition does not happen to them except because they are cartilaginous. And its knowledge is not hidden from the sense upon examination with the fingers.
And its restoration is done in such a way that you equalize the fracture with the fingers as much as possible, until the shape is equalized, as is necessary. Then apply plasters, and bind the broken bone with a splint, if it needs it.
And if the fracture of the ribs is sloping inwards: then intense pain and a stabbing pain happens to the patient, like the stabbing pain that happens to someone who has pleurisy: because the bone pierces the membrane, and also shortness of breath, cough, and vomiting of much blood happens to him: and this is difficult to cure.
And the ancients have devised many methods for it: and some of them say that you should give the patient foods that produce inflammation and flatulence, so that the belly is inflamed and distended, and the fracture is pushed outwards.
But we avoid causing a rapid hot abscess if it does not occur. But if it has already occurred, then add to it.
And some of them said: a cupping glass should be placed on the area, then sucked with force, and this is more reasonable. Nevertheless, it is feared that the cupping glass will draw superfluities to the area, because of the weakened condition.
And some of them said: the area should be covered with wool that has been submerged in hot oil: and small cushions should be placed between the ribs until it is full: so that the bandage is even when you wrap it around the circumference.
Then the patient should be treated for pleurisy with diet and medicine.
But if a severe condition constricts the patient, which he cannot tolerate, and the bone pierces the covering with a lacerating puncture, and we fear for the patient: then it is necessary that we incise the area and expose the fractured rib. Then we place on it the instrument which preserves the sifac, the mention of which preceded: and we incise the bone easily, and we cut it.
Then we bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with suture: and we treat it with plasters until it heals.
But if a hot abscess should occur meanwhile, then hurry, and dip pledgets in rose oil, and place them on the area: and the patient should be treated with what is suitable for the abscess.
Meanwhile, also, let him be laid on the side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
Fracture sometimes occurs to the bones of the neck, and attrition happens to them rarely; and similarly also to the vertebrae of the back. Therefore when this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not, then consider. For if you see his hands already softened and numb and dead, and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor contract them; and when you contract them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then know that he will not be healed, and in most instances it is mortal.
But if he moves them, and feels constriction with the fingers and the prick: then know that the bone of the neck is safe, and that the sick man will be healed with treatment.
But if something similar to that happens to the vertebrae of the back, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not: then consider his feet; then if you see that they are softened, and that which happened in the hands happens in them: then when he lies on his back, flatulence and excretion come out involuntarily; and when he is laid on his belly, excretion comes out involuntarily; and when he is laid on his back, and wishes to urinate, and cannot: then know that it is mortal. Therefore do not labor in his cure.
If, however, nothing of that happens to him, the matter is less serious.
And the treatment of what happens from that is that you try to sedate the hot abscess: thus, you put on the affected vertebra rose oil alone; or with roasted egg yolks.
Put that on it several times a day, until the hot abscess is sedated: then put on the place one of the strengthening, drying plasters, and bind it on tightly: and instruct the patient to use rest and quiet. Nor let him sleep on the side where he finds pain, until it is healed.
But if it happens at the rubbing of the bone, saturated, or something that is already separated from it: then it is necessary that you cut above the skin, and remove it. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a suture. Then treat it with some incarnative ointment, until it is healed.
But if the last bone of the alhausos is broken, and it is the end of the tail: then it is necessary that you insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and set the broken bone with the other hand, as much as possible, and with a second setting.
Then put it on, and remove it: and treat the wound with the treatment which preceded, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the hip.
Chapter XI.
The bones of the hips are rarely broken. But if they are broken, then their fracture is nothing but a comminution at their extremities: and they are split lengthwise, and are displaced inwards: and the patient experiences pain in the place, and a prickling sensation: and the leg which is broken becomes numb.
And its restoration is such that you extend your hand over it, until you stand over the fracture in the way its shape is.
But if the fracture is only at its extremities, then set the fracture, according to what is prepared for you from the setting, until it is similar to its natural shape.
But if the fracture is lengthwise, or is already displaced inwards: then make the patient lie on his belly, until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. When therefore you have set it, place a plaster over it: then place over it a splint of wood, or of leather: and bind it tightly above which you do not fear a displacement of the fracture, nor the removal of the splint.
And equalize the concavity of those with that which fills it, until the binding encompasses it according to equality: and instruct the patient to sleep on his back, or on his sound side.
But if a hot abscess should occur to him, then cease from its extension and its restoration, until the hot abscess subsides: and place upon it what reduces it, according to what precedes, then return to its restoration: and bind it as is necessary.
If however it happens in a projecting bone, or is comminuted, extract it.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the Ulna.
The ulna is that which is between the elbow and the head of the scapula.
If therefore it is fractured, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form which follows.
And two bandages are tied at the two extremities.
Then let it be suspended from a high place; and let the patient sit on a seat. Then let him throw his fractured arm onto the wood, until it reaches its armpit, in the middle of which is attached the curve of the wood.
Then hang something heavy from above it, so that the assistant stretches it downwards. Then let the physician equalize the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns as it ought.
And another method is that the patient lies on his back: and suspend his hand from his neck with a ligature. Then instruct two assistants, that one of them hold that which is above the fracture with his hands: and that the other hold that which is below it, and each of them stretch to his own part.
And if you wish a stronger extension, then bind below the fracture with a ligature, and above it with another ligature: and let each of the two assistants stretch to his two parts.
If therefore the fracture is near the extremity of the humerus, then it is necessary that one ligature be placed under the armpit, and another under the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow: then it is necessary that a ligature be placed above that place, and above the elbow itself.
Then set the fracture easily without effort, until when the fracture is set as it should be, and a good application is applied: then bind it, if a hot abscess does not occur.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave off the binding until the seventh day. And place upon it cut wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until when the abscess is quiet: then bind it: and the method of binding it is, to place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a bandage of new cloth over the plaster. Then let him place the arm on a support, and place his open hand upon his shoulder.
And place a cloth, and bind the arm to the support so that the arm stands in the place of the splints: if nothing prevents you from it, nor is anything changed in you from the broken bone.
But if you fear that something will be changed in you from the broken bone, then use splints: and it is that you place over the fracture itself a splint which is broader and stronger than the other splints. And place between each splint the width of a finger; and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
Then bind over the splints the bandage which I described at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandage over the place of the fracture be tighter. And the more the fracture is lengthened, the less tight should the bandage be.
If therefore you see the placement of the splints and the bandage as we have said, at the hour in which you restore the limb immediately, then do so; and if you fear a hot abscess, then leave off the bandage and splints until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then examine the bandage in all three days, lest there occur in the place itching, or swelling, or the food be prevented from reaching the limb, because of the tightness of the bandage. Therefore rectify all that, according to what we have said.
But if you are confident that nothing will happen from it, then do not loosen the bandage except after three days, or more.
And let the sleep of the sick man be upon his back, and his hands upon his stomach; and place under the injured part a cushion filled with wool evenly.
And observe at every hour of the night or day, lest the shape of the broken limb be loosened, or the bandage softened. Therefore rectify all that by your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order which we have given beforehand, so that the food may be light at first, until the mouth begins to tighten; then it is necessary that you increase his food.
For it is the custom of a broken bone and leg, that it be bound for forty days; then therefore it is necessary that it be unbound, and a bath be administered, and it be treated with ointments which will rectify it.
But if the fracture be foul, and crushed, then do not unbind the bandage and splints until fifty days, and even up to two months.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the two focilia.
Of which one is small; and it is that which follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is that which is placed under the small one from the lower part.
Perhaps therefore only the larger bone is broken, or the smaller; and perhaps they are broken together.
When therefore the small upper bone is broken, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower bone is broken, its fracture is bad, and its healing difficult.
And it is worse when both bones are broken together.
If therefore the bone which is broken is the small upper bone, then it is fitting for the physician in its restoration, that he apply a small extension with ease, until he sets it.
But if it is the large bone which is broken: then it is necessary that he apply a much stronger constriction.
And it is necessary that he place the shape of the hand in its restoration, and its extension stretched out upon a cushion. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers: and let the joint be lower than all the fingers, or the rest: and let the patient sit squarely upon himself, and let the cushion be before him, or directly in front of him, at a height such that the patient does not incur labour.
Then let the assistant extend the arm from the lower part, either with his hand or with a ligature. And let another assistant extend from above similarly.
Then let the physician set the bone until he restores it according to the best shape that he can.
But if in the fracture of the bone there are splinters, then strive to restore each fragment to its place by your skill.
If however a separated splinter appears in it, and it pierces the skin, and there is no hope for its restoration: then incise over it, and remove it according to the method which we have described in what precedes.
But if the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter for that, therefore take its treatment from there.
But if a hot abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then smear a cloth with cerate made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be medium between thickness and thinness: and bind the cloth over it with a fine bandage, until when the abscess subsides: then remove the cerate, and apply a plaster prepared from the powder of the molendinum with egg white. Then apply splints, and let the splint which is placed over the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most arrangements: whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones together.
Then apply your bandage over the place of the fracture more strongly and tightly, and somewhat more with the bandage to the upper parts of the fracture, or to the lower parts of it: or apply a bandage somewhat softer than that, according to what the previous recollection of it in the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths which are wrapped around the fracture be soft, moist cloths: and let them not be hard, rough. And let the thread with which it is bound be of linen properly, medium between fine and coarse, as we have narrated.
And consider the member and the binding after days. If something happens which requires rectification, such as itching which happens to the member: then it is necessary that you bathe the member with warm water, until that itching ceases, and you leave the member unbound, until it rests, then repeat the bandage.
But if the bandage is now softened, and the bone now removed, and similar things: then rectify that whole thing with your skill, and consider again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the member, because of the excess of the binding, then it is necessary that you soften it a little, and leave it for some days, until nourishment flows to it, then bind it.
But if nothing of what we have said happens to the patient, then it is not necessary that it be loosened, except after twenty days, or about that many.
Then let the patient's hand be suspended to his neck. And let his neck be level, and be kept by its own strength from agitated movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All that is according to the dispositions of complexions, and dispositions of strength.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the hand.
Know that fracture rarely happens to the back of the hand and the knuckles of the fingers. And it does not happen to it, except by much rubbing.
When therefore a fracture or rubbing happens to the palm, then it is necessary that the sick person sit squarely; and in front of him let there be a seat of equal height. Then place his hand upon it extended.
Then let the assistant extend the broken bones, and let the physician set them, until when they are joined with a good joining.
Then it is necessary that he apply a plaster and lint, if a hot abscess does not occur. Then place a splint on top according to the size of the place: and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is towards the lower parts around the sole of the foot, then make something like a sphere from cloth: and instruct the patient to bind it over the broken sole of his foot: then let it be bound with a long cloth.
And let the splint be of leather, in which there is softness, so that the leather may be applied with the body of the sole: and bind it, according to what is necessary.
But if the fracture is on the outside, then it is necessary: that you place one splint on top, and another splint below on the sole: and let the hand be open and straight. Then place a bandage, as the hand is rolled up: and extend it between the fingers with a ligature.
But if a fracture happens to any of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it is to be set according to what is necessary. Then let it be bound with the sole: and if you wish, place a small, straight splint on it: so that the fracture may be raised, and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle, and index, or little, and ring finger, then it is to be set, and bound with the healthy finger which follows it: or all are to be bound together in a row: and that is better. Or place a small splint on it, as we said in the case of the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the disposition of a warm abscess: and oppose it with what is necessary, when something happens from it, according to what its account repeats.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the hip.
The hip bone is very often fractured, and this is evident to the senses: because it is turned towards the inner and posterior part.
And its restoration is done in such a way that a ligament is tied above the fracture, and another ligament below the fracture. And let the patient be laid prostrate on his face.
Then let the attendant extend each ligament to its part equally; and this, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if the fracture is close to the root of the hip, then let the ligament be placed at the part of the pecten, so that the extension falls upwards; and another ligament below the fracture.
And so similarly if the fracture is close to the knee, then let the ligament be placed close to the knee, so that the extension is downwards.
