Then let the physician equalize the bone with both his hands, until he restores it according to the likeness of its natural form; and let the bone be joined with a good join.
Then it is necessary that you apply a plaster and a bandage, if an abscess does not occur in the limb. But if an abscess occurs in it, then leave it for a few days, until the hot abscess subsides, then return to your treatment.
Now the bandaging of it is as follows. It is necessary that you wrap over the fracture a hard linen cloth, that is, a bandage; and double it twice or thrice; and let there remain of it a superfluity. Then bandage the leg until you reach the heel at the root of the buttocks.
And insert a long thread between the hip and the leg near the knee to the lower parts, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of the two parts. Then roll over the leg and hip what is superfluous from the bandage.
Then place over the hip at the place of the fracture itself splints, and place of them one splint over the bone of the leg.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and hip with soft cloths, so that the tightness is equalized.
Then begin with strong binding from the middle over the place of the fracture with three or four turns. And the more you extend with the bandage from the place of fracture, let your binding be less and softer, and gentler.
Then attend to the two ends of the thread, which you inserted between the hip and leg. Bind therefore with both three small bandages which are above. Then go with the two ends of the thread to the lower parts, until you reach the heel of the foot. And with both tighten also the ends of the bandages from the other side, so that the bandage is not removed from its place.
Then loosen the binding over it, while it remains, so that there does not happen to the limb corrosion, nor abscess, nor swelling, and similar things. If anything of that happens. Therefore hasten with its loosening, and correct all that has happened from it, according to what we have said many times.
But if there is a fragment of bone in the bone which pricks: then it is necessary to smooth it down, if it is possible for you. And if not, cut into it and remove it: and treat it with what has been previously mentioned, until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is constricted, besides which splints are added to it, as we have said in the case of the arm and forearm.
Nevertheless, in the restoration of it thus fractured, lameness does not happen to the patient. And if it is restored alone, except that another leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that he whose it is, always limps.
And know that the hip is constricted in fifty days, or a little is added, or a little is subtracted: all that is according to the diversity of complexions, and the other dispositions.
Concerning the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap rarely happens, but attrition happens to it very often.
If, however, a fracture happens to it, then it is only either a fissure or a hole in its parts. And this is with a wound, and without a wound. And the whole thing is dealt with according to the situation.
And its restoration is thus: that which is disrupted of its parts is aligned with the fingers until it is brought together and joined as much as possible by alignment and ease, and fabrication.
Then apply a plaster of sesame flour, or mill flour, with egg white: and place a round compress over it, if you need it: and bind it above with a bandage suitable for it.
Then consider all the dispositions which we have recounted in the other fractures: such as a hot abscess, and similar things: so that you oppose every accident with that which suits it, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, of which one is thick, and is called by the name of Leg: and the other is thin, and is called Fibula.
And to both of these kinds of fracture happens what happens to the two bones of the arm: and on account of that its restoration is done, just like the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation is one.
If both bones are broken at the same time, the leg is turned in all directions.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is turned to the front.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you find it below; and it is among those which are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and setting, and the binding of splints according to that equally.
However, if the leg fracture is a bad fracture, having many splinters, the extension should be less, and gentler in your alleviation.
And in the leg indeed there is something of the operation above the arm: and it is, that when you set the splints, and have completed all your operation, then take two splints of pine wood, which is used in the making of sunshades which are placed under the boards: and they are made from palm spatulas: and choose from them those which are of small thickness, and not from the small ones: and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.
Then wrap over each one a cloth with two wrappings according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side, and the other on the other side: and let them be from the knee to the lower part of the foot.
Then bind the two splints in three places: at the two ends and in the middle. For when constriction is prevented, the leg inclines neither to the right nor to the left, and stands in a good position.
And now indeed let a cradle be made of wood according to the length of the leg, and let the leg be placed in it, so that it may be kept from movement.
And it is especially necessary to do this in a fracture when there is a proper wound with it.
Then examine the leg every day, and treat it diligently against abscess, or swelling, or other such things. If therefore anything of these things happens, then oppose it with what is necessary, until it is healed, if God wills.
Of the fracture of the bones of the foot and toes.
Indeed, a complete fracture does not happen to the heel bone.
However, a fracture sometimes happens to the bones of the foot, and a fracture also happens little to the toes; and attrition happens to them in most instances, not fracture.
But if a fracture happens to the bones of the foot, and you see those bones raised up on top of each other, then let the patient place his foot on the prepared ground, as if walking.
Then stand, and place your foot on that which is raised up from those bones; afterwards press, or tread on it; and level it, until it returns to its proper places.
Then apply a plaster, and lint on top; and place under the sole of the foot a small board, which has two flat ends.
Then bind it with a suitable bandage to the foot, after you have wrapped it with cloths, and other things which it needs.
When therefore three or four days have passed, then unbind the bandage. For you will find the bones set, whether they be fractured or dislocated. Therefore the operation in them is as you see.
But if some fingers are broken, then restore them and set them according to what I have told you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a small splint along the length of the finger, and let it be a little wider than it: then place this splint, which I have described to you, under the foot, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
But if what is broken of the bones is two, or three, or more, then place over each finger a splint of its size, wrapped in linen cloth: and bind the splint to the bottom of the foot: and let it stand on one end of the splint outside the belly of the foot, so that it holds with a good retention.
And it is fitting for you, that you not forget, but consider all that I have said to you in the other fractures from the accidents which we have mentioned: and apply to each accident what is appropriate.
Concerning the fracture of the woman's vulva, and of the pubic bone, and of the man's penis.
When the vulva of a woman is broken, then make her sit squarely. Then incline her slightly towards her back, and let her be held back.
Then let the midwife fill her vulva with lint until she fills it: and let it be made into a sphere in the vulva from it.
Then let the woman be shaken, and raise her back gradually. For that lint comes out until it becomes like a sphere before the opening of the vulva, and the broken bone returns.
Then place a cushion on her back. Therefore when she wants to urinate, let her remove the lint easily, then let her urinate: and let her replace it in the same way in which you first inserted it: then let her return to her first cushion.
Let her do this for seven days, or thereabouts, for the bone itself is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder, and tie a reed tube over its opening, and introduce the whole bladder into her vulva. Then blow into the tube forcefully, until the bladder is inflated inside the vulva, for the fracture returns.
Then let it be filled with lint, and let it remain for some days, as we have described, until it is healed.
And when the pubic bone of a man or woman is broken, then administer for its restoration and its alignment that which we have described for the hip bone.
And the rectum is not hidden in these nearby fractures, which happen little, especially to someone who has some skill or experience.
And understand this book of mine well, because the fracture itself shows you in many situations the right way in its restoration and its tightening: therefore understand.
But when the penis of a man is broken, take a goose's neck and insert the penis into it. Then place a bandage of cloth over it and it should be bandaged and left for three days, or thereabouts, and it will heal, if God wills.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of bones when it is with a wound.
It is fitting for him who has suffered a fracture with a wound, especially if it is large, such as a fracture of the hip bone, or of the thigh bone, or similar, that you should hasten and immediately bleed him if the conditions for bloodletting are present, as we have explained before.
But if the wound flows with blood, then it is necessary that you hasten to cauterize it, so that you sprinkle ground verdigris upon it, if nothing else is at hand.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day and do not postpone it, if an abscess does not occur.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then delay its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach it on the third and fourth day absolutely, for you would cause bad things to happen to it.
But if the broken bone is prominent and exposed above the skin, then it is necessary that you try to reduce it and level it with your hands gently and with slight extension.
But if extension of it and its leveling with your hands is not prepared, then reduce it with this instrument. And it is an instrument made of iron, whose length is seven or eight fingers' breadth, and its width is according to the size of the wound. And because of that it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of them, according to the quantity which he needs for it in the treatment of every kind of fracture.
And let it be round; and let there be a small thickness in it, so that it does not bend during compression on it at the time of operation; and let it have a sharp end, which has a margin at its extremities. And let its upper part be thicker than its lower part.
And this is its form.
It is necessary in this operation that its sharp curved end be placed on the end of the prominent bone; and push it with it at once until the bone returns and is leveled with a certain evenness.
Therefore try to even the ends of the fracture together.
But if the ends of the broken bone are thin, and the instrument does not grasp them well, then it is necessary to cut the end of that bone until the instrument can grasp it.
If however you cannot reduce them completely with what we have described, then cut it with whichever of the cutting instruments we have mentioned is suitable; or saw it with one of the saws in whichever way is possible for you.
Then shave off what remains on the bone of roughness and small splinters.
Therefore, when the bone is reduced, and the patient feels intense, hurting pain after its reduction, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Then do this. For you provide the patient with great help when your restoration of the bone is completed.
Then submerge the cloth in astringent, black wine. And especially if it is summer. And do not place cerate or anything containing oil on the wound, lest it cause putrefaction and corruption in it.
Then administer splints at the completion of your bone restoration. And leave the wound uncovered, so that you cut a hole in the bandages with scissors according to the size of the wound.
And take every precaution that you do not constrict the wound with the fracture. For often foolish physicians have done this, and have caused death, or corrosion, or zuchem, that is, pus, to befall their patients. And let your binding be soft, gentle, different from the binding of other fractures.
But if the wound is bad, or is a large wound, and you fear some bad occurrences which we have mentioned about it, and you find pain suspended in the place: then it is not necessary that you place splints on it. And make for them bandages of hard cloths in place of splints, and bind it with them.
But when it is after one day, or two days, and you see the wound beginning to generate pus: then remove from above it the cloth which you placed on it with wine. Then administer lint and ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds: such as tetrapharmacon ointment, and similar things.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the bandage. And examine the wound every day morning and evening, until it heals and is consolidated.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that pus flows from it downwards with ease.
But if many days pass for the wound and it does not heal, nor is pus discharged from it: then know that there are small splinters of bone there. Therefore it is necessary that you probe the wound with a probe. However many of those splinters are separated, remove them, and extract them. And those of them which are not separated, and pierce the limb, and cause pain to happen, try to cut them and remove them in every way that it is possible for you.
But if a swelling or an ulcer, that is, an estiomenus, or another kind of corruption and putrefaction happens to the wound: then it is necessary that you apply to each of them that which is suitable to it from the treatment, the account of which preceded in its chapter.
And that in which my speech ought to stand, and to which you should apply your present mind when a bone is broken and protrudes above a limb, such as the hip bone, and the collarbone, and similar large bones, is that you should not presume to pull it, for very often death results from this; rather, leave it until it putrefies.
For perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then therefore treat the wound, if it seems good to you; and if not, leave it.
Concerning the treatment of a node in the track of a fracture.
This node often happens in the track of the healing of a fracture. Especially that which is near the joints: and the shape of the limb is disfigured by it. And perhaps it prevents the limb from its natural function.
Therefore consider then, if the node is recent: administer to it medicines which have astringency, such as aloe, and olibanum, and myrrh, and sarcocol, and acacia, and similar things.
So that you take some of these, or all of them: and mix them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar: and put them on the node in a pledget, and bind them tightly over it with a good bandage.
And leave the bandage, and do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and apply another, until the node is removed.
Or bind a thin lead plate neatly over it. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the limbs.
But if the node has already turned into stone, and is constricted, and urgent necessity compels its removal: then make an incision on it from its upper part, and cut away the protruding excess; or shave it with some razors, until it is removed: and treat it, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment when it is restored, and the limb remains thin afterwards, not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is restored, and the limb remains thin and weak afterwards, then this does not happen except for many reasons. One of which is, the multitude of the loosening and binding of it not as it ought; or because of the excess of the constriction of the ligaments, until nourishment is prevented from flowing to the limb; or because of the multitude of superfluous embrocation at the wrong time; or because of the small amount of blood in the body of the patient, and its weakness.
And its treatment is, to nourish the patient and make his body strong, until the blood is multiplied in him. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and happiness to him.
Then place pitch on the limb: so that the pitch may draw to it much nourishment. And frequently embrocate it with warm water, until nourishment flows, and it returns to its natural form.
Concerning the treatment of fractured bones when they are reset crookedly.
When crookedness and a prominence of the fractured bone, or a knot, happens to a limb already reset after its healing, and the shape of the limb is marred by it, yet the limb is not prevented from its natural function: then you should not heed the advice of one who thinks that the limb should be broken again.
And there have been many foolish physicians and bone-setters who have done this in our land.
And this operation is indeed highly blameworthy, leading to death, or great danger, so that it is certainly mortal.
But if the lameness and knotting are recent, then it should be poulticed with warm water in which emollient herbs have been boiled, such as mallow leaves and root, and melilot, and the like. And let a poultice be made with a softening plaster, such as properly made diachylon.
Or let the mucilage of mallow root be taken, and shaken with hen's fat or sesame oil: and let it be plastered with it.
Or let fat figs be taken, and ground with pigeon dung, and similar things which are named among medicines that lessen consolidation.
And sometimes the knotting is resolved with frequent, gentle, and prolonged rubbing, which is done with wet hands. And let the movement of the limb be administered to every part at all hours.
But if the tortuosity has preceded, and has become strong, and has been converted into stone, and the necessity has been provoked to cure it with iron: then it is necessary that its upper part be cut, and the continuity of the bone be resolved, and that which is superfluous from the knotting or bone be incised with fine scalpels: and let ease be administered in it with care and diligence.
Concerning speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is the exit of any of the joints from its place. Therefore it is prohibited from its movement, and the shape of the member is deformed. And it causes the afflicted to suffer pains and severe injuries.
Therefore, when a dislocation happens to someone, then it is necessary to hasten to its restoration; nor should one delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed, and perhaps with it the reduction of the dislocation.
Therefore, on account of that, it is not fitting that it be moved, nor extended at the time of its inflammation. Because it often causes spasms and painful injuries to occur.
But when that happens, then it is necessary that you hasten to the bloodletting of the patient. Then let it be left alone until the inflammation subsides.
Then let the limb be rubbed with warm water and oil. Then let it be reduced easily.
And let every limb be treated with that whose mention will come in its place.
I have indeed already arranged the chapters on dislocations, even as preceded in fractures, from the upper part of the body to the lower part of it.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws are seldom dislocated, except rarely.
And the dislocation of both happens according to one of two methods. For either they are removed from their places by a small removal, and are somewhat softened. Or they are dislocated by a complete dislocation, until they are softened to the breast, until the saliva of the sick person flows, and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is involved with speech.
But when its dislocation is small, then it returns in most instances by itself with ease.
But if the dislocation is complete, then it is necessary that its reduction be administered quickly, and not be delayed at all: and it is so that the assistant holds the head of the sick person; and the physician inserts the thumb of one of his hands into the root of the jaw of the dislocation between his mouth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he inserts his two thumbs together, if the dislocation is on two sides. And the remaining fingers of his hands should be outside, with which he may pull.
And instruct the sick person to clean his jaws, and free them for eating on every side. And the physician pulls the jaw, and pushes the jaw, until it returns to its place.
But if its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are together: then administer a vaporization with warm water and oil, until the reduction of both becomes easy: and do not delay the reduction of both completely, as we said.
Therefore, when they return and are set, and the mouth of the sick person is closed, and they are not softened: then place on them small cushions of cloths, with cerate which is made from wax and rose oil.
Then let it be bound easily with soft bandages.
And let the sleep of the sick person be on his back: and let his head be positioned between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right and left: nor let him try, or be forced to chew anything: rather let his food be gentle sipping, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is bound: then let him eat what he wishes, and let it be administered with ease.
Let him not exert himself over the operation of his mouth in eating and drinking, and yawning, until the jaw is bound, and healed.
But if the reduction of the two jaws becomes difficult, when they are dislocated at some time, and are not returned to their places: then fevers often result from this, and constant thirst: and perhaps the belly of the sick person is loosened, and perhaps he vomits pure bile.
Therefore when you see that, then know that it is a loss, and very often he to whom this happens dies within ten days.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle, and the extremity of the shoulder.
Indeed, the clavicle is not dislocated from the inner side, because of its connection with the chest. And it is sometimes dislocated outwards: and this is evident to the senses.
And its restoration is that the patient lies on his back, and extends his arms. Then compress the place with your palm with a strong compression, for it will return itself.
Then place a plaster and pads on it, and bandage it.
However, its extremities which follow the elbow and are continuous with it, are not dislocated except rarely. But if it is dislocated on some day: then it is necessary that it be reduced and set according to what we have said, and what is prepared for you.
Then place a plaster, and pads, and a bandage on it. And instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And with this same treatment the extremity of the humerus is reduced when it is also separated from its place.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the humerus.
Know that the humerus is not dislocated except in three ways.
One of which is that it is dislocated towards the armpit to the lower parts. The second is that it is dislocated towards the chest. And perhaps it is dislocated to the upper part of the humerus: and that happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated to the posterior, because of the scapula. And it is not dislocated anteriorly, because of the nerves.
And it is mostly dislocated and exits inferiorly towards the armpit: especially in those whose flesh is little. Because it exits quickly in them, and enters quickly.
But in those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite of that, namely because it exits with difficulty, and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps a blow or a fall happens to some men, and the humerus becomes inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is thought because of that that it is already dislocated.
Therefore, it is necessary that you examine it until you stand upon its certainty, and then you proceed in its cure.
And a dislocation is known when it is towards the lower parts, towards the armpit: and distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a healthy humerus. For you find a manifest difference between them, and you find in the head of the humerus a concavity, and under the armpit, on palpation, the head of the humerus as if it were an egg: and the sick man cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it in all motions.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest, or upwards: then you find a manifest touch, nor is it hidden.
And indeed, the reduction of this dislocation is easy when it is recent, or the patient is a child.
And its reduction is that the attendant raises his hand upwards. Then place your thumbs under his armpit, and raise the joint forcefully upwards to its place: and the attendant raise his hand, and extend it upwards. Then let him put it lower, for it itself returns quickly.
But if it does not return by that which we have said, and many days have passed since the dislocation occurred: then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water, and a softening and soothing embrocation be administered, such as if the roots of mallow, fenugreek, and melilot are decocted in water, and administered.
Then the patient is laid on his back: and a sphere and wool are placed under his armpit, which is equal between softness and firmness.
Then let the physician place his heel on the sphere, and raise the head of the humerus forcefully, and pull the patient's hand downwards.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method: and it is that you cause a man to be present who is taller than the patient, and stands on the side, and inserts his shoulder under the patient's armpit: and raises his armpit upwards, until the patient is suspended in the air. And another assistant pulls the patient's hand towards the lower part of his abdomen. But if the patient is light, then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him, to weigh him down: for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced according to another method: and it is that a long piece of wood be fixed in the earth, the head of which is round in shape, like the handle of a mortar: which is neither thick nor thin. Then it is placed under the patient after soft cloths are placed on top of the wood; and the patient stands along the length of the wood.
Then his hand is extended downwards on the one side, and his body is extended on the other side with force: for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But if its reduction is difficult by all that we have said, then use this treatment: and it is that you take a piece of wood whose length is the quantity of two arms, and its width the quantity of four fingers, and its thickness the quantity of two fingers: which has a round head, so that its entry into the depth of the rectum is eased, according to this form.
Then soft cloths are tied over the round head, so that the wood does not injure the patient.
Then it is placed under the patient's rectum, so that the hand or arm is extended over the wood downwards; and the wood is tied over the support and arm and the end of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on the rung of a ladder across its width, and the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is left hanging on the other side. For the joint immediately enters.
When therefore its entry is completed in whichever way is possible, then it is necessary that you place under the rectum a sphere of equal size made of wool. Then place the plaster prepared from mill dust with olibanum and egg white over the whole shoulder, as it is rolled over from above.
Then bind beneath the rectum with a suitable bandage; and let the bandage be rolled over the plaster from above; and let its hand be bound to its neck, and let it be left, nor should its hand be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that the food of the patient be meager until the limb is strengthened, because this is quicker for its healing.
Then let more be given after seven days, or five, and let it be exercised with movement. If therefore it is strengthened and not softened, then it is now healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times, because of the moisture which happens to it, or because of another cause: then it is necessary that a cautery with three holes be administered to it, according to what was premised in the chapter on the cautery.
But if all this is done, and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the joint is not strengthened, and the plaster and bandage are applied again to it several times, and it is not strengthened, and it falls, and he cannot raise it upwards: then know that its nerves which are at the head of the humerus, are now cut, or stretched, or softened: and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
But the reduction of a dislocation which occurs towards the chest and breast, or towards the back, is done with pushing and extension with the hands: until it returns, and the remaining bandage and care is administered to it, until it is healed.
But if after healing heaviness occurs in the limb, and slowness in its movement, then let the infirm person use a bath many times, until that heaviness is lessened, and it returns to its first nature.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocation of the elbow.
Know that the joint of the elbow is dislocated with difficulty, and similarly returns with difficulty also: and it indeed is dislocated to all parts, and especially to the anterior, or to the posterior.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: because it is falling under sight, and under touch, according to whichever figure it is dislocated. And when you join a dislocated elbow with a healthy one, that is clearly shown to you. For the joint is hollowed, and cannot double the arm, nor touch with it its shoulder.
And it is necessary that you hasten, and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot abscess happens to it.
For if a hot abscess happens to it, its reduction becomes difficult: and perhaps it is not healed at all, especially when it is to the rear. For it is worse than all kinds of dislocation, and of more vehement pain than them: and very often death comes down with it.
And its restoration, when it is of those which it is possible to reduce, is that his hand be extended with two hands: and his arm be expanded, and the hands of the physician be above the elbow and under it, and he himself impel the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or indeed the dislocation is to the front: then it returns thus, that he doubles the hand with his hand, until he strikes with the root of his palm the elbow which is dislocated.
But if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer a vehement extension, very strong: and it is that two assistants extend the arm: and two assistants also hold him, so that he is not moved during the extension.
Then let the arm be revolved to every part after a long doubled cloth, or a broad ligament, is wrapped around his hand.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, let him anoint it with oil: so that it may be of those things which aid in the lubricity of the joint with ease. Then let him impel the joint with a vehement impulse, until it returns.
And after its reduction it is necessary that a plaster be placed upon it, in which there is stypticity and desiccation with egg white: and let it be bound with a fitting bandage. And let the arm be suspended from the neck of the infirm: and leave it for several days.
Then let it be unbound. If therefore the joint is fixed in its place, then unbind the ligature from it, and leave it.
And if you see the joint not well bound, then repeat the plaster and the ligature, and leave it for several days, until it is bound. Then unbind it.
But if heaviness occurs to it after the reduction of the joint, and the disposition is in motion: then administer moistening in bathing, and gentle friction, and compression, until it is anointed, or put on the joint the fat of a ram's rump. Then let it be bound, and leave it on it day and night. Then remove it, and put it into the bath. Therefore when it sweats, then rub the joint with a moderate rubbing. Then repeat on it the application of ram's fat once, and twice, and three times with the bath, until it is softened.
And if you wish, put on it cow dung, moist, warmed with butter, and bind it on.
Do that several times. For it is thus softened, and returns to its proper condition.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation, by Almahassem.
The hand is very often dislocated by Almahassem, that is, the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. Contrary to the other joints. However, speed is necessary in the reduction of its dislocation at the time in which it is dislocated, before the place becomes inflamed, or a hot abscess occurs in it.
And the reduction of the dislocation in it is that you place the weakened hand of Almahassem on a board, and the assistant extends its hand; and the physician places his palm on the prominence of the joint and pushes it until it returns.
However, it must be considered if the palm has already receded to the belly of the hand: then the infirm person should place the back of his hand on the board during extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding towards the back of the hand, then the position of his hand should be inside on the board, that is, the inner part of his hand should be placed on the board, so that the physician's hand falls upon the prominence of the joint itself.
If therefore it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then bind it with a plaster that soothes the abscess, and leave it, and do not repeat it.
For if it does not tolerate, nor can you effect its reduction after days pass, yet the joint remains according to its crookedness, and it does not harm the infirm person in any way, except that the hand is weakened, and he cannot grasp anything; then know that the nerves are severed or worn away.
Therefore there is no remedy for it except that it be bound with a cautery near its fall. And perhaps that does not help at all.
Therefore, when Almahassem does not return, then place upon it the plaster which we have described. Then let it be bound, and left for five days; afterwards let it be unbound and left, and let the hand be exercised.
But if its movement is deprived, and something serious happens to it, then soften it with warm water and friction several times until it softens.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of fingers.
When a finger is dislocated in any direction, or a finger is dislocated towards the back of the palm, or its belly: then extend the finger, and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it towards the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it, and extend it until it is straight on that day.
When therefore it is night, bind it according to the same account: and do not cease to unbind it during the day: and exercise it with movement: and bind it at night.
Do this for several days, until it is tightened.
And similarly do with it, if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand: and bind it to that part, and do with it your first operation, until it is healed.
And similarly do with it when it is dislocated to the other parts.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation of the vertebrae of the back or neck happens to someone, or many vertebrae are removed from their places, then there is no cure in it, because death hastens for the infirm person.
And the sign of that is that the excretion of the infirm person goes out without will, nor can he restrain it; and oftentimes some of his limbs soften from it, or his two legs, or his two arms, or one of them; or one vertebra is removed from its place.
For oftentimes it is removed, which oftentimes makes the removal of them to four parts.
That therefore which is removed to the hinder parts, is named gibbosity.
The cure however of it is, that you consider then, if gibbosity happens from childhood: then there is no cure in it, nor is there any healing from it at all.
But in that which happens from a fall, or blow, or similar things, the ancients have already spoken of methods of cure at great length. Of which very much does not return with profit.
I however have now shortened it from that, of which little excuses much of it from my nearness to the intention: and my exposition in which I have explained it, and have formed its instrument in a different way from that in which they showed it, and explained it.
I say therefore, that in gibbosity which happens from the front, there is no skill, nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which is done to two sides also.
And it is not cured from them, except that which happens in the back properly, with that which I relate: and it is, that the infirm is extended upon his face upon an even surface near a wall: and a wet mat is spread under him, that his chest may not be injured.
Then let a straight piece of wood be placed fixed in a pit in the earth, at the head of the surface: and another piece of wood about his feet at the other end of the surface: and let a servant hold the wood, and let it not be fixed in a pit. And let another servant hold the other.
Then wrap around the head of the infirm, and under his armpits a soft, firm ligature: and extend the end of the ligature to the wood which is at his head: and tie it to it.
Then tighten another ligature above his hips, and above his knees, and at his ankles. Then gather all the ligatures, and tie them to the other wood which is at his feet.
Then let each servant extend the ligature to the part of the wood, and let the two woods not be removed from their places in which they are fixed: however, let them not be fixed, as we said.
And the physician shall place his palms upon the vertebrae with strength, until it returns; or let him place a board upon it. Then let him attach his feet to the board, until it returns.
But if it does not return by this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three cubits. And hollow out in the wall of which we spoke, which is near the infirm, a place into which the end of the board may enter. Then place the middle of the board upon the vertebra. And let the physician place his feet upon the other end. And let him pull strongly until the vertebra is constricted, and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, work with a lathe which is revolved with your hand: and it is so that you fix in the earth near the head of the patient at the end of the bed two pieces of wood, whose length is that of one arm: and near his head at the end of the bed two pieces of wood.
And let the length which is between each piece of wood be one palm: and now a hole has been made in every place, through which the lathe runs: and let each piece of wood be firmly fixed in the earth, so that it does not move at all.
And let a round piece of wood be inserted: and it is a lathe in which the cord revolves in the two holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its extremity is a hole, in which is fixed a piece of wood, whose length is a palm together with that which revolves; and in the other two pieces of wood there is similarly one of these.
Then tighten the bandages which you have tightened on the chest of the patient with the lathe which is near his head; and the bandages which you have tightened on his legs with the lathe which is near his feet.
Then let an assistant stand by each lathe, who turns his hand with the cord, with which the lathe revolves; and let the physician adjust the vertebra, as we have said.
This is the form of the lathe, which follows.
Then after the vertebra returns and the place is even, then it is necessary that you put a drying plaster with egg white. Then lint. Afterwards place above the plaster a small plate of wood, whose width is three fingers, or about that. And its length a quantity which takes in the place of the hump, and above the healthy part of the vertebra.
Then let it be bound with the bandage that we mentioned. And let the patient use a light diet until he is healed.
But if something of a prominence remains in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary that medicines which soften and soothe be administered, with the application of the plate which we have described for a long time. And now a lead plate is applied to it.
And sometimes a prominence occurs in the last vertebra of the back; and it is thought to be a dislocation by it, and it is that bone which now protrudes. Therefore it is not fitting that one should presume to treat it with this medicine; for perhaps it will cause death.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that in the hip joint and the shoulder joint, only dislocation occurs; nor does that happen to them which happens to the other joints from a small displacement and concavity.
And indeed the hip joint is dislocated in four directions. That is, because it is dislocated anteriorly and posteriorly.
And it is most often dislocated inwards; and it is rarely dislocated forwards and backwards.
And the treatment of its inward dislocation is this: when you join the diseased leg with the healthy one, it is longer; and its knee is more prominent than that of the healthy one, and the diseased person cannot bend his foot at the groin; and the place following the groin becomes inflamed with a warm inflammation, because the head of the femur has now arrived there.
And the sign of one who suffers dislocation outwards is that its symptoms are contrary to these symptoms.
And the sign of one who suffers dislocation forwards is that he extends the healthy leg to its full extent; however, he cannot bend it without pain in the knee; and if he tries to walk, he cannot put weight on it forwards; and his urine is retained, and his groin becomes inflamed, and when walking his weight is on his heel.
And the sign of one who suffers dislocation backwards is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the groin; and his leg is also shorter than the other; and the groin is softened, and the head of the femur is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction, however, of this species of dislocation is such that you consider then if the dislocation is old, which has already become chronic in the one having it. Then if you administer its reduction, and it remains in its disposition: then there is no cure in it at all. Therefore, no presumption should be made for it completely.
But in him whose dislocation is recent, and is of one of the four species of dislocation: then hasten to this, that you move the joint, and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left: for perhaps it returns, and does not need other care.
But if it does not return in this way, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong assistant, and he extends his leg from the lower part: either with his hands, or with a ligature, which is tied above his leg above the knee: and another assistant extends his hand from above, so that he inserts his hands under his armpits. Then let him bind with a soft ligature above the hip joint, and hold the end of the other ligature.
And let its extension be either from the front of the groin to the part of the clavicle, or from the back to the part of the back.
And let the extension of them all be at once, until the sick man is raised with his body from the ground, and remains hanging.
For this kind of extension is a common kind of the four kinds.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good: and if not, then it is necessary for each kind that which I shall say of its proper cure.
In its proper reduction, when the dislocation is inwards, it is necessary that the sick man be laid on his sound side. Then let him place a bandage above the root of the hip, in that which is between the head of the hip and the place which is under the groin. Then extend the bandage upwards from the groin to the higher part of the body, to the part of the clavicle.
Then let another strong assistant take his arms, and reduce the thickened place of the diseased hip, and extend it outwards with a strong extension. Now indeed it returns to its place.
And this method is easier than the other methods of treatment by which this limb is reduced.
But if it is excused above you, and does not obey you in entering with this method of treatment completely: then it is necessary that the two feet of the infirm be bound together with a strong, soft bandage, over the two heels, and over the two knees: and let the length of each from its fellow be the quantity of four fingers: and let the infirm leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then let the infirm person be suspended from the head of a piece of wood which is in the house: and let its length from the ground be the quantity of two arms.
Then instruct a strong young man to hold the head of the hip; and let another young man be suspended with the infirm; and let another young man push, holding the hip with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
In its proper reduction, however, when the dislocation is outwards, it is necessary that the infirm person lie on a bench, according to what we have narrated in the case of one having a hump. And let a bandage be tightened over his leg, the infirm one properly, and over his chest.
Then let two pieces of wood be placed, one at his feet, and another at his head. Then let an additional piece of wood be placed in the middle of the bench, firmly fixed: upon which let wet cloths be wrapped, so that it does not injure the infirm person: and let the wood be between his hips, so that he is not pulled downwards during extension.
Then let each assistant pull on his part, and let the physician equalize the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it yields to repositioning, it will be good.
And if not, place it on the board, and press it, as we said in the case of a hump, evenly. However, it is necessary that the patient lie on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that the leg of the injured person be extended quickly: and it is according to this same account on a stool: and let the physician place the palm of his right hand on the groin of the injured person. Then he should constrict it with the other hand, and with that constriction place it extended downwards towards the knee.
And if the dislocation is to the rear: then it is not necessary that the injured person be extended downwards, and be raised above the ground: rather it is necessary that he be placed on something hard, just as it is necessary that it be done if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we said about his lying on his two hips: and it is on his face, and the ligaments extended: according to what we recently said.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a board also on the place to which the joint goes, when the reduction is completed as it should be.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you: and it is that you extend the two legs of the injured person: and when you see them equal, and the injured person tightens his leg, and extends it without difficulty: then you know that the limb has returned, as it should be: then join the two hips, and apply a plaster, and bind it with a bandage, that is, a roller: so that the hip does not move to any side: and let the injured person adhere to rest for three or four days.
Then loosen the bandage and plaster, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see them equal in position, then you know that the dislocation is now fixed: therefore allow the injured person to walk.
And if you see any softening: then return, and plaster it, and bind it according to your first binding: and leave it for three days also.
Then loosen it and make him delay in walking on both for several days, until it is strengthened well.
Concerning the dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outwards, inwards, and downwards, that is to say, backwards.
And it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you instruct the patient to apply his leg to his hip. If, however, it does not adhere to the hip, then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all types of its dislocation is that you make the patient sit upright, so that he extends his leg upon it if he can, and you place your back to his face; and place his foot between your feet.
Then make his knee adhere with your palms, and straighten them between your fingers above his knee. Then apply with your palm the two sides of his knee with strength. And another assistant extend his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres with the hip gently, not laboriously.
Then plaster it, and make the leg adhere to the hip. Then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days. Then unbind them.
And let the infirm person not use walking for several days, until it is healed.
But if reduction is refused to you, then administer strong extension with the bandages, the description of which preceded in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the heel bone.
The heel bone is sometimes removed by a small removal, and sometimes dislocated according to the whole. And its dislocation happens either inwards or outwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel bone swollen, protruding towards the part to which it is dislocated.
The treatment, however, of its removal is easy repositioning: and it is that it is extended easily with the hands, and is made level, until it returns.
In the treatment, however, when it is dislocated completely, it is necessary that you make the sick person sit upright: and a strong assistant holds him from the posterior part of his back, in the middle of it.
Then you hold with your right hand his foot from its upper part, and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot at the place of the heel.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with the left [hand], twice.
Then extend with the left [hand], and push the front of the foot with the right towards the leg without effort.
Do that twice, as we have narrated.
Then push the front of the foot to the leg a third time, and you pull the hook.
If therefore it returns in one time, or in two according to this method, and you see the foot set, it is good: and if not, then repeat the operation on it; because it returns.
But if the reduction is prevented by what we have narrated, then make the patient lie on his back on the ground, and fix a peg firmly in the ground so that it falls between his hips; and you have already wrapped a cloth around it so that it does not injure the patient.
Then let the assistant take his hip; afterwards let another assistant extend the foot, either with his hands or with a bandage which he ties above the ankle. Then let each assistant extend against the extension of his fellow; and let the peg stand between the patient's two hips, retaining him so that his body is not drawn downwards during extension.
Then let the physician set the dislocation with his hands, and let another assistant hold the sound leg downwards. For the dislocation returns quickly.
When therefore the dislocation returns, and the soundness of its return is declared to you: then apply a plaster and lint, and bind it firmly with bandages, and bind the foot with a bandage to the leg.
And it is necessary that you beware or fear the nerves which are above the heel behind, lest the bandage be too tight upon them and injure them.
Then leave it for two or three days. If the bandage becomes loose, tighten it; then loosen it on the third or fourth day: and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if he attempts to walk before that time, there is no security that the dislocation will not be loosened and corrupted, and will not receive a cure afterwards.
But if a hot abscess should occur to him, then it is necessary that you administer for its alleviation that which has been previously narrated by us in another place concerning treatment, and an embrocation, until it is removed.
Concerning the dislocation of the toes, and the cure.
It is necessary that what is dislocated of them be set with a little extension without effort; and this indeed is not difficult, rather it is easy.
But if the dislocation is in some of the joints of the dorsum of the foot, then it is necessary that the infirm person make his foot sit upon a place level with the ground, or upon a board; and he is standing as if walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot upon that which protrudes from those joints. Then tread upon it with your foot with strength, until it returns, and you see it now set, and no prominence appears in the place.
Then place under the belly of his foot a board which receives the whole foot, to which there are two heads. Then bind it with a suitable bandage for three days. Then unbind it, and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is bound and secured by reduction.
Concerning the types of dislocation with a wound or fracture.
When something of that sort happens, and you attempt its cure and restoration, death often follows: and therefore one ought not to presume to treat such a thing unless one is skilled in the art through long experience, compassionate, pious, cautious, not bold, nor presumptuous: and to use, at the outset, only those medicines which soothe hot abscesses; and to leave the patient to divine judgment. Unless you see, or hope for, salvation from destruction with the mildness of the illness, and in which something of hope appears. Therefore, attempt its reduction immediately at the beginning, before a hot abscess develops.
If then the member returns as we wish, then administer a regime that soothes the abscess: and treat the wound with what is suitable from drying ointments.
But if the dislocation is with a fracture, and separated splinters occur in the bone, then attempt their removal, and do in this what we have said in simple illnesses from what precedes in their places.
And be cautious according to your ability, and guard yourself from entering the path of fear: as my previous instruction to you.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and safer for your body among them in your time.
